وهذا باب بيان وجوه دفع المناقضة
Valid marital bed. If the opponent objects that the second has an invalid marital relationship, thereby establishing the lineage of the child like a man who married a woman without witnesses and she gave birth. This objection is apparently invalid due to the difference in ruling, but since lineage could not be established from Zayd after being established from 'Amr, the objection is valid as a cause for entitlement to lineage. So the opponent needed to give preference by saying the first's bed is valid. Then the opponent objected that the second is a witness and the water is his, so the fiqh of the issue became clear: that validity and ownership are more deserving of consideration than presence because the invalid is a doubt, so it does not oppose reality, thus the preference is invalid. As for objections in the original [qiyas], they are three: an objection that provides extension? [unclear] meaning that does not extend and that is invalid due to lack of ruling and its corruption. Second: that it extends to a point of consensus. Third: that it extends to a disputed meaning. Some scholars consider this objection good because of the consensus of jurists that the cause is one of them, so they become mutually repelling by consensus, thus establishing the other becomes nullification by necessity. The answer: the consensus was on the corruption of one of them for a meaning in it, not for the correctness of the other, like measurement and taste, the correct one is one, not otherwise, but the corruption is not due to the correctness of the other, but due to a meaning in it that corrupts it, so establishing corruption for the correctness of the other is invalid. Thus the objection is invalid. Every correct statement in the original mentioned as a distinction: mention it as a counter-objection, like their statement about the mortgagor's emancipation that it is an action of the mortgagor that meets the mortgagee's right with nullification, so it is rejected like sale. They said: it is not like sale because it can be rescinded unlike emancipation. The explanation: we say that qiyas is for extending the ruling of the text, not changing it, and we do not admit the existence of this condition here. Its explanation: the ruling of the original is suspension of what can be rejected and rescinded, and you in the branch nullify absolutely what cannot be rejected or rescinded. Likewise if he analogizes it to the emancipation of the sick, because the ruling of consensus there is suspension of emancipation and binding of emancipation, and you have extended nullification absolutely. If he claims in the original a ruling other than what we said: we do not admit it. And like their statement: killing a human is compensable, so it necessitates wealth, like killing by mistake. It is said: it is not like killing by mistake because there the equivalent is not legally attainable, and the way is what we said: we do not admit the condition of qiyas. Its interpretation: the ruling of the original is legislating wealth as a substitute for retaliation, and you have made it competing with it. We have explained that contradiction does not affect effective causes after the validity of their effect, and that is made clear by four ways. This is the chapter on explaining the ways to repudiate contradiction.
Source pages p. 637

Commentary
This passage discusses the types of objections (mu'aradha) in qiyas according to Hanafi usul. It begins with a fiqh example about lineage to illustrate how an apparent objection can be valid when it properly serves as an alternative cause. Then it categorizes mu'aradha in qiyas into three types: (1) an objection that does not extend to a ruling (invalid), (2) an objection that extends to a point of consensus (i.e., the objector claims that the opponent's 'illa contradicts a consensus ruling), (3) an objection extending to a disputed meaning. Some scholars (likely from other schools) consider the third type valid because they argue that if two 'illas are mutually repelling by consensus, then establishing one necessarily nullifies the other. The Hanafi response is that the consensus was on the corruption of one 'illa for a specific internal reason, not because of the other's correctness. Therefore, the objection fails. The passage then gives a methodological principle: if the opponent presents a valid argument as a distinction (mufaraqa), you should respond by presenting it as a counter-objection (mumana'a) — i.e., deny the applicability of the condition. Two fiqh examples are given: (a) emancipation by a mortgagor — the opponent says it is like sale (void), but the Hanafi response is that emancipation can be rescinded unlike sale, so the analogy fails. (b) killing a human — the opponent says it is like accidental killing (requires wealth), but the Hanafi response is that in intentional killing, the equivalent (qisas) is legally attainable, so the condition for qiyas is not met. Finally, the passage notes that contradictions do not affect effective 'illas after their effects are established, and this is elaborated in four ways (not detailed here).

Key Terms
المعارضة (al-mu'āraḍah): Objection in qiyas where the opponent presents a counter-analogy to invalidate the proposed 'illa.
المفارقة (al-mufāraqah): Distinction or difference between the original and branch that prevents the application of qiyas.
الممانعة (al-mumāna'ah): Counter-objection denying the condition for qiyas, i.e., that the alleged distinction is not valid or that the condition for analogy is not met.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of mu'aradha in qiyas is considered invalid?
A) An objection that extends to a point of consensus.
B) An objection that extends to a disputed meaning.
C) An objection that does not extend to any ruling.
D) An objection that is based on a valid distinction.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rejects the third type of mu'aradha because consensus on corruption of one 'illa does not imply the correctness of the other.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Rebutting Contradiction (Manaqada) in Qiyas
Explaining the method to rebut objections against a claimed 'illa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four aspects of rebutting a contradiction (manaqada) against a claimed 'illa in qiyas and apply the method of reconciliation (tawfiq) to defend a Hanafi qiyas argument.

Core Idea
A contradiction against an 'illa is rebutted by reconciliation through four aspects: the description, its effect, the ruling, and the objective.

When a Hanafi faqih presents a qiyas argument, the opponent may raise a contradiction (manaqada) claiming that the 'illa is inconsistent. This lesson teaches the classical Hanafi method to rebut such objections. The key is to show that the apparent contradiction can be reconciled by examining the 'illa from four aspects: the description itself, the meaning that makes it an 'illa, the ruling sought, and the ultimate objective. Mastering this method strengthens your ability to defend Hanafi qiyas against challenges.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of manaqada
First, recognize that manaqada is an objection claiming inconsistency between the claimed 'illa and another ruling or principle. In Hanafi usul, if reconciliation (tawfiq) is possible, the objection is invalid.
This mirrors the rejection of contradictions in court when testimonies can be harmonized.
2
Learn the principle of reconciliation
The text states: 'whenever the respondent can reconcile between what he claimed as the 'illa and what is perceived as a contradiction by harmonization, the contradiction is invalidated.' So the goal is to show that there is no real contradiction.
Reconciliation is the first line of defense before questioning the opponent's argument.
3
Study the four aspects of rebuttal
The text lists four aspects: (1) the description (wasf) itself, (2) the meaning of the description that makes it an 'illa (its effect), (3) the ruling sought by that description, and (4) the objective behind the ruling. Each aspect provides a vantage point to reconcile the apparent contradiction.
The first aspect is the easiest: show that the description in the original case differs from the branch case.
4
Apply the aspects to the given examples
Examine the examples: emancipation of a mortgaged slave (first aspect: emancipation admits revocation but sale does not), killing a protected human (second aspect: exact equivalent not possible), wiping the head (first aspect: wiping head is not like wiping khuff because it's not removing impurity). Practice identifying which aspect is being used.
The examples illustrate that the contradiction is not fatal once the precise difference is identified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فبطلت المعارضة.
وكل كلام صحيح في الأصل يُذكر على سبيل المفارقة: فاذكره على سبيل الممانعة، كقولهم في إعتاق الراهن إنه تصرف من الراهن يلاقي حق المرتهن بالإبطال فكان مردوداً كالبيع، فقالوا ليس كالبيع؛ لأنه يحتمل الفسخ بخلاف العتق.
والوجه فيه أن نقول: إن القياس لتعدية حكم النص، دون تغييره، وأنا لا نسلم وجود هذا الشرط ها هنا.
وبيانه أن حكم الأصل وقْفُ ما يحتمل الرد والفسخ، وأنت في الفرع: تبطل أصلاً ما لا يحتمل الردَّ والفسخ، وكذلك إن اعتبره بإعتاق المريض؛ لأن حكم الإجماع ثمةَ توقف العتق، ولزوم الإعتاق، وأنت قد عديت البطلان أصلاً.
فإن ادعى في الأصل حكماً غير ما قلنا: لم نسلم له.

والثالث بالحكم المطلوب بذلك الوصف.
والرابع: بالغرض المطلوب بذلك الحكم.
أما الأول فظاهر، مثل قولنا في مسح الرأس: إنه مسح، فلا يُسن تثليثه كمسح الخف ولا يلزم الاستنجاء؛ لأنه ليس إزالة نجاسة بمسح، ألا يُرى أنه إذا أحدث ولم يتلطخ به بدنه: لم يكن الاستنجاء سنة.

So the objection is invalidated.
And every sound statement in the original analogy mentioned by way of difference: mention it as a prevention. Like their statement regarding the emancipation of a mortgaged slave: it is an action of the mortgagor that meets the right of the mortgagee by nullification, so it is rejected like a sale. They said: it is not like a sale because it admits of revocation, unlike emancipation.
The correct approach is to say: analogy is for extending the ruling of the text, not changing it. And we do not concede the existence of this condition here.
Its explanation: the ruling of the original case is suspension of what admits of nullification and revocation, while you in the branch nullify entirely what does not admit of nullification and revocation. Similarly, if he analogizes it to the emancipation of a sick person: the ruling of consensus there is suspension of emancipation and its binding effect, and you have transferred nullification entirely.
If he claims for the original case a ruling other than what we said: we do not concede to him.

This is a chapter on explaining the aspects of rebutting contradiction.

Chapter on explaining the aspects of rebutting contradiction
And the summary of that: whenever the respondent can reconcile between what he claimed as the 'illa and what is perceived as a contradiction by harmonization between them, the contradiction is invalidated. Just as that occurs in contradictions in the court of justice between the claim and the testimony, and between testimonies, whenever reconciliation is possible and that becomes apparent, the contradiction is invalidated.
As for the first: it is by the description which he made the 'illa.
The second: by the meaning of the description by which the description became an 'illa, and it is the indication of its effect.
The third: by the ruling sought by that description.
The fourth: by the objective sought by that ruling.
As for the first, it is apparent, like our statement regarding wiping the head: it is wiping, so its threefold repetition is not sunna like wiping the khuff, and istinja is not required, because it is not removing impurity by wiping. Do you not see that if he had an event (hadath) and his body was not soiled, istinja would not be sunna.
And similarly our statement regarding the impurity exiting from other than the two passages: it is impure exiting from...
Source pages p. 638

Commentary
This passage is a usul al-fiqh discussion on how to rebut an objection (manaqada) against a claimed 'illa in qiyas. The author begins by stating a principle: every sound argument presented in opposition should be converted into a preventive argument. He gives the example of freeing a mortgaged slave: the opponent claims it is like a sale because both are actions that nullify the mortgagee's right, but the response is that emancipation admits of revocation (since the slave can be re-enslaved by the mortgagee?) while a sale does not, so the analogy fails. Then the author explains the correct method: the respondent must show that the condition for the analogy is not met — the original ruling (suspension of an action that admits revocation) is different from the branch (complete nullification). He further illustrates with the example of killing a protected person: the opponent analogizes it to accidental killing (both require payment of compensation), but the response is that the exact equivalent (qisas) is not legally possible, so the condition for extending the ruling is absent. The author then introduces the main topic: rebutting contradictions (manaqada) against effective 'illas. He states that if the respondent can reconcile (jama') between what he claims as the 'illa and the apparent contradiction, the objection is invalid, similar to reconciling contradictory statements in court. He then lists four aspects for analyzing the contradiction: (1) the description (wasf) itself, (2) the meaning of the description (its effect), (3) the ruling sought, and (4) the objective behind the ruling. As an example of the first aspect, he gives the case of wiping the head: the description is 'wiping' and the opponent might object that just as wiping the khuff is done three times, so should wiping the head. But the response is that the ruling (recommendation of three times) is based on the specific description of 'wiping' and the effect (it is not removing impurity), so the analogy fails. Similarly, for impurity exiting from other than the two passages, the text cuts off but likely continues. The passage is a core Hanafi usul methodology for defending an 'illa by showing that the objection can be reconciled through considering these four aspects.

Key Terms
المناقضة (al-munāqaḍa): Contradiction or objection raised against a claimed 'illa in qiyas showing an inconsistency.
التوفيق (al-tawfīq): Reconciliation between the claimed 'illa and the apparent contradictory evidence to remove the objection.
الوصف (al-waṣf): The description or attribute chosen as the 'illa (effective cause).
العلة المؤثرة (al-‘illa al-mu’aththira): An effective cause that actually produces a change in the ruling; contradication does not apply if its effect is valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the first of the four aspects through which a contradiction against an 'illa can be rebutted?
A) Through the ruling sought by the description
B) Through the description itself that was made the 'illa
C) Through the objective sought by the ruling
D) Through the meaning of the description that made it an 'illa

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method to rebut contradiction in qiyas is to reconcile through four aspects: the description, its effect, the ruling, and the objective.

Daf' by Description (Wasf) in Hanafi Usul
Explains daf' by description with two examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish daf' by description from daf' by ruling and apply the concept to examples from wudu and najasah.

Core Idea
Daf' by description uses the meaning of an attribute, not its wording, to refute a false analogy.

In Hanafi usul, when an opponent uses an analogy (qiyas) that seems to apply, you can refute it by showing that the underlying description (wasf) in the original case does not carry the same meaning in the new case. This is called daf' by description. The author explains that the description gains its legal force from its intended meaning, not its mere wording. He begins with this type even though the other (by ruling) is more correct, because it is more apparent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original description (wasf)
Locate the attribute that the analogy is based on, e.g., 'wiping' (masah) in wudu or 'impure exiting substance'.
The description is not taken literally but by its understood meaning.
2
Determine the meaning of the description in the original case
Analyze what legal meaning the description carries in its original context. For example, 'wiping' in wudu is a symbolic, non-physical purification; repetition is only for emphasis and not required.
This meaning is established by the text (nass) or linguistic usage.
3
Compare with the new case
Check if the same meaning holds for the new case. If the meaning differs, the analogy fails (daf' applies). Example: wiping on khuff does not require repetition, but istinja' does because its meaning is removal of actual impurity.
Repetition in istinja' is required because it cannot be fulfilled by part of the place.
4
Apply to the example of flowing vs. non-flowing impurity
Impurity that flows (like urine) necessitates purification and is hadath; non-flowing impurity (like dry blood) does not, because the meaning of 'exiting' implies flow.
If it does not flow, it is not considered exiting and thus not hadath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الدفع بمعنى الوصف: فإنما يصح؛ لأن الوصف لم يصر حجةً بصيغته، وإنما صار حجة بمعناه الذي يُعقل به. وذلك ضربان : أحدهما : ثابت بنفس الصيغة ظاهراً. والثاني: بمعناه الثابتُ به ،دلالةً على ما ذكرناه فيما سبق، فكان ثابتاً به لغةً، فصح الدفع فصح الدفع به، كما صح بالقسم الأول، فكان دفعاً بنفس الوصف. وهذا أحقُّ وجهي الدفع، لكن الأول أظهر، فبدأنا به. وذلك مثل قولنا : مَسَحُ في وضوء، فلم يكن التكرار فيه مسنوناً، كمسح الخف، ولا يلزم الاستنجاء؛ لأن معنى المسح تطهير حكمي غير معقول، والتكرار لتوكيد التطهير، فإذا لم يكن مراداً بطل التكرار. ألا يُرى أنه يتأدى ببعض محله بخلاف الاستنجاء؛ لأنه لإزالة عين النجاسة، وفي التكرار توكيده، ألا يُرى أنه لا يتأدى ببعضه، فصار ذلك نظير الغسل، وهذا معنى ثابت باسم المسح لغة. وكذلك قولنا : إنه نجس خارج، فكان حدثاً كالبول، ولا يلزم إذا لم يسل؛ لأن ما سال منه نجس أوجب تطهيراً، حتى وجب غسل ذلك الموضع ، فصار بمعنى البول ، وهذا غير خارج إذا لم يسل، حتى لم يتعلق به وجوب التطهير. وأما الدفع بالحكم فمثل قولنا في الغصب : إنه سبب لملك البدل، فكان سبباً لملك المُبدّل. ولا يلزم المدبَّرُ؛ لأنا جعلناه سبباً فيه أيضاً، لكنه امتنع حكمه لمانع، كالبيع يضاف إليه. ومثل قولنا في الجَمَل الصائل: إن المَصُّولَ عليه أتلفه لإحياء نفسه، والاستحلال لإحياء المهجة لا ينافي عصمة المتلف، كما إذا أتلفه دفعاً للمخمصة. ولا يلزم مالُ الباغي، وما يجري مجراه؛ لأن عصمته لم تبطل بهذا المعنى، فكان طرداً، لا نقضاً. 29 وكذلك متى قلنا في الدم إنه نجس خارج، فكان حدثاً : لم يلزم دم الاستحاضة؛ لأنه حدث أيضاً؛ لكن عمله امتنع لمانع. وأما الرابع فمثل قولنا نجس خارج. ولا يلزم دم المستحاضة، ودمُ صاحب الجرح السائل الدائم؛ لأن غرضنا التسوية بين هذا وبين الخارج من المخرج المعتاد، وذلك حَدَثُ، فإذا لزم صار عفواً ؛ لقيام وقت الصلاة، فكذلك هذا. وكذلك قولنا في التأمين: إنه ذكر، فكان سبيله الإخفاء. - ولا يلزم عليه الأذان، وتكبيرات الإمام ؛ لأن غرضنا أن أصل الذكر و الإخفاء.
As for the refutation by way of description, it is only valid because the description did not become a proof by its wording, but rather it became a proof by its meaning which is understood. And that is of two types: one is established by the wording itself in a apparent manner, and the second is established by its meaning that is established by it, as an indication of what we mentioned previously, so it is established by it linguistically, so refutation by it is valid, just as it is valid by the first type, so it is a refutation by the description itself. This is the more correct of the two types of refutation, but the first is more apparent, so we began with it. For example our saying: wiping in ablution, so repetition therein is not sunnah, like wiping the khuff, and it does not necessitate istinja' (washing of private parts) because the meaning of wiping is a legal purification that is not physical, and repetition is for emphasizing the purification, so if it is not intended, repetition becomes invalid. Do you not see that it is fulfilled by part of its place, unlike istinja' because it is for removing the actual impurity, and repetition emphasizes it, do you not see that it is not fulfilled by part of it, so it became analogous to washing, and this is a meaning established by the name 'wiping' linguistically. Similarly our saying: it is impure and exiting, so it is a hadath (ritual impurity) like urine, and it does not follow if it does not flow; because what flows from it is impure and necessitates purification, so washing that spot became obligatory, so it became like urine. And this is not exiting if it does not flow, so the obligation of purification is not attached to it. As for the refutation by the ruling: For example our saying in usurpation: it is a cause for ownership of the substitute, so it is a cause for ownership of the original. And it does not follow the mudabbar (slave whose freedom is deferred) because we also made it a cause there, but its ruling is prevented due to an obstacle, like a sale that is attributed to it. And like our saying about the attacking camel: the attacked person destroyed it to save his life, and causing the destruction to save the soul does not contradict the inviolability of the destroyed, as if he destroyed it to ward off starvation. And it does not follow the property of the rebel and what runs in its course; because his inviolability is not invalidated by this meaning, so it is a mere analogy (tard), not a refutation. 29 And similarly, whenever we say about blood that it is impure and exiting, so it is a hadath: it does not follow menstruation blood because it is also a hadath, but its effect is prevented due to an obstacle. As for the fourth: like our saying: impure and exiting. And it does not follow the blood of the mustahadah (woman with chronic uterine bleeding) and the blood of the person with a continuous weeping wound; because our purpose is to equate this with the exit from the usual exit, and that is a hadath, so if it follows, it becomes pardoned due to the prayer time; so also this. And similarly our saying about saying 'amen': it is remembrance, so its way is quietness. And it does not follow the adhan and the takbirat of the imam, because our purpose is that the origin of remembrance is quietness.
Source pages p. 640

Commentary
This passage from Usul al-Fiqh (Hanafi) discusses the concept of 'daf'' (دفع) which means refuting an objection or a qiyas (analogy). The author explains that there are two ways to perform a daf' : (1) by description (wasf) and (2) by ruling (hukm). Daf' by description is valid because the description (like 'wiping' or 'impure exiting substance') carries a meaning that functions as a proof, not just its wording. This meaning-based proof is actually stronger, but the author starts with the wording-based one for clarity. The author gives examples: wiping in wudu does not require repetition unlike istinja' because the meaning of wiping is symbolic purification. Similarly, impurity that exits (like blood or pus) is considered hadath (ritual impurity) only if it flows; if it does not flow, it does not necessitate purification. Daf' by ruling is illustrated with ghasb (usurpation): causing ownership of the substitute implies ownership of the original, but it does not apply to a mudabbar slave because the ruling is hindered. Another example is the attacking camel: destroying it to save one's life is allowed, but this does not apply to a rebel's property because the rebel's inviolability is not lost. The author then discusses a 'fourth' type (possibly a sub-type): considering blood as impure and exiting; chronic bleeding (istihadah) and continuous wound blood are treated as hadath but are pardoned for prayer due to the time. Finally, saying 'amen' quietly is compared to silent remembrance, but it does not require that adhan or takbirat be quiet because those have different purposes. This passage is typical of Hanafi usul, focusing on precise legal analogies and their limits.

Key Terms
دفع (daf'): Refutation of an objection or analogy by showing a difference in the legal cause or ruling.
وصف (wasf): Description or attribute that serves as the basis for the legal ruling.
حكم (hukm): Legal ruling derived from the description or cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does wiping in wudu not require repetition, unlike istinja'?
A) Because wiping is a symbolic purification that can be fulfilled by part of the place.
B) Because repetition is prohibited in all acts of purification.
C) Because wiping requires less water than istinja'.
D) Because the sunnah does not apply to wiping.

Key Takeaway
Daf' by description refutes an analogy by demonstrating that the legal attribute (wasf) in the original case carries a different meaning in the new case, thereby invalidating the extension.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
10 min
Daf' by Ruling (Hukm) in Hanafi Usul
Explains daf' by ruling with examples from ghasb, camel, and blood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply daf' by ruling to examples from ghasb, self-defense, and chronic bleeding, and distinguish it from daf' by description.

Core Idea
Daf' by ruling refutes an analogy by showing that the ruling in the original case is blocked by an obstacle (mani') in the new case.

The second type of daf' is by ruling (hukm). Here, the refutation does not depend on the meaning of the description, but on the fact that the ruling itself cannot be applied to the new case due to a preventing factor (mani'). The author gives examples from ghasb (usurpation), the attacking camel, and chronic bleeding (istihadah) to illustrate how a ruling is hindered without invalidating the original analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling and its cause
Find the ruling that is being analogized. For example, in ghasb: usurping property makes the usurper liable to compensate, and the cause is that he took possession of something valuable.
The cause (sabab) is 'taking possession of property'.
2
Recognize the obstacle (mani')
In the new case, a preventing factor blocks the ruling. For instance, in the case of a mudabbar slave (whose freedom is deferred), the ruling of compensation is blocked because the slave is already owned by the master in a way that prevents substitution.
The obstacle is a legal barrier that prevents the cause from producing its effect.
3
Apply to self-defense: destroying the attacking camel
The ruling that destroying property requires compensation does not apply when the attacker destroys the camel to save his life, because necessity (darurah) is an obstacle that permits the act.
The owner's inviolability is not lost; the act is justified by necessity.
4
Apply to chronic bleeding (istihadah) and wound blood
Blood exiting the body is normally hadath, but for mustahadah and continuous wound bleeding, the ruling is pardoned due to the difficulty of maintaining wudu during prayer times. The obstacle here is 'necessity' and 'time constraint'.
The ruling is still valid in principle, but its effect is lifted due to the obstacle.

As for the refutation by way of description, it is only valid because the description did not become a proof by its wording, but rather it became a proof by its meaning which is understood. مانع (mani'): An obstacle that prevents the cause from producing its ruling.

Key Takeaway

Tarjih by Strength of Effect (Quwwat al-Athar)
Explains tarjih by strength of effect with the tawl al-hurrah example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi principle of tarjih by strength of effect (quwwat al-athar) and apply it to distinguish between stronger and weaker proofs.

Core Idea
Prefer a proof whose underlying meaning (athar) is stronger, just as istihsan is preferred over qiyas.

This lesson introduces the first subtype of the second type of tarjih: preponderance by strength of effect. The author defines athar as the meaning or trace of a proof and explains that when this meaning is stronger, the proof is more deserving of preference. This concept is fundamental for evaluating conflicting evidences in Hanafi usul.

Guided Path
1
Identify the concept of athar
What does the author mean by 'athar'? It is the meaning or trace of a proof. In the context of tarjih, athar is the effective force that makes one proof outweigh another.
Think of athar as the 'punch' of the dalil.
2
Understand the basis for tarjih by athar
Tarjih by strength of effect is based on an excellent attribute in the proof itself, such as narrator precision or continuity of transmission. Subjective qualities like piety are not used because they are not quantifiable.
Contrast this with other types of tarjih that rely on number of sources or adherence to rulings.
3
Apply the principle to the example
Analyze the Hanafi position on tawl al-hurrah: a free man with a free wife may marry a slave girl because the marriage contract is valid and the man has capacity. The Shafi'i position relies on an effect-based description (lineage debasement), but the Hanafi view prevails because the permissibility effect is stronger.
The example shows how tarjih by athar works in practice.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
** وأما القسم الثاني : فعلى أربعة أوجه:
1 - الترجيح بقوة الأثر.
2- والترجيح بقوة ثباته على الحكم المشهود به.

أما الأول : فلأن الأثر معنى الحجة فمهما قوي: كان أولى؛ لفضل وصف في الحجة على مثال الاستحسان في معارضة القياس، وهو كالخبر لما صار حجةً بالاتصال : ازداد قوةً بما يزيده قوةً في ذلك المعنى بضبط الراوي وإتقانه، وسلامته عن الانقطاع على ما مرَّ ذكره. وليس كذلك فضل عدالة بعض الشهود على عدالة بعض؛ لأنه ليس بذي حد، ولا متنوع، بل هو التقوى، ولا وقوف على حدوده. مثاله : ما قلنا في طول الحُرَّة : إنه لا يمنع الحرَّ من نكاح الأمة. وقال الشافعي رحمه الله : يمنع ؛ لأنه يُرِقُ ماءَه على غُنية، وذلك حرام على كل حر كالذي تحته حرة، وهذا وصف بين الأثر.

** As for the second section: it is of four types: 1) Preponderance by strength of effect. 2) Preponderance by strength of its adherence to the witnessed ruling. 3) Preponderance by the multiplicity of its sources. 4) Preponderance by its absence when it is absent. As for the first: because the effect is the meaning of the proof; whenever it is stronger, it is more deserving due to an excellent attribute in the proof, like istihsan in opposing qiyas. It is like the report: when it becomes a proof by continuity, it increases in strength by what increases its strength in that meaning, such as the precision of the narrator, his mastery, and his freedom from discontinuity, as has passed. But the superiority of some witnesses' uprightness over that of others is not like that, because it has no limit, nor is it varied; rather it is piety, and there is no ascertainment of its limits. Its example is what we said about the tall free woman: it does not prevent a free man from marrying a slave girl. And al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, said: it prevents because it causes his lineage to be servile to a wealthy woman, and that is forbidden for every free man like the one who has a free wife. And this is an effect-based description. And we said: it is permissible because it is a marriage that a slave can own with his master's permission if he pays a dowry suitable for both free and slave women, and he says: marry whomever you wish, so the free man owns it like the rest
Source pages p. 646

Commentary
This passage introduces the second main type of tarjih (preponderance) in Hanafi usul, which has four subtypes. The first subtype is preponderance by 'strength of effect' (quwwat al-athar). The athar (effect) is the meaning or trace of the legal proof; when it is stronger, the proof is more deserving of preference. The author compares this to istihsan, which is a stronger effect opposing qiyas. For transmitted reports, strength comes from attributes like narrator precision and continuity of chain. However, the superior uprightness of one witness over another is not a valid basis for tarjih because piety is not quantifiable. The example given is the case of a free man with a free wife (tawl al-hurrah) marrying a slave girl: Hanafis say it is permissible because the man has the capacity, similar to a slave with permission. Shafi'i holds it is forbidden because it debases his lineage. The text sides with the Hanafi view, noting that Shafi'i's argument is an effect-based description, but the Hanafi position is based on the permissibility of the marriage act.

Key Terms
أثر (athar): The effect or trace of a proof; the meaning that makes the proof strong.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; an exception to qiyas based on a stronger effect.
طول الحرة (tawl al-hurrah): The existence of a free wife (literally 'the tall free woman'), used as a technical term for a free wife's effect on the husband's marriage options.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preponderance; giving preference to one proof over another when they conflict.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the justification for tarjih by strength of effect (quwwat al-athar)?
A) Because the effect is the meaning of the proof, and a stronger effect indicates a more deserving proof.
B) Because the effect is always measurable and quantifiable.
C) Because the effect is the same as the number of transmitters.
D) Because the effect eliminates all contrary evidence.

Key Takeaway
Tarjih by strength of effect prefers proofs whose underlying meaning (athar) is stronger; this principle is distinct from relying on subjective qualities like piety.

Tarjih bi-Quwwat al-Athar: Preponderance by Strength of the Effect
Definition and example of tarjih bi-quwwat al-athar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the meaning of tarjih bi-quwwat al-athar, apply it to the example of a free man marrying a slave woman, and evaluate the Hanafi versus Shafi'i reasoning.

Core Idea
Strength of effect (athar) derives from intrinsic qualities like narrator precision, not external counts.

This passage introduces the first of four aspects of the second category of tarjih: preponderance by the strength of the effect (athar). The author explains that the effect is the inherent evidential force of a proof, and when it is stronger, it deserves preference. He illustrates this with the Hanafi position on a free man marrying a slave woman while having a free wife, contrasting it with the Shafi'i view. Through this example, you will see how intrinsic qualities of evidence (such as the logical half-life of slavery) function as a basis for tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its basis
The passage defines tarjih bi-quwwat al-athar as preponderance due to the strength of the effect (athar) itself. This is compared to istihsan opposing qiyas and the increase in strength of a hadith due to narrator precision.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof for the example
The Hanafi position is that a free man with a free wife may still marry a slave woman. The proof relies on the principle that slavery only halves the solution (tansif al-hall) in matters that accept division (like talaq, idda, qasm), and marriage does not accept such division. Thus the effect of slavery is limited.
3
Examine the Shafi'i counter-proof
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that it is prohibited because it debases the free man's lineage while he has a free wife, analogous to wasting seed. The passage then critiques this reasoning as having a weak effect.
4
Apply the tarjih mechanism
Compare the two proofs based on the strength of their effect (athar). The Hanafi proof has a stronger effect because it relies on a consistent principle (half-effect of slavery) that can be verified across several rulings, while the Shafi'i proof is weaker because it relies on an analogy that does not hold logically (enslaving is less than wasting, and 'azl is permissible).
5
Extend to the case of a kitaabi slave woman
The same principle applies: the Hanafi view allows marriage to a kitaabi slave woman because the effect of slavery does not add a prohibition beyond the effect of religion. The opponent's view fails because the combination of slavery and disbelief does not create a stronger effect.

وقلنا: إنه جائز ؛ لأنه نكاح يملكه العبد بإذن مولاه إذا دفع إليه مهراً يصلح للحرة والأمة جميعاً، وقال: تزوج من شئت، فيملكه الحر كسائر الأنكحة، وهذا قوي الأثر؛ لأن الحرية من صفات الكمال، وأسباب الكرامة، والرق من أسباب تنصيف الحل، فيجب أن يكون الرقيق في النصف مثل الحر في الكل فأما أن يزداد أثر الرق، ويتسع حله: فلا، وهذا أثر ظهرت قوته ويزداد وضوحاً بالتأمل في أحوال البشر. ألا يُرى أنه أُحِلَّ لرسولنا عليه الصلاة والسلام التسع، أو إلى ما لا يتناهى ؛ لفضله وشرفه
فأما ما ذكر من الأثر فضعيف بحقيقته ؛ لأن الإرقاق دون التضييع، وذلك جائز بالعزل بإذن الحرة، فالإرقاق أولى ، وضعيف بأحواله، فإن نكاح الأمة جائز لمن يملك سُرية يستغني بها عنه.
ومن ذلك قولهم في نكاح الأمة الكتابية : إنه لا يجوز للمسلم؛ لأن الرق من الموانع.

And it is not the same as the superiority of the integrity of some witnesses over the integrity of others because it is not bounded or varied; rather it is piety, and its limits cannot be known. And this is an effect whose strength has appeared and increases in clarity by reflecting on the conditions of humans. The number as was said in divorce, 'idda, division, and hudud, and that is specific to what accepts rulings, and the marriage of a woman in itself, as opposed to men, is not multiple. Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the first aspect of the second category of tarjih (preponderance of evidence), namely الترجيح بقوة الأثر (preponderance by the strength of the effect). The author explains that the effect (athar) is the meaning of evidence (hujja); the stronger it is, the more deserving it is of preference. This is analogous to istihsan overriding qiyas. He then provides an example: the issue of a free man marrying a slave woman while having a free wife. The Hanafi position is that it does not prevent such marriage, while Imam al-Shafi'i holds that it does prevent it because it debases the free man's lineage. The Hanafi response argues that the effect of slavery is limited to half of the solution (tansif al-hall) in matters that accept division, such as divorce, 'idda, division, and hudud. Therefore, slavery does not expand its effect beyond that. The free man, by his freedom, has full capacity. The author contrasts the strength of the two effects: the Shafi'i evidence is weak because it relies on unnecessary preservation, while the Hanafi evidence is strong because it aligns with the nature of freedom and slavery. Then the passage extends to the case of a kitaabi slave woman: the opponent argues that the combination of slavery and disbelief creates a stronger prohibition, but the Hanafi view allows it because the religion itself (Christianity/Judaism) permits marriage to free women, so slavery does not add an extra prohibition. This demonstrates the principle of tarjih by the strength of the effect.

Key Terms
الترجيح بقوة الأثر (at-tarjīḥ bi-quwwat al-athar): Preponderance given to a piece of evidence based on the strength of its inherent evidential effect, as opposed to external factors like the number of witnesses.
الأثر (al-athar): The effect or evidential force of a proof; essentially the probative value of the evidence itself.
تنصيف الحل (tansīf al-ḥall): Halving of the solution; a principle that slavery halves the legal effect in matters that accept division (like talaq, idda, qasm, hudud).

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Because marrying a slave woman is always better for the free man.

Key Takeaway
Tarjih bi-quwwat al-athar gives precedence to evidence with stronger intrinsic evidential qualities, as demonstrated by the Hanafi position on a free man marrying a slave woman.

The Weakest Tarjih: Understanding al-‘Aks (Inverse)
Defines and illustrates the weakest mode of tarjih using inverse reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the ‘aks (inverse) as a mode of tarjih in Hanafi usul, apply it to given examples, and explain why it is considered the weakest form of preference.

Core Idea
The absence of a description implies the absence of its ruling, but non-existence itself carries no legal weight — making ‘aks the weakest tarjih.

In this passage, the author introduces the fourth and weakest type of tarjih: the inverse. Unlike direct evidence, the ‘aks relies on the absence of a description to infer the absence of the ruling attached to it. This principle is illustrated through three Hanafi positions to show its validity despite its weakness. Let’s dissect the reasoning and examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of ‘aks
The ‘aks states that if a ruling is attached to a specific description (sabab), then when that description is absent, the ruling is absent. This is a form of reasoning by contrapositive.
Non-existence (absence) does not itself create a ruling; the connection is made through the description.
2
Identify the requirement: ruling must be attached to a description
The inverse only works if the ruling is directly linked to a description. For example, wiping (mash) is a description that entails the ruling of being a valid act of wiping. If something is not wiping, it lacks that ruling.
If the description is not the direct cause, the inverse may not hold.
3
Analyze the first example: wiping the head vs. pillar
Hanafis argue that wiping the head is mash (wiping), and the inverse holds: what is not wiping (e.g., gargling) does not have the ruling of being a pillar. The opposing view calling it a rukn does not invert because gargling is repeated but not a rukn.
This shows that the Hanafi position is preferred because its inverse is consistent.
4
Analyze the second example: brotherhood and zakat
Hanafis define brotherhood as a kinship that prohibits marriage and necessitates manumission. This definition inverts for paternal cousins (they also necessitate manumission). The opposing view (allowing zakat between brothers) does not invert because placing zakat on an unbeliever is not permissible.
Consistent inverse strengthens the Hanafi position.
5
Analyze the third example: selling food for food
Hanafis hold that selling food for food is a sale of an ‘ayn (specific item), so delivery is not a condition. The inverse holds for currency exchange and salam capital (both are debts, so delivery is required). The opposing ‘illa (usury reasoning) does not invert because salam sale, though not involving riba goods, still requires delivery to avoid debt-for-debt.
This illustrates how the inverse acts as a tarjih factor.
6
Recognize why ‘aks is the weakest tarjih
The absence of a description does not directly establish a ruling; it only provides an indication. Therefore, ‘aks is weaker than direct textual evidence, consensus, or stronger analogies.
It can tilt the scale when other evidence is equal, but never overrides stronger proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع فهو العكس الذي ذكرناه، وهو أضعف وجوه الترجيح ؛ لأن العدم لا يتعلق به حكم، لكن الحكم إذا تعلق بوصف، عُدم عند عَدَمِه : كان ذلك أوضح لصحته ، فصلح أن يدخل في أقسام الترجيح. وذلك مثل قولنا في مسح الرأس : إنه مسح، وهو ينعكس بما ليس ،بمسح وقولُهم : ركن : لا ينعكس؛ لأن المضمضة تتكرر وليست بركن. وكذلك قولنا في الأُخوَّة : إنها قرابةٌ محرمة للنكاح لإيجاب العتق : أحق من قولهم : يجوز وضع زكاة أحدهما في الآخر؛ لأن ما قلناه ينعكس في بني الأعمام ، وقولهم لا ينعكس؛ لأن وضع الزكاة في الكافر لا يحل، ولا يجب به عتق . وكذلك قولنا في بيع الطعام بالطعام : إنه مبيع عين، فلا يشترط قبضه : أولى من قولهم مالان لو قوبل كلّ واحدٍ منهما بجنسه حرم ربا الفضل ؛ لأنه ينعكس ببدل الصرف ورأس مال السلم؛ لأنه دين بدين، ولا ينعكس تعليله ؛ لأن بيع السلم لم يشمل أموال الربا ومع ذلك وجب فيه القبض ؛ احترازاً عن الكالي بالكالي (1) قوله : احترازاً عن الكاليء بالكاليء : يعني وهو منهي عنه، كما أخرجه ابن أبي شيبة وإسحاق والبزار وابن عدي عن عبد الله بن عمر قال: «نهى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أن يباع كالى بكاليء». يعني: ديناً بدين.
As for the fourth, it is the inverse that we mentioned, and it is the weakest mode of preference because non-existence does not attach to a ruling. However, if a ruling is attached to a description, and it is absent when its description is absent, that is clearer for its validity, so it is suitable to enter into the divisions of preference. That is like our statement regarding wiping the head: it is wiping, and it is inverted by what is not wiping. And their statement: a pillar does not invert, because gargling is repeated and is not a pillar. Likewise our statement regarding brotherhood: it is a kinship that prohibits marriage and necessitates manumission, is more deserving than their statement that it is permissible to place the zakat of one upon the other, because what we say is inverted in the case of paternal cousins, and their statement does not invert because placing zakat upon an unbeliever is not permissible, and manumission is not obligatory thereby. And likewise our statement regarding selling food for food: it is a sale of an 'ayn, so its delivery is not a condition, is preferable to their statement that if two properties are each confronted with their own kind, the usury of excess is prohibited, because it is inverted by the exchange of money and the capital of salam, because it is a debt for a debt, and their reasoning does not invert because the salam sale did not include usurious goods, yet delivery was obligatory in it as a precaution against kāli' bi kāli'. (1) He said: a precaution against kāli' bi kāli': meaning it is prohibited, as narrated by Ibn Abi Shayba, Ishaq, al-Bazzar, and Ibn Adi from Abdullah ibn Umar who said: The Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, prohibited selling kāli' for kāli'. Meaning: debt for debt.
Source pages p. 651

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth and weakest type of tarjih (preference) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the 'aks (inverse). The author explains that non-existence itself does not entail a ruling, but if a ruling is attached to a specific description, the absence of that description implies the absence of the ruling. This becomes a valid but weak form of tarjih because it relies on the inverse relationship. Several examples illustrate the principle: The Hanafi position that wiping the head is 'mash' (wiping) is preferred over calling it a 'rukn' (pillar) because the inverse holds; if it were a pillar, gargling would also be a pillar, which it is not. Similarly, in inheritance, defining brotherhood as a kinship that prohibits marriage and necessitates manumission is preferred over the opposing view that zakat can be placed between them, because the Hanafi definition inverts in the case of paternal cousins (i.e., the inverse also indicates manumission), whereas the opposing view does not. In the sale of food for food, the Hanafi ruling that it is a sale of an 'ayn (specific item) and therefore does not require immediate delivery is preferred because the inverse holds for currency exchange and salam capital (both are debts), whereas the opposing 'illa does not invert. The footnote explains the hadith prohibiting kāli' bi kāli' (debt for debt).

Key Terms
الترجيح (al-tarjīḥ): Preferring one evidence or ruling over another due to a stronger attribute
العكس (al-‘aks): Inverse: using the absence of a description to indicate the absence of the ruling attached to it

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The ruling that brotherhood is a kinship prohibiting marriage because it is inverted by paternal cousins
C) The ruling that selling food for food is a sale of ayn because it is inverted by currency exchange
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The ‘aks is a valid but weak mode of tarjih in Hanafi usul, relying on the absence of a description to imply absence of its ruling; it never overrides stronger evidence.

Priority of Essence Over State in Tarjih
Establishes essence-over-state tarjih rule

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that essence-based tarjih (dhāt) precedes state-based tarjih (hāl) in cases of conflict, and distinguish between dhāt and hāl in legal reasoning.

Core Idea
In legal preference, essence (dhāt) always takes priority over incidental state (hāl); the subsidiary cannot abrogate the original.

This lesson explores a fundamental principle in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: when two conflicting grounds for tarjih exist—one based on intrinsic essence (dhāt) and the other on incidental state (hāl)—the essence prevails. The passage explains the reasoning and provides inheritance examples to illustrate the rule, and notes that Imam al-Shafi‘i was unaware of this limit.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish dhāt and hāl
Identify the two types of tarjih: essence-based (dhāt) concerns the core nature of an entity; state-based (hāl) concerns incidental circumstances. Understand that dhāt is primary and hāl is secondary.
Think of dhāt as the substance, hāl as an attribute.
2
State the Hanafi rule
When these two grounds conflict, the essence-based tarjih is stronger and takes precedence. The state-based cannot abrogate the essence-based because the subsidiary (hāl) cannot invalidate the original (dhāt).
The author provides two reasons: essence is prior, and state depends on essence.
3
Analyze the reasons
First reason: essence is prior temporally and logically, so its preference is like a finalized ruling not subject to abrogation. Second: if state were preferred, it would abrogate essence, but a subsidiary cannot be an abrogator.
The analogy to naskh (abrogation) is used here to emphasize the priority.
4
Apply to inheritance examples
Work through the examples: (a) Son's son of a brother (full or consanguine) is preferred over paternal uncle because the former is closer in essence of kinship. (b) Paternal aunt by mother is preferred over maternal uncle because she is closer in essence. (c) When essences are equal, state decides (e.g., full brother's son vs. consanguine brother's son).
These are the thamarah al-khilaf showing practical impact.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
The author states that Imam al-Shafi‘i was unaware of this limit. According to Hanafi, his view would be valid but less strong. The disagreement is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
The author adds that Shafi‘i is excused and rewarded for correct opinions in other matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث : فإن الأصل في ذلك : أن كل موجود مما يحتمل الحدوث موجود بصورته ومعناه الذي هو حقيقة وجوده، وتقوم به أحواله الحادثة على وجوده، فإذا تعارض ضربا ترجيح في الذات، والثاني في الحال على مضادة الوجه الأول: كان الرجحان في الذات أحق منه في الحال؛ لوجهين: أحدهما: أن الذات أسبق من الحال فيصير كاجتهادِ أُمضي حكمه، لا يحتمل النسخ بغيره، ولأن الحال قائمة بالذات، فلو اعتبرنا على مضادة الأول: كان ناسخاً للأول مبطلاً له، والتبع لا يصلح مبطلاً للأصل، ناسخاً له، وهذا عندنا. والشافعي رحمه الله خفي عليه هذا الحد، وهو معذورٌ في مَزَلِّ القدم، والمصيب في مراكز الزَّلَل مأجور. وبيانه فيما هو موضع الإجماع: قولنا في ابنِ ابنِ الأخ لأب وأم، أو لأب : إنه أحقُّ بالتعصيب من العم؛ لأن هذا راجح في ذات القرابة، والعم راجع بحاله. وكذلك العمة لأم مع الخال لأب وأم : أحقُّ بالثلثين، والثلث للخال؛ لأنها راجحة في ذات القرابة، والخال راجح بحاله. وابن الأخ لأب وأم أحقُّ من ابن الأخ لأب؛ لاستوائهما في الذات، فيترجح بالحال وابنُ ابن الأخ لأب وأم لا يرث مع ابن الأخ لأب؛
As for the third section: the principle in that is that every existent that is subject to origination exists in its form and its meaning which is the reality of its existence, and its incidental states subsist upon its existence. So if two modes of preference conflict – one based on essence and the second based on state, in opposition to the first mode – then the preference in essence is more deserving than that in state, for two reasons: first, that essence is prior to state, so it becomes like a ruling that has been finalized, not subject to abrogation by another; and because the state subsists by the essence, so if we considered it in opposition to the first, it would be an abrogator of the first, invalidating it, and the subsidiary cannot be an invalidator of the original, nor an abrogator of it. And this is according to us. And al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, was unaware of this limit, and he is excused in the slip of the foot, and the one who hits the mark in positions of error is rewarded. And its clarification is in what is a matter of consensus: our statement regarding the son's son of a brother by both parents or by father: he is more entitled to 'asaba (agnatic inheritance) than the paternal uncle, because this one is preponderant in the essence of kinship, and the uncle is preponderant by his state. Likewise, the paternal aunt by mother with the maternal uncle by both parents is more entitled to two-thirds, and the one-third is for the maternal uncle, because she is preponderant in essence of kinship, and the maternal uncle is preponderant by his state. And the son's brother by both parents is more entitled than the brother's son by father, because they are equal in essence, so the preponderance is by state. And the son's son of a brother by both parents does not inherit with the brother's son by father.
Source pages p. 652

Commentary
This passage explains the third type of tarjih (preference) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: when two contradictory bases for tarjih exist – one relating to the essence (dhāt) of an entity and the other to its state (hāl) – the former is stronger. The reasoning is that essence is prior to state temporally and logically; the state depends on the essence, so giving weight to the state over the essence would be like abrogating the primary by the secondary. The author notes that Imam al-Shafi‘i did not recognize this specific limit, but he is excused. The principle is illustrated by inheritance examples: In the ‘asaba hierarchy, the son’s son of a brother (a closer relative in essence) is preferred over a paternal uncle (who has a connection by state, i.e., being a paternal relative but further in essence). Similarly, a paternal aunt by mother (a female agnate) is closer in essence than a maternal uncle (a non-agnate), so she takes two-thirds and the maternal uncle one-third. When relatives are equal in essence, like two brothers’ sons, the one from both parents is preferred by state (being full sibling) over one from father only. The principle ensures consistency in the inheritance system.

Key Terms
الذات (al-dhāt): The essence or core nature of an entity, its intrinsic reality
الحال (al-ḥāl): An incidental state or circumstance that attaches to an essence
النسخ (al-naskh): Abrogation – here used metaphorically for one tarjih overriding another

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the son's son of a brother (by both parents) more entitled to 'asaba inheritance than the paternal uncle?
A) Because his state is stronger
B) Because his essence of kinship is closer
C) Because he shares more property with the deceased
D) Because he is older

Key Takeaway
Essence-based tarjih (dhāt) always overrides state-based tarjih (hāl) in Hanafi legal reasoning; the subsidiary cannot abrogate the original.

Tarjih of Dhāt over Ḥāl: The Zakat Example
Application of tarjih principle to mixing zakat properties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi tarjih principle that essence (dhāt) preponderates over state (ḥāl) to determine whether different forms of wealth are combined for zakat.

Core Idea
A profit is essentially tied to its origin; a gift is only circumstantially linked. Essence overrides temporal nearness.

In this passage, Imam Abu Hanifa demonstrates a key usul principle using a concrete zakat scenario. He shows that when determining zakat on mixed properties, the essential connection (dhāt) of wealth to its origin takes precedence over mere temporal closeness (ḥāl). This lesson unpacks the reasoning and applies it to similar cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and rulings
Read the passage and list the three sub-cases: sale price of camels, a gift, and profit from sale price. Note the ruling for each regarding combining with existing cash.
2
Distinguish the connections
For each sub-case, determine whether the wealth is connected in essence (dhāt) or by state (ḥāl) to the existing cash or to its origin.
Sale price is essentially connected to camels, profit to sale price, gift close by time.
3
Apply the tarjih principle
State the Hanafi rule: dhāt preponderates over ḥāl. Explain how this rule determines whether to start a new year or combine.
Essence – start new year; state – combine.
4
Recognize the limit
Note that preponderance by precaution (ihtiyat) is not considered here. The principle is based on the nature of the connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى هذا قال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله : في رجل له خَمْس من الإبل السائمة مضى من حولها عشرة أشهر، ثم ملك ألف درهم، ثم تم الحول في الإبل فزكاها ثم باعها بألف درهم إنه لا يضمها إلى الألف التي عنده، لكنه يستأنف الحول. فإن وهبت له ألف أخرى ضمها إلى الألف الأولى؛ لأنها أقرب. فإن تصرف في ثمن الإبل، فربح ثمن الإبل، فربح ألفاً ضمّ الربح إلى أصله وإن بَعد عن الحول ولا يُعتبر الرجحان بالاحتياط في الزكاة؛ لما قلنا إن الألف الربح متصل بأصله ذاتاً، متصل بالألف الأخرى حالاً، وهي القُرب إلى مضي الحول، والذات أحقُّ من الحال. وإنما ذكرنا من هذه الأقسام أمثلةً معدودة؛ لتكون أصلاً لغيرها من الفروع.
And on this Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) said: concerning a man who has five grazing camels, ten months of their year having passed, then he acquires one thousand dirhams, then the year is completed for the camels and he pays zakat on them, then he sells them for one thousand dirhams: he does not add that (sale price) to the thousand he already has, but starts a new year. If he is gifted another thousand, he adds it to the first thousand because it is closer. If he transacts with the price of the camels and profits—the price yields a profit of a thousand—he adds the profit to its origin even if it is far from the year, and preponderance by precaution is not considered in zakat; because, as we said, the thousand profit is connected to its origin in essence, and connected to the other thousand by state—that is nearness to the passing of the year—and essence is more entitled than state. And we have only mentioned from these sections a few examples to serve as principles for other branches.
Source pages p. 653

Commentary
This passage provides a concrete fiqh example of the tarjih principle using zakat rules. It is a ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa illustrating how the preponderance of essence (dhāt) over state (ḥāl) applies in determining whether different forms of wealth should be combined for zakat. The scenario: A person owns five grazing camels (nisab for camels). After ten months of the zakat year, he acquires 1000 dirhams (not yet a full year). Then the camel year completes, he pays zakat on the camels, then sells them for 1000 dirhams. Question: Should the 1000 dirhams from the sale be added to the 1000 dirhams he already had to determine nisab? The ruling: No, because the sale price is connected to the camels (its origin) in essence (dhāt), but it is only connected to the existing dirhams by state (ḥāl)—i.e., temporal nearness to the end of the year. Since essence preponderates over state, he starts a new year for the sale price. However, if he is gifted another 1000 dirhams, that is essentially separate from the first 1000? Actually, the text says: if gifted another thousand, he adds it to the first because it is 'closer'—meaning the gift is closer in time to the end of the year? Wait, careful: 'closer' likely means the gift is more proximate to the existing thousand in terms of the state of possession? Actually, the principle: the gift is added because it is 'closer' to the first thousand? Let's analyze. The text: 'فإن وهبت له ألف أخرى ضمها إلى الألف الأولى؛ لأنها أقرب.' The gift is added to the first thousand because it is closer—meaning the gift is closer in time to the haul of the first thousand? But the first thousand hasn't completed its year? Actually, the first thousand was acquired at month 10 of the camel year. So when the camel year completes, the first thousand has only 2 months into its own year. The gift later? The text doesn't specify exact timing. The reasoning may be that the gift is more closely related to the first thousand by state (both are cash) and the time proximity. Then: if he trades with the camel sale price and profits, the profit is added to its origin (the sale price) even if that origin is far from the year. This is because the profit is essentially connected to its origin. The passage concludes that we do not consider preponderance by precaution in zakat. The examples are given to serve as foundational guidelines.

Key Terms
ذات (dhāt): Essence or inherent connection, e.g., profit attached to its original asset.
حال (ḥāl): A state or circumstantial connection, e.g., temporal proximity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because it is essentially connected to the camels, overriding temporal nearness.
C) Because the man paid zakat on the camels already.
D) Because the year for camels is different from cash.

Key Takeaway
The essential connection (dhāt) of wealth to its origin overrides circumstantial temporal nearness (ḥāl) when determining whether to combine properties for zakat.

Fourth Type of Tarjih: Four Ways of Preponderance
Enumeration and critique of four tarjih methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four methods of tarjih in qiyas, explain the Hanafi critique of tarjih by predominance of similarities and by generality, and apply the valid methods.

Core Idea
Not all methods of preferring one analogy are valid; only those that avoid circularity and distinguish form from meaning.

The Hanafi school of usul holds that when two analogies conflict, certain methods exist to prefer one over the other. This passage from al-Bazdawi presents the fourth category of tarjih, which comprises four specific ways. Two of these—tarjih by predominance of similarities and tarjih by generality—are critically examined and rejected, while tarjih by another qiyas and tarjih by fewer attributes are upheld. Understanding the reasoning behind these rejections is essential for sound analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Enumerate the four methods
Read the passage and list the four methods of tarjih mentioned: (1) tarjih by another qiyas, (2) tarjih by predominance of similarities, (3) tarjih by generality, and (4) tarjih by fewer attributes.
The first and fourth are accepted; the second and third are rejected.
2
Analyze the critique of tarjih by predominance of similarities
The text gives an example: comparing a brother to a cousin on multiple points (zakat, marriage, testimony, retaliation) suggests he resembles the cousin more than the child. The Hanafi critique: each similarity can itself be the basis of a separate qiyas, so this method reduces to tarjih by another qiyas, making it redundant and invalid.
The brother-child comparison is just one aspect (consanguinity), while the brother-cousin comparison involves many aspects, but each aspect is a potential qiyas.
3
Analyze the critique of tarjih by generality
Some argue that a description like 'taste' (ta'm) is more general (applies to both little and much) and thus should be preferred. The Hanafi critique: a description is subsidiary to the text; a general text does not override a specific text. In Hanafi usul, the 'illah is considered by its meaning, not its form; generality is merely a form, not a meaningful attribute.
The text also notes that the opposing school (Mutakallimūn) does not consider transference (ta'addi), so their reliance on generality is inconsistent.
4
Understand the acceptance of tarjih by fewer attributes
The text states: 'an entity with one attribute is more entitled than an entity with two attributes.' Simplicity is preferred because a cause with fewer conditions is more likely to be the effective cause. This method is accepted without critique.
This is a straightforward principle: fewer conditions mean broader applicability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع : فعلى أربعة أوجه: -١ - ترجيح القياس بقياس آخر، وما يجري مجراه على ما قلناه. والثاني: الترجيح بغلبة الأشباه مثل قولهم : إن الأخ يُشبه الولد بوجه، وهو المحرمية، ويُشبه ابن العم بسائر الوجوه، مثل وضع الزكاة من الطرفين، وحل الحليلة وقبول الشهادة ووجوب القصاص من الطرفين، فكان هذا أولى، وهذا باطل؛ لأن كل شبه يصلح قياساً، فيصير کترجيح القياس بقياس آخر. والثالث : الترجيح بالعموم، مثل قولهم: إن الطعم أحقُّ؛ لأنه يعمُّ القليل والكثير، وهذا باطل؛ لأن الوصف فرعُ النص، والنص العام والخاص سواء عندنا وعندكم الخاص يقضي على العام، فكيف صار العام أحقَّ من الذي هو فرعه؟ ولأن التعدي غير مقصود عندكم، فبطل الترجيح به، وعندنا صار علة بمعناه لا بصورته، والعموم صورة. ٤ والرابع: الترجيح بقلة الأوصاف، فيقال: ذات وصف: أحق من ذات وصفين.
As for the fourth (type of tarjih), it is in four ways: First: preferring one qiyas by another qiyas, and what runs in its course on what we have said. Second: preferring by predominance of similarities, like their saying: the brother resembles the child in one aspect, which is consanguinity, and resembles the cousin in all other aspects, such as the placement of zakat from both sides, permissibility of marriage, acceptance of testimony, and obligation of retaliation from both sides, so this (the brother resembling the cousin) is more appropriate; and this is invalid because every similarity is suitable as a qiyas, so it becomes like preferring one qiyas by another qiyas. Third: preferring by generality, like their saying: the description of 'ta'm' (taste) is more entitled because it includes little and much; and this is invalid because the description is subsidiary to the text, and the general text and specific text are equal in our view and in your view—the specific overrides the general; so how can the general become more entitled than that which is its branch? And because transference (ta'addi) is not intended in your view, so preference by it becomes invalid; and in our view it became a cause ('illah) by its meaning not by its form, and generality is form. Fourth: preferring by fewer attributes, so it is said: an entity with one attribute is more entitled than an entity with two attributes.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth category of tarjih (preponderance) in four sub-types, as discussed in Usul al-Bazdawi. The text is a matn that enumerates and critiques each. (1) Tarjih by another qiyas: prefer one analogy over another using a different analogy; this is valid. (2) Tarjih by predominance of similarities (ghalabat al-ashbah): for example, comparing the brother to the cousin (ibn al-amm) on many points (zakat, marriage, testimony, retaliation) makes him more like the cousin than like the child, but this is invalid because each similarity could be a basis for a separate qiyas, thus reducing to type (1). (3) Tarjih by generality (ʿumūm): e.g., the description 'ta'm' (taste) is more general and thus should be preponderant, but this is invalid because the description is derived from the text, and a general text does not have precedence over a specific one; also, the opposing school (Mutakallimūn) does not consider transference, so generality is merely a form, not a meaningful cause. In Hanafi view, the cause is considered by its meaning, not form. (4) Tarjih by fewer attributes: a description with one attribute is preponderant over one with two attributes because simplicity is preferred. This is presented without explicit validation/critique in this excerpt. The text serves as a critical analysis of these methods.

Key Terms
ترجيح بغلبة الأشباه (tarjīḥ bi-ghalabat al-ashbāh): Preferring one analogy because it shares more attributes with the original case.
ترجيح بالعموم (tarjīḥ bi-l-ʿumūm): Preferring a description because it is more general in application.
ترجيح بقلة الأوصاف (tarjīḥ bi-qillat al-awṣāf): Preferring an attribute with fewer conditions over one with more.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the text reject tarjih by predominance of similarities (ghalabat al-ashbah)?
A) Because it is a form of istihsan.
B) Because each similarity constitutes a separate qiyas, making it essentially tarjih by another qiyas.
C) Because it relies on weak hadith.
D) Because it contradicts the principle of precaution.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, valid tarjih methods are those that do not reduce to another method or confuse form with meaning; hence, tarjih by another qiyas and by fewer attributes are accepted, while tarjih by predominance of similarities and by generality are rejected.

Four Invalid Methods of Tarjih in Qiyas
Classification of four rejected tarjih methods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the four invalid types of tarjih and explain why they are rejected in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
Tarjih in qiyas is based on substantive meanings (ma'ani), not superficial forms like number of attributes or generality.

This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul classifies four methods of tarjih that are invalid in the Hanafi school. They all rely on external forms rather than the underlying meaning of the cause. Understanding these helps refine your ability to evaluate competing analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four invalid types
List the four methods explicitly mentioned: (1) preferring one qiyas over another, (2) by preponderance of similarities, (3) by generality, (4) by fewer attributes.
The author uses 'وهذا باطل' for each.
2
Understand the reason for invalidity
For each type, note the author's argument. For example, type 2 reduces to type 1; type 3 confuses form with meaning; type 4 treats count as criterion.
Focus on the logical flaw.
3
Analyze the third type in depth
The dispute about generality: opponents argue a general attribute is stronger; Hanafis respond that the attribute is a branch of the text, and generality is a form, not a meaning.
Refer to the madhhab dispute map.
4
Connect to the general principle
Conclude that preference in this chapter is only by the meanings previously mentioned, not by forms. The count and generality are forms.
Apply this principle to future cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الرابع : فعلى أربعة أوجه:
-١ - ترجيح القياس بقياس آخر، وما يجري مجراه على ما قلناه.
والثاني: الترجيح بغلبة الأشباه مثل قولهم : إن الأخ يُشبه الولد بوجه، وهو المحرمية، ويُشبه ابن العم بسائر الوجوه، مثل وضع الزكاة من الطرفين، وحل الحليلة وقبول الشهادة ووجوب القصاص من الطرفين، فكان هذا أولى، وهذا باطل؛ لأن كل شبه يصلح قياساً، فيصير كترجيح القياس بقياس آخر.
والثالث : الترجيح بالعموم، مثل قولهم: إن الطعم أحقُّ؛ لأنه يعمُّ القليل والكثير، وهذا باطل؛ لأن الوصف فرعُ النص، والنص العام والخاص سواء عندنا وعندكم الخاص يقضي على العام، فكيف صار العام أحقَّ من الذي هو فرعه؟ ولأن التعدي غير مقصود عندكم، فبطل الترجيح به، وعندنا صار علة بمعناه لا بصورته، والعموم صورة.
والرابع: الترجيح بقلة الأوصاف، فيقال: ذات وصف: أحق من ذات وصفين. وهذا باطل؛ لأن العلة فرع النص، والنص الذي خُص نظمه بضرب من الإيجاز والاختصار والنص الذي أشبع بيانه: سواء، وإنما الترجيح في هذا الباب بالمعاني التي مرَّ ذكرها، فأما بالصور: فلا، والقلة والكثرة صورة، ولم يُعتبر ذلك في الذي جعل نظمه حجةً، ففي هذا الباب أولى.
As for the fourth [type], it is of four aspects:
1. Preferring one analogy over another analogy, and what follows its course, as we have said.
2. Preference by preponderance of similarities, such as their statement: The brother resembles the son in one aspect, which is prohibition of marriage, and he resembles the cousin in all other aspects, such as the payment of zakat from both sides, the permissibility of the wife, the acceptance of testimony, and the obligation of retaliation from both sides; so this (the second) is more appropriate, but this is invalid because every similarity is suitable for qiyas, so it becomes like preferring one qiyas over another.
3. Preference by generality, such as their statement: 'taste' is more entitled because it includes little and much, and this is invalid because the attribute is a branch of the text, and the general and specific texts are equal according to us, and according to you the specific overrules the general, so how can the general be more entitled than that which is its branch? And because extension (ta'diya) is not intended according to you, so preference by it becomes invalid, and according to us the cause is by its meaning not its form, and generality is a form.
4. Preference by fewer attributes, such as saying: a thing with one attribute is more entitled than a thing with two attributes. And this is invalid because the cause is a branch of the text, and the text whose wording is characterized by a measure of brevity and conciseness and the text that is fully elaborated are equal; preference in this matter is only by the meanings that have been mentioned earlier; as for forms, no, and few and many are form, and that which made its wording a proof is not considered, so in this matter [the former] is more appropriate.
Source pages p. 654

Commentary
This passage is from the usul discussion on invalid methods of tarjih (preference) in qiyas. The author presents four rejected types of tarjih that rely on superficial features rather than substantive meanings. 

First type: Preferring one qiyas over another directly, or similar methods, which the author says is as previously stated (likely already rejected). 

Second type: Preference by preponderance of similarities. Example: A brother resembles a son in the aspect of mahramiyya (prohibition of marriage) but resembles a cousin in all other aspects (e.g., zakat, halal spouse, testimony, qisas). Some claim the brother is more like a son in some rulings, but the author deems this invalid because each resemblance can serve as a basis for qiyas, thereby reducing to the first type (preferring one qiyas over another). 

Third type: Preference by generality. Example: 'taste' (al-tu'm) is considered more entitled (as a cause) because it applies to both small and large quantities. The author refutes this: The attribute (wasf) is a branch of the text, and general and specific texts are equal in authority; according to the opposing school (indakum), the specific overrules the general, so how can the general be more entitled than its branch? Moreover, the extension (ta'diya) is not intended by the opponent, so preference by generality fails. For the Hanafis (indana), the cause is considered by its meaning, not its form; generality is a form. 

Fourth type: Preference by fewer attributes. Example: one attribute is better than two because the cause is a branch of the text, and texts—whether concise or detailed—are equal in authority. Tarjih is only by the meanings previously mentioned (e.g., strength of proof, suitability, etc.), not by forms like count of attributes. 

The author concludes that preference in qiyas is based on substantive meanings, not on external forms like number of attributes or generality.

Key Terms
ترجيح (tarjih): The process of preferring one legal analogy (qiyas) or cause over another when both appear plausible.
عموم (umum): Generality of a word or attribute, i.e., applying to many cases. Here it is considered a form, not a meaning, and thus not a valid basis for tarjih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a valid method of tarjih in qiyas?
A) Preference by preponderance of similarities.
B) Preference by generality.
C) Preference by fewer attributes.
D) Preference by meanings (ma'ani).

Key Takeaway
Tarjih in qiyas is only valid when based on substantive meanings (ma'ani), not on formal aspects like generality, number of attributes, or preponderance of similarities.

Preferring 'Illah by Meanings, Not Form
Refuting that textual brevity influences preference of 'illah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects textual length or brevity as a basis for preferring one 'illah over another and apply the principle that preference is by meanings (ma'ani).

Core Idea
The authority of an 'illah derives from its text, not the text's length or conciseness.

When two legal causes ('illal) conflict, a student might think the one from a longer, more detailed text is stronger. This passage refutes that by establishing a foundational rule: 'illah is a branch of the text, and the text's authority does not depend on its form. Preference (tarjih) among 'illal is determined solely by intrinsic meanings, not superficial attributes like brevity or elaboration.

Guided Path
1
Identify the refuted claim
The passage begins by rejecting a view: that the conciseness or exhaustiveness of a text affects the strength of the 'illah derived from it. Recognize this as the target of refutation.
The claim: a concise text yields a weaker 'illah than a detailed one.
2
State the relationship between 'illah and nass
Understand that the 'illah is a branch (far') of the text (nass). The 'illah's validity is tied to the text's authority, not its wording style.
The text's status as evidence (hujjiyya) is independent of its length.
3
Recognize the equality of concise and detailed texts
Both a concise and an elaborate text are equal in terms of being valid sources for deriving 'illah. The passage explicitly states they are 'sawa' (equal).
Conciseness (ijaz) and elaboration (ishba') are merely formal attributes.
4
Identify the correct basis for tarjih
Preference among 'illal is by meanings (ma'ani) such as strength of indication, appropriateness, or other qualitative factors previously discussed in usul. Formal attributes (suwar) like quantity or length are not considered.
The passage says 'إنما الترجيح في هذا الباب بالمعاني' – preference is by meanings.
5
Apply the principle to a hypothetical
Consider two 'illal for the same ruling: one derived from a short hadith, another from a long verse. According to this passage, you cannot prefer one over the other based on text length. Instead, evaluate their conceptual strength.
If one 'illah is more directly connected to the ruling (munasaba), it is preferred.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا باطل؛ لأن العلة فرع النص، والنص الذي خُص نظمه بضرب من الإيجاز والاختصار والنص الذي أشبع بيانه: سواء، وإنما الترجيح في هذا الباب بالمعاني التي مرَّ ذكرها، فأما بالصور: فلا، والقلة والكثرة صورة، ولم يُعتبر ذلك في الذي جعل نظمه حجةً، ففي هذا الباب أولى.
