Rather, preference in this chapter is by the meanings that have been mentioned previously. As for by forms, no. And fewness and manyness are form, and that was not considered in that which made its wording an evidence, so in this chapter it is even more appropriate.

Commentary
This passage states a rule in the methodology of preferring one 'illah over another. The author asserts that it is invalid to argue that a 'illah derived from a concise text is weaker than one from a detailed text. Both are equal because the 'illah is a branch (far') of the text (nass), and the text's authority does not depend on its length. Preference (tarjih) among 'illal is determined by intrinsic meanings (ma'ani) — such as strength of indication, appropriateness, etc. — not by formal attributes like brevity (ijaz) or elaboration. The passage critiques a view that considers quantity (qilla and kathra) as a factor. Since even the original text that serves as proof is not judged by its length, the derived 'illah is even less so.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illah): The legal cause or effective reason that connects a ruling to its textual basis.
النص (al-nass): The primary textual evidence from Quran or Sunnah.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): The process of preferring one evidence (here 'illah) over another.

C) The meanings (ma'ani) that indicate strength or relevance

Key Takeaway
Preference among legal causes ('illal) is based on conceptual meanings, not textual form; a concise text is as authoritative as a detailed one.

The First Method of Rebutting Circular ʿIllal: Al-Qawl bi-Mujab al-ʿIllah
Introduction to four methods, focusing on first method with example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first method of rebutting a circular ʿillah (al-qawl bi-mujab al-ʿillah) by identifying the necessary consequence of the opponent's cause and demonstrating its absurdity or forcing a refinement, using the Hanafi example of wiping the head.

Core Idea
Acknowledging the implication of the cause forces the opponent to either abandon the cause or accept substantive meanings.

In Hanafi usul, not every claimed legal cause (ʿillah) is acceptable. Some are 'circular' (tardiyya) and must be rebutted. The first and most effective rebuttal method is to accept the logical consequence of the opponent's ʿillah and show it leads to an unintended or contradictory result. This lesson walks through the method and a concrete example from wudu.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of rebutting ʿillal
Recognize that the passage deals with the second section of a chapter on rebutting circular ʿillal. There are four methods; the first is 'al-qawl bi-mujab al-ʿillah'.
This method is preferred because it lifts the dispute (ikhtilaf) directly.
2
Define al-qawl bi-mujab al-ʿillah
It means accepting what the proponent of the ʿillah necessarily implies by his ʿillah, without conceding the ʿillah itself. The goal is to show that the ʿillah forces an absurd conclusion or compels the opponent to adopt more substantive (fiqhi) meanings.
It is not agreement with the ʿillah, but agreement with its logical consequence.
3
Analyze the example: wiping the head (masah al-raʾs)
The opponent argues: Wiping the head is a rukn (pillar) in wudu, so performing it three times (tathlith) is sunnah, just like washing the face (which is also a rukn). The Hanafi rebuttal: Both schools agree tathlith is sunnah, but the obligation is only a quarter of the head. Wiping the entire head in one motion is not tathlith but excess, because tathlith requires repetition on the same locus, not covering a larger area.
The analogy to entering three houses clarifies the concept of repetition on the same locus.
4
Observe the Hanafi response to an alternative phrasing
If the opponent rephrases: 'Its repetition should be sunnah', the Hanafi does not concede, because the cause for sunnah is no longer established. The passage ends as the author begins to challenge this alternative.
This shows the method forces the opponent to refine or abandon the ʿillah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب وجوه دفع العلل الطردية وهو القسم الثاني من هذا الباب، وذلك أربعة أوجه: ١ - القول بموجب العلة ؛ لأنه يرفع الخلاف، فهو أحق بالتقديم. ٢- ثم بيان الممانعة. - ثم بيان فساد الوضع. ٤ ـ ثم المناقضة. أما القول بموجب العلة فالتزامُ ما يلزمه المعلل بتعليله، وأنه يلجىء أصحاب الطرد إلى القول بالمعاني الفقهية. وذلك مثل قولهم في مسح الرأس إنه ركن في وضوء، فيسن تثليثه كغسل الوجه. فيقال لهم: عندنا وعندكم بأقل يسن تثليثه ؛ لأن فرضه يتأدى بقدر الربع عندنا، منه، فما تجاوزه إلى استيعابه فتثليث وزيادة إذ ليس مقتضى التثليث اتحاد المحل، لا محالة، ألا يُرى أن مَن دخل ثلاثَ دُورٍ: كان ثلاث دَخَلات بمنزلتها في دار واحدة، وإذا كان كذلك : فقد ضَمَّ إلى الفرض أمثاله، فكان تثليثاً وزيادة. فإن غير العبارة، فقال: وجب أن يسن تكراره: لم نُسلّم ذلك في
Chapter: Aspects of rebutting striking/circular legal causes. And it is the second section of this chapter. And that is four aspects: 1. Acknowledging the implication of the cause, because it lifts disagreement, so it is more deserving of precedence. 2. Then demonstrating prevention (mumana'ah). [3.] Then demonstrating the corruption of the postulation (fasad al-wad'). 4. Then contradiction (munqadah). As for acknowledging the implication of the cause, it is to accept what the proponent of the cause necessitates by his cause, and that it compels the proponents of the striking cause to accept substantive juristic meanings. That is like their statement regarding wiping the head that it is a pillar (rukn) in wudu', so its tathlith (doing three times) is sunnah, like washing the face. It is said to them: In our school and in yours, tathlith is sunnah with less [than the full head]; because its obligatory part is performed with a quarter according to us, from it, so what exceeds it to covering the whole is tathlith and excess, since the requirement of tathlith is not necessarily the unity of the locus. Do you not see that one who enters three houses: that is three entries like the entry into one house? And when it is so, he has added to the obligatory part its likes, so it is tathlith and excess. If he changes the expression and says: 'it is necessary that its repetition be sunnah', we do not concede that in...
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage opens a new section on rebutting 'illal. The author lists four methods. The first method is 'al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illah' (acknowledging the implication of the cause). The scholar accepts the logical consequence of the opponent's 'illah and shows that it leads to an absurdity or forces the opponent to adopt more substantive meanings. The example involves wiping the head (masah al-ra's). Some argue that because wiping is a rukn in wudu, it should be done three times (tathlith) like washing the face. The Hanafi response: both schools agree tathlith is sunnah, but in Hanafi fiqh, the obligatory part is only a quarter of the head. Wiping the entire head in one motion is not tathlith because tathlith requires repetition on the same locus — analogous to entering three houses versus entering one house three times. Thus, the opponent's analogy fails. The passage ends as the author begins to respond to an alternative wording.

Key Terms
العلة الطردية (al-'illah al-tardiyya): A circular or striking legal cause that does not produce a valid ruling.
القول بموجب العلة (al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illah): Acknowledging the implication of the cause to rebut it.
الممانعة (al-mumana'ah): Demonstrating prevention, i.e., the claimed 'illah is prevented from being effective.
تثليث (tathlith): Performing an act three times

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first method of rebutting a circular 'illah according to this passage?
A) Demonstrating prevention (mumana'ah)
B) Acknowledging the implication of the cause (al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illah)
C) Demonstrating corruption of postulation (fasad al-wad')
D) Contradiction (munaqadah)

Key Takeaway
The most effective rebuttal of a circular cause is to accept its necessary consequence and show it leads to an absurd or unintended result, thereby compelling the opponent to refine or abandon the cause.

Refuting a Superficial Cause: Affirming Its Implication
Explaining the first method to refute superficial causes with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of 'al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illa' to refute a superficial cause in a Hanafi usul argument.

Core Idea
Accept a superficial cause's implications to expose its absurdity.

In Hanafi usul, when an opponent uses a superficial cause (عِلَّة طَرْدِيَّة), one of the most effective rebuttals is to accept the cause at face value and then demonstrate that its logical implications lead to an unacceptable conclusion. This lesson introduces the first of four methods, al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illa, using the classic example of wiping the head during wudu. By following the argument step by step, you will see how the Hanafi scholar forces the opponent to confront the consequences of his own analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the superficial cause
Pinpoint the opponent's claim that a certain attribute (e.g., being a pillar) is the cause for a ruling (e.g., triple repetition).
In the example, the superficial cause is 'being a pillar (rukn)' for the ruling 'triple repetition is sunna'.
2
State the implication (موجب) of that cause
Determine what must necessarily follow if the cause is accepted. Here, if being a pillar mandates triple repetition, then performing the pillar over its entire locus would count as triple repetition plus extra.
The Hanafi argues: since the obligation is fulfilled by a quarter, covering the whole head exceeds the minimum and becomes repetition.
3
Show that the implication leads to contradiction or absurdity
Demonstrate that the implication contradicts a known principle or leads to a prohibited outcome. In this case, triple repetition of wiping would amount to washing, which is prohibited in wudu.
Thus, the implication proves the original cause is false or needs refinement.
4
Conclude that the cause is invalid
Since the implication is unacceptable, the cause must be rejected or restricted. The opponent is forced to abandon the superficial analogy and adopt a meaningful legal consideration.
The Hanafi concludes that repetition is not a sunna for wiping; rather, prolongation is sunna, as in other pillars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب
وجوه دفع العلل الطردية
وهو القسم الثاني من هذا الباب، وذلك أربعة أوجه:
١ - القول بموجب العلة ؛ لأنه يرفع الخلاف، فهو أحق بالتقديم.
٢- ثم بيان الممانعة.
٣- ثم بيان فساد الوضع.
٤ ـ ثم المناقضة.

أما القول بموجب العلة فالتزامُ ما يلزمه المعلل بتعليله، وأنه يلجىء أصحاب الطرد إلى القول بالمعاني الفقهية.
وذلك مثل قولهم في مسح الرأس إنه ركن في وضوء، فيسن تثليثه كغسل الوجه.
فيقال لهم: عندنا وعندكم بأقل منه، فما تجاوزه إلى استيعابه فتثليث وزيادة إذ ليس مقتضى التثليث اتحاد المحل، لا محالة، ألا يُرى أن مَن دخل ثلاثَ دُورٍ: كان ثلاث دَخَلات بمنزلتها في دار واحدة، وإذا كان كذلك : فقد ضَمَّ إلى الفرض أمثاله، فكان تثليثاً وزيادة.
فإن غير العبارة، فقال: وجب أن يسن تكراره: لم نُسلّم ذلك في الأصل ؛ لأن التكرار في الأصل غير مسنون، ولكن المسنون تكميله، وهو الأصل في الأركان، وتكميله بإطالته في محله إن أمكن، بمنزلة إطالة القيام والركوع والسجود ولكن الفرض لما استغرق محله : اضطررنا إلى التكرار خَلَفاً عن الأصل، والأصل ها هنا مقدور عليه في مسح الرأس؛ لاتساع محله فبطل الخلف.
وظهر بهذا فقه المسألة، وهو أن لا أثر للركنية في التكرار أصلاً، كما في أركان الصلاة ، ولا أثر لها في التكميل لا محالة، ألا يُرى أن الرأس شاركه مسحُ الخف في الاستيعاب سُنّةً، وهو رخصةٌ، وكذلك المضمضة، فأما المسح : فله أثر في التخفيف لا محالة؛ لأنه لا يؤدي لطهرِ معقول، فلما كان كذلك كانت الإطالة فيه سنةً، لا التكميل بالتكرار. ألا يُرى أن التكميل بالتكرار ربما يُلحقه بالمحظور، وهو الغسل، فكيف يصلح تكميلاً؟
وأما الغسل : فقد شُرع لطهرٍ معقول، فكان التكرار تكميلاً، ولم يكن محظوراً، فقد أدى القول بموجب العلة إلى الممانعة.
وهذا كله بناء على أن فرض مسح الرأس يتأدى ببعض الرأس، لا محالة، وذلك غير مسلّم على مذهبهم، بل الفرض يتأدى بكله، ولكن الشرع رخص في الحط إلى أدنى المقادير وذلك كالقراءة عندكم وإن طالت كانت فرضاً، وقد يتأدى بآية واحدة، وإذا كان كذلك : لم يلزمه شيء من هذه الوجوه.
والجواب عنه : أن هذا خلافُ الكتاب، قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَامْسَحُوا
Chapter: Ways to Refute Superficial Causes. This is the second section of this chapter. There are four ways: 1. Affirming the implication of the cause, because it removes the disagreement, so it is more deserving of precedence. 2. Then demonstrating obstruction (mumana‘a). 3. Then demonstrating invalidity of the basis (fasad al-wad‘). 4. Then contradiction (munaqada). As for affirming the implication of the cause, it is to accept what the specifier (mu‘allil) is committed to by his specification, and it forces the proponents of superficiality (ashab al-tard) to resort to legal meanings (ma‘ani fiqhiyya). An example is their statement regarding wiping the head: it is a pillar (rukn) in wudu, so its triple repetition is sunna, like washing the face. It is said to them: According to us and according to you, the obligation is fulfilled with the minimum portion; so what exceeds that to full coverage is triple repetition and an addition, because the implication of triple repetition is not the unity of the locus (ittihad al-mahall), necessarily. Do you not see that if someone enters three houses, that is three entries, but by its analogy in one house, if it is so, then he has added to the obligatory like amounts, making it triple repetition and an addition. If he changes the expression and says: it becomes obligatory that its repetition be sunna, we do not concede that in principle, because repetition in the principle is not sunna, but the sunna is its completion (takmil), and that is the principle in pillars, and its completion is by prolonging it in its locus if possible, akin to prolonging standing, bowing, and prostration. But when the obligation occupied its entire locus, we are forced to repetition as a substitute for the principle, and the principle here is attainable in wiping the head due to the spaciousness of its locus, so the substitute is invalidated. Thereby the fiqh of the matter becomes clear: that the quality of being a pillar has no effect on repetition at all, as in the pillars of prayer, and it has no effect on completion necessarily. Do you not see that wiping of the head is shared by wiping of the khuff in full coverage as sunna, and it is a concession, and likewise rinsing (madmada). As for wiping, it has an effect in lightening necessarily because it does not lead to a reasonable purification; so when it is so, prolonging it is sunna, not completion by repetition. Do you not see that completion by repetition may bring it to the prohibited, which is washing, so how can it be valid as completion? As for washing, it is legislated for a reasonable purification, so repetition is completion and is not prohibited. Thus affirming the implication of the cause led to obstruction (mumana‘a). All of this is based on the fact that the obligation of wiping the head is fulfilled by part of the head, necessarily, and that is not conceded according to their school. Rather, the obligation is fulfilled by the whole head, but the law granted a concession to lower to the minimum measures, like recitation according to you: if it is long, it is obligatory, and it may be fulfilled by one verse. If it is so, nothing of these ways applies to him. The response is: this contradicts the Book; Allah the Exalted said: 'And wipe...'
Source pages p. 656

Commentary
This passage presents the first of four methods for refuting a superficial cause (علة طردية) in usul al-fiqh: affirming the implication of the cause (القول بموجب العلة). The author explains that this method requires accepting the logical consequences that the one positing the cause must accept, thereby forcing him to engage with substantive legal meanings rather than mere formalism. The example given is the analogy that since wiping the head is a pillar of wudu, it should be repeated three times like washing the face. The Hanafi response is that the obligation is fulfilled by a part of the head; thus, performing it over the entire head constitutes triple repetition plus extra, which is not required. If the opponent then says repetition itself is sunna, the Hanafi points out that repetition is not originally a sunna; rather, the sunna is completion (تكملة). For pillars, completion means prolongation in one place, as in standing, bowing, and prostration. Only when the entire locus is obligatory (as in washing the face) is repetition resorted to as a substitute. But since the head's wiping can be extended over a larger area, the substitute is unnecessary. The passage further clarifies that being a pillar does not inherently mandate repetition; rather, the nature of the act (light vs. heavy purification) determines it. Wiping is a light purification that does not require repetition, whereas washing, which is for substantial purification, does. Thus affirming the implication leads to a contradiction (ممانعة) in the opponent's position. Finally, the passage notes that the opponent might object that they consider wiping the entire head obligatory, and the concession to a quarter is a reduction akin to shortening recitation. The Hanafi response is that this contradicts the Quranic verse (5:6) which implies wiping part of the head.

Key Terms
العلة الطردية (al-'illa al-tardiyya): A cause that is merely formal or superficial, lacking true legal effect and requiring refutation.
القول بموجب العلة (al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illa): Affirming the implication of the cause, i.e., accepting its logical consequences to show inconsistency.
الممانعة (al-mumana'a): Obstruction; a contradiction that arises from the implication of the cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does affirming the implication of a superficial cause lead to in the wiping head example?
A) It proves that repetition is sunna.
B) It shows that repetition would become prohibited because it resembles washing.
C) It demonstrates that the head must be wiped entirely.
D) It forces the opponent to accept that the head wiping is not a pillar.

Key Takeaway
Al-qawl bi-mujab al-'illa is a powerful Hanafi tool to expose the absurdity of a superficial cause by showing its logical implications lead to contradiction.

Making Up Corrupted Supererogatory Acts: Liability by Vow
Hanafi rebuttal of qiyas opposing qada for supererogatory acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on when a supererogatory act requires qada (make-up) and evaluate the qiyas argument used by the opponent.

Core Idea
A supererogatory act is only guaranteed if vowed; mere beginning does not obligate qada.

This passage presents a Hanafi rebuttal to an opponent who argues that invalidating a supererogatory act never necessitates qada, using the analogy of wudu. The Hanafis respond that while qada is not generally required for invalidation, it becomes obligatory if the act was invalidated without the person's choice and, crucially, only if the act was undertaken as a vow. This distinction demonstrates how attributes of an act can change its legal ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed ruling
What is the core legal question? It is whether a supererogatory act of worship, if invalidated, must be made up (qada).
The opponent says no; Hanafis say yes under certain conditions.
2
State the opponent's proof
The opponent uses qiyas: just as wudu (a supererogatory act) does not require qada when invalidated, so too any supererogatory prayer does not require qada.
The illa is 'being a supererogatory act of worship (nafl qurbah)'.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
Hanafis reject the analogy: the far' (prayer) becomes guaranteed upon beginning (shuru'), unlike wudu. Moreover, the guarantee only holds if the act was vowed.
They illustrate with the slave example: a slave's value is determined by humanity, not property status.
4
Extract the usul principle
Attributes can affect rulings: a supererogatory act is not guaranteed by being a qurbah, but by the attribute of being a vow. This is like the slave case where the attribute of humanity determines blood money.
This shows that qiyas can be invalid if the illa is not precisely identified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقلنا لهم : عندنا لا يجب القضاء بالإفساد حتى إنه يجب إذا فسد لا باختياره بأن وجد المتيمم في النفل ماءً، لكنه بالشروع يصير مضموناً عليه وفواتُ المضمون في ضمانه يوجب المثل. فإن قيل : وجب أن لا يلزمه القضاء بالشروع، ولا بالإفساد. قلنا : القربة عندنا بهذا الوصف لا تُضمن وإنما تُضمن بوصف أنه يلتزم بالنذر. وذلك مثل قولهم : العبد،مال، فلا يتقدر بدله بالقتل، كالدابة. وعندنا : لا يتقدر بدله بهذا الوصف بل بوصف الآدمية، وهذا كلام حسن، ألا يُرى أن الموجود قد يكون ببعض صفاته حسناً، وببعض صفاته رديئاً ، فيجوز أن تكون القربة مضمونةً بوصف خاص،
We said to them: According to us, making up (qada') is not obligated due to invalidation, except that it is obligated when it becomes invalid without his choice, like when the one performing tayammum finds water during a supererogatory prayer, but by beginning it becomes guaranteed upon him, and the loss of the guaranteed (act) in his guarantee necessitates its equivalent. If it is said: Then it should not be obligated to make up by beginning nor by invalidation. We say: The act of worship according to us is not guaranteed by this description, but is guaranteed only by the description that he is bound by a vow. And that is like their statement: The slave is property, so his blood money is not determined by killing, like an animal. And according to us: His blood money is not determined by this description, but by the description of humanity. And this is good speech. Do you not see that an existing thing may be good due to some of its attributes and bad due to some of its attributes, so it is permissible that the act of worship is guaranteed by a specific description.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage discusses whether a person who begins a supererogatory (nafl) worship must perform qada (make-up) if it becomes invalid. The opponent argues that no qada is required, citing the analogy of wudu where if one begins wudu (which is also a supererogatory act of worship) and it becomes invalid, qada is not required. The Hanafi response distinguishes: qada is not required for invalidation due to one's own action, but if it becomes invalid without choice (e.g., a person doing tayammum for a nafl prayer finds water), then qada is required because the act became guaranteed upon beginning it. An objection is raised that neither beginning nor invalidation should necessitate qada. The Hanafi reply clarifies that a supererogatory act of worship is not guaranteed simply by being a qurbah (act of drawing near to Allah); rather, it is only guaranteed if it is undertaken as a vow (nadhr). They illustrate with the example of a slave: a slave is property, so if killed, his blood money is not fixed like that of a free person; but according to Hanafis, his blood money is determined by the attribute of humanity (he is a human) not by his property status. Similarly, an act of worship is guaranteed only when it has the attribute of being a vowed act. This shows that attributes can affect legal rulings.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada'): Making up or performing a missed or invalidated act of worship.
الإفساد (al-ifsad): Invalidation of an act of worship, making it void.
الضمان (al-daman): Guarantee or liability; here, the obligation to make up the act once begun.
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow to perform an act of worship, which makes it binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when does a supererogatory act of worship become subject to qada (make-up) if invalidated?
A) Whenever it is begun
B) Only if it was vowed
C) Only if the invalidation was without one's choice
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
A supererogatory act of worship is guaranteed (requiring qada if invalidated without choice) only when it is undertaken as a vow.

Guarantee in Supererogatory Acts: Attribute Analysis
Dispute on whether nafl acts require makeup when invalidated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on guarantee in nafl acts and evaluate the qiyas used to distinguish between supererogation and vow.

Core Idea
A supererogatory act is guaranteed by the attribute of vow, not by being supererogatory.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute about whether invalidation of a supererogatory act of worship requires makeup (qada). The Hanafi school holds that deliberate invalidation does not necessitate makeup, but if the act becomes invalid without choice (e.g., finding water during tayammum) or was vowed, then it must be made up. The reasoning hinges on a subtle attribute analysis, analogous to the dual nature of a slave as both property and human. Understanding this distinction clarifies the binding force of different types of worship.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafis say about makeup of an invalidated nafl act. Distinguish between deliberate invalidation and invalidation without choice.
The text explicitly says: 'no qada for deliberate invalidation; qada required if invalidated without choice.'
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
The opponents (likely Shafi'i) argue that once commenced, a supererogatory act becomes binding and its invalidation should require makeup.
The text rejects this by saying commencement does not create a guarantee for all supererogatory acts.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof: the analogy of the slave
Break down the qiyas: the slave has two attributes – property (which would make compensation fixed by value) and humanity (which makes compensation fixed by diya). Similarly, a supererogatory act has attributes: being a nafl (does not guarantee) and being a vow (guarantees).
The text says: 'the substitute is not determined by this description (property) but by the description of humanity.'
4
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafis respond that the guarantee (daman) attaches only to the attribute of vow (nadhr), not to the attribute of supererogation. Thus, deliberate invalidation of a plain nafl does not trigger makeup.
This is the key innovation: the same act can be guaranteed by one attribute and not by another.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
A Hanafi who deliberately invalidates a nafl prayer does not have to make it up, while a Shafi'i would. However, if the nafl was performed as a vow, both agree makeup is required.
This shows the importance of intention (niyyah) and the nature of commitment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن ذلك : قولهم : باشَرَ نَفْلَ قُرْبةٍ : لا يُمضى في فاسدها، فلا يلزمه القضاء بإفسادها، كما قيل في الوضوء. فقلنا لهم : عندنا لا يجب القضاء بالإفساد حتى إنه يجب إذا فسد لا باختياره بأن وجد المتيمم في النفل ماءً، لكنه بالشروع يصير مضموناً عليه وفواتُ المضمون في ضمانه يوجب المثل. فإن قيل : وجب أن لا يلزمه القضاء بالشروع، ولا بالإفساد. وقلنا : القربة عندنا بهذا الوصف لا تُضمن وإنما تُضمن بوصف أنه يلتزم بالنذر. وذلك مثل قولهم : العبد ،مال، فلا يتقدر بدله بالقتل، كالدابة. وعندنا : لا يتقدر بدله بهذا الوصف بل بوصف الآدمية، وهذا كلام حسن، ألا يُرى أن الموجود قد يكون ببعض صفاته حسناً، وببعض صفاته رديئاً ، فيجوز أن تكون القربة مضمونةً بوصف خاص، غير مضمونة بسائر الأوصاف.
And from that is their saying: He commenced a supererogatory act of worship: it is not continued in its invalid state, so he is not obliged to make it up by invalidating it, as said in wudu. So we said to them: According to us, making it up is not obligatory by invalidation; rather, it becomes obligatory if it becomes invalid without his choice, such as when the one performing dry ablution finds water during a supererogatory prayer. But by commencement it becomes guaranteed upon him, and the loss of what is guaranteed in his guarantee necessitates the equivalent. If it is said: Then he should not be obligated to make it up by commencement, nor by invalidation. We said: The act of worship according to us with this description is not guaranteed, but it is guaranteed with the description that he commits by vow. And that is like their saying: A slave is property, so his substitute is not determined by killing, like an animal. According to us: His substitute is not determined by this description, but by the description of humanity. This is good speech. Do you not see that a thing may be good by some of its attributes and bad by some of its attributes? So it is possible that an act of worship is guaranteed by a specific attribute and not guaranteed by other attributes.
Source pages p. 658

Commentary
This passage addresses the Hanafi position on supererogatory (nafl) acts of worship. The opponents argue that if a supererogatory act becomes invalid, one is not required to make it up, similar to wudu. The Hanafis refine this: making up is not required if one deliberately invalidates it, but it becomes required if the invalidation happens without choice (e.g., a mutayammim finding water during prayer). Upon commencement (shuru'), the act becomes 'guaranteed' (madhmun) upon the performer, meaning its completion is owed. However, this guarantee applies only when the act is undertaken as a vow (nadhr), not as a general supererogatory act. The analogy of a slave is used: a slave is considered property but also a human; the substitute (diya) for killing him is determined by his humanity, not property status. Similarly, an act of worship can be guaranteed by one attribute (vow) and not by another (general supererogation).

Key Terms
نفل (nafl): Supererogatory act of worship, not obligatory but recommended.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee; a legal obligation to compensate or fulfill.
شروع (shuru'): Commencement; entering into an act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, when does a supererogatory act of worship require makeup if invalidated?
A) If invalidated deliberately
B) If invalidated without choice, e.g., finding water during prayer
C) Always
D) Never

Key Takeaway
A supererogatory act is not inherently guaranteed; guarantee only attaches through the attribute of vow or when invalidation occurs without choice.

The Four Types of Mumana'a (Obstruction) in Usul al-Fiqh
Introduction of the four types of objection to a description's validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of mumana'a and apply them to examples from qiyas debates.

Core Idea
Mumana'a challenges a description's validity in qiyas through four distinct objections: the description itself, the ruling, its suitability, or its attribution.

In this passage, Imam al-Bazdawi introduces the concept of mumana'a, a technical term in Hanafi usul al-fiqh for objections raised against the validity of a description (wasf) as a cause (illa) in analogical reasoning. The passage lists four types of objections and provides concrete examples from fiqh debates. Understanding these objections is crucial for evaluating the soundness of qiyas arguments.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the four types
The passage states 'وهي أربعة أوجه' and lists them: 1) obstruction in the description itself, 2) in the ruling itself, 3) in its suitability for the ruling, 4) in the attribution of the ruling to the description. Remember these categories in order.
The Arabic terms: ممانعة في نفس الوصف, في نفس الحكم, في صلاحه للحكم مع وجوده, في نسبة الحكم إلى الوصف.
2
Analyze the first example
The first example given is about a penalty related to intercourse. Opponents argue that since the penalty is for intercourse, it cannot apply to eating. The Hanafi response is that the expiation (kaffara) for breaking the fast is related to eating, not intercourse, so the description 'penalty related to intercourse' is not present. This is an example of the first type: obstruction in the description itself.
The example is from the hadd of zina, but the context is about expiation for fasting.
3
Analyze the second example
The second example is the sale of an apple for an apple by guesswork. Opponents claim it is invalid as a guesswork sale of foodstuff. The Hanafis argue that guesswork can be about substance or description, and here the measure must be considered because foodstuff exchanges by measure are permissible. This example illustrates the first type as well, but also touches on the need to prove that foodiness is the cause.
The passage cuts off, but the intent is to show how to rebut a mumana'a.
4
Relate to qiyas methodology
In qiyas, the proponent asserts a cause (illa) that links the original case (asl) to the new case (far'). The opponent can object by raising one of these four types of mumana'a. Being able to identify which type is being used is key to constructing or refuting a qiyas argument.
This is a foundational concept in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني وهو الممانعة وهي أربعة أوجه: ممانعة في نفس الوصف. والثاني: في نفس الحكم. والثالث: في صلاحه للحكم، مع وجوده. والرابع: في نسبة الحكم إلى الوصف. أما الأول: فمثل قولهم: عقوبة متعلقة بالجماع، فلا تجب بالأكل. كحد الزنى. وهذا غير مسلم عندنا؛ لأن كفارة الفطر متعلقة بالفطر، دون الجماع. ومن ذلك: قولهم في بيع التفاحة بالتفاحة: إنه بيع مطعوم بمطعوم مجازفة فيبطل كبيع الصبرة بالصبرة؛ لأنا نقول: مجازفة ذات أو وصف، فلا بد من القول بالذات، ثم نقول مجازفة في الذات بصورته، أو بمعياره، فلا بد من القول بالمعيار؛ لأن المطعوم بالمطعوم كيلاً بكيل جائز وإن تفاوتا في الذات. فإن قال: لا حاجة لي إلى هذا لم نسلم له المجازفة مطلقةً، فيضطر إلى إثبات أن الطعم علة لتحريم البيع بشرط الجنس مع أن الكيل الذي
The second chapter: It is 'al-mumana'a' (obstruction), and it has four aspects: obstruction in the description itself; the second: in the ruling itself; the third: in its suitability for the ruling, despite its existence; the fourth: in the relation of the ruling to the description. As for the first: e.g., their saying: A penalty related to intercourse, so it is not obligatory for eating, like the hadd of zina. And this is not accepted by us, because the expiation for breaking the fast is related to eating, not intercourse. And from that is their saying in the sale of an apple for an apple: It is a sale of a foodstuff for a foodstuff by guesswork, so it is invalid like the sale of a pile for a pile; because we say: Guesswork regarding the substance or the description, so it is necessary to affirm the substance, then we say: guesswork regarding the substance by its form or by its measure, so it is necessary to affirm the measure; because foodstuff for foodstuff by measure for measure is permissible even if they differ in substance. If he says: I do not need this, we do not accept the guesswork as absolute, so he is forced to prove that foodiness (tu'm) is the cause for prohibition of sale with the condition of genus, despite that the measure...
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage introduces the second chapter on 'الممانعة' (obstruction/opposition) in the context of qiyas (analogical reasoning). It defines four types of objection to the validity of a description as a cause for a ruling. The first type is objection to the description itself, i.e., denying that the description exists in the original case. The second is objection to the ruling itself, i.e., denying that the ruling applies to the original case. The third is objection to the suitability of the description for the ruling, i.e., arguing that the description is not a proper cause even if it exists. The fourth is objection to the attribution of the ruling to the description, i.e., denying that the ruling is linked to that description. The text then provides examples. For the first type, opponents give the example of a penalty related to intercourse (like hadd for zina) not applying to eating, but Hanafis respond that the expiation for breaking the fast is related to eating, not intercourse, so the description of 'penalty related to intercourse' does not apply. Another example is the sale of an apple for an apple: opponents claim it is invalid as a guesswork sale of foodstuff, but Hanafis argue that guesswork can be about substance or description; here the measure must be considered because foodstuff exchanges by measure are permissible. The passage continues with a detailed rebuttal aiming to force the opponent to prove that foodiness (tu'm) is the cause for prohibition, which requires the condition of genus.

Key Terms
الممانعة (al-mumana'a): An objection in usul al-fiqh that challenges the validity of a description as a cause for a ruling, with four specific types.
وصف (wasf): The attribute or characteristic proposed as the cause (illa) for a ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Mumana'a is a structured objection in qiyas with four distinct categories, each targeting a different aspect of the causal link.

The Four Types of Muman'a with Examples for the First Two
Defines four types of muman'a with examples for first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of muman'a and apply the first two types to refute an opponent's analogy.

Core Idea
Muman'a invalidates an analogy by exposing contradiction in the description, ruling, or their relationship.

In Hanafi usul, when an opponent uses qiyas to extend a ruling, you can block it by showing muman'a – a legal contradiction. This lesson defines the four types and walks through examples of the first two, teaching you how to deploy this critical tool in argument.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition and four categories of muman'a
Understand that muman'a is a method to reject an opponent's analogy by showing a contradiction. The four categories are: (1) in the description itself, (2) in the ruling itself, (3) in the suitability of the description for the ruling, (4) in the attribution of the ruling to the description.
Memorize the four types as they form the basis for structured rebuttal.
2
Parse the first type: muman'a fi nafs al-wasf
Identify how the opponent's description (wasf) does not actually apply to the branch case. Work through the examples given: kaffara for eating vs. intercourse, apple sale, young non-virgin consent.
Look for the pattern: opponent claims a common 'illa, but the Hanafi shows the 'illa is absent in the branch.
3
Work through the examples for type one
For each example, trace the opponent's analogy and the Hanafi response that exposes the contradiction. Note how the Hanafi forces the opponent to refine or abandon the claimed 'illa.
The examples illustrate the precise analytical move.
4
Parse the second type: muman'a fi nafs al-hukm
Understand that this type arises when the ruling sought to be applied to the branch would conflict with a settled ruling in the original or elsewhere. The example: triple wiping on the head analogy.
Here the opponent's extension leads to a ruling that contradicts the established nature of wiping.
5
Distinguish between the first two types in practice
Summarize the difference: type one attacks the presence of the description, type two attacks the consistency of the ruling. Knowing which type to use depends on the opponent's argument.
This distinction sharpens your rebuttal strategy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني وهو المُمَانعة وهي أربعة أوجه: ممانعة في نفس الوصف. والثاني: في نفس الحكم. والثالث: في صلاحه للحكم، مع وجوده. والرابع: في نسبة الحكم إلى الوصف. ١ - أما الأول: فمثل قولهم: عقوبة متعلقة بالجماع، فلا تجب بالأكل. كحد الزنى. وهذا غير مُسلَّم عندنا؛ لأن كفارة الفطر متعلقة بالفطر، دون الجماع. ومن ذلك: قولهم في بيع التفاحة بالتفاحة: إنه بيع مطعوم بمطعوم مجازفة فيبطل كبيع الصبرة بالصبرة؛ لأنا نقول: مجازفة ذات أو وصف، فلا بدَّ من القول بالذات، ثم نقول مجازفة في الذات بصورته، أو بمعياره، فلا بد من القول بالمعيار؛ لأن المطعوم بالمطعوم كيلاً بكيل جائز وإن تفاوتا في الذات. فإن قال: لا حاجة لي إلى هذا لم نُسلّم له المجازفة مطلقةً، فيضطر إلى إثبات أن الطعم علة لتحريم البيع بشرط الجنس مع أن الكيل الذي يظهر به الجواز لا يُعْدِم إلا الفضل على المعيار. ومن ذلك: قولهم في الثيب الصغيرة: إنها ثيب تُرجى مشورتها، فلا تنكح إلا برأيها، كالثيب البالغة؛ لأنا نقول برأي حاضر، أم برأي مستحدث؟ فأما الحاضر: فلم يوجد في الفرع، وأما المستحدث: فلا يوجد في الأصل. فإن قال: لا حاجة لي إلى هذا. قلنا له: عندنا لا تُنكح إلا برأيها؛ لأن رأي الولي رأيها. وهو أن فإن قال: بأيهما كان انتقض بالمجنونة؛ لأن لها رأياً مستحدثاً أيضاً؛ لأن الجنون يحتمل الزوال، لا محالة، فيظهر به فقه المسألة، الولاية ثابتة فلا يمنعُها إلا رأي قائم فأما المعدوم قبل الوجود فلا يحتمل أن يكون شرطاً مانعاً، أو دليلاً قاطعاً. وهذا الذي ذكرنا أمثلة ما يدخل في الفرع، وفيه قسم آخر، وهو ما يدخل في الأصل. ومثل قولهم في مسح الرأس: إنه طهارة مسح، فيسن تثليثه كالاستنجاء. فنقول: إن الاستنجاء ليس بطهارة مسح، بل طهارة عن النجاسة الحقيقية، فيضطر إلى الرجوع إلى فقه المسألة، وهو بيان ما يتعلق به التكرار وهو الغسل، وما يتعلق به التخفيف، وهو المسح، وهما في طرفي نقيض التكرار في أحدهما: يُحقق غرضه، وفي الثاني: يُفسده، ويُلحقه بالمحظور، وهذا أكثر من أن يُحصى. ٢ـ وأما الممانعة في الحكم فمثل قولهم في مسح الرأس: إنه ركن في الوضوء، فيسن تثليثه كغَسل الوجه.
The second chapter: Muman'a. It has four aspects: (1) muman'a in the description itself; (2) in the ruling itself; (3) in its suitability for the ruling despite its existence; (4) in the attribution of the ruling to the description. 1- As for the first: example is their saying: A punishment attached to intercourse, so it is not obligatory by eating, like the hadd of zina. This is not accepted by us because the kaffara of breaking fast is attached to breaking the fast, not intercourse. And from that: their saying in the sale of an apple for an apple: it is a sale of food for food by guesswork, so it is invalid like the sale of a stack for a stack. For we say: guesswork in the substance or description, so one must judge by the substance; then we say guesswork in the substance by its form or its measure, so one must judge by the measure because food for food by measure is permissible even if they differ in substance. If he says: I have no need for this, we do not accept from him absolute guesswork, so he is forced to prove that taste is the cause for prohibition of sale on condition of genus, while the measure by which permissibility appears does not remove except the excess over the standard. And from that: their saying about the young non-virgin: she is a non-virgin whose opinion is hoped for, so she is not married except by her opinion, like the adult non-virgin. For we say: by present opinion or by newly formed opinion? As for the present: it does not exist in the branch; as for the newly formed: it does not exist in the original. If he says: I have no need for this. We say to him: According to us, she is not married except by her opinion because the guardian's opinion is her opinion. And it is that if he says: by whichever it was, it is refuted by the insane woman because she also has a newly formed opinion because insanity may pass, necessarily. So by it the fiqh of the issue becomes clear: guardianship is established, so nothing prevents it except a present opinion; as for the non-existent before existence, it cannot be a preventing condition or a decisive proof. And this is what we mentioned as examples of what enters the branch, and there is another section: what enters the original. And like their saying about wiping the head: it is a purification by wiping, so triple wiping is sunna like istinja. We say: istinja is not a purification by wiping, but purification from actual impurity, so he is forced to return to the fiqh of the issue, which is the explanation of what repetition attaches to – which is washing – and what alleviation attaches to – which is wiping – and they are at opposite extremes: repetition in one achieves its purpose, and in the other spoils it and brings it near to prohibition. This is more than can be enumerated. 2- As for muman'a in the ruling: example is their saying about wiping the head: it is a pillar in wudu, so triple wiping is sunna like washing the face.
Source pages p. 660

Commentary
This passage is a definition and exemplification of the concept of muman'a in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. Muman'a is a method of rejecting an opponent's analogy (qiyas) by showing a contradiction or inconsistency in the description (wasf), ruling (hukm), or their relationship. The author lists four types: (1) muman'a in the description itself – where the description cited as the cause (illa) does not actually apply to the branch case; (2) muman'a in the ruling – where the ruling sought to be applied would conflict with a settled ruling; (3) muman'a in the suitability of the description for the ruling despite its existence – where the description exists but is not suitable for producing the ruling; (4) muman'a in the attribution of the ruling to the description – where the ruling is not properly attributable to the description. The author then provides examples. For the first type: opponents argue that since a punishment is attached to intercourse, the kaffara for fasting (which is a punishment) should not be due for eating (since it is not intercourse). The Hanafi response: kaffara is attached to breaking the fast, not to intercourse, so the analogy fails. Another example: the sale of an apple for an apple is claimed to be invalid because it is a guesswork sale of food for food, like a stack for a stack. The Hanafis counter that guesswork can be in substance or description, and here it is in substance with equal measure, which is permissible. The opponent is forced to prove that taste (ta'm) is the cause for prohibition, but measure allows permissibility. A third example: a young non-virgin woman should require her own consent like an adult non-virgin. The Hanafis distinguish between present opinion and newly formed opinion; the young girl has no formed opinion, so the guardian's opinion suffices. The opponent's objection about the insane woman is refuted because her opinion is not reliably present. Then an example of muman'a in the ruling: opponents argue that wiping the head (a purification by wiping) should have triple wiping like istinja (which is also a purification by wiping). The Hanafis clarify that istinja is not a purification by wiping but removal of actual impurity; repetition is suitable for washing but not for wiping. Then, for the second type (muman'a in the ruling), the example: opponents claim that since wiping the head is a pillar of wudu, it should be done three times like washing the face. The Hanafis would argue that the ruling (triple repetition) contradicts the nature of wiping, which is light and repetitive rubbing would be contrary to the intended ease.

Key Terms
الممانعة (al-mumāna‘ah): In usul, a method to refute an analogy by showing a contradiction in the description (wasf), ruling (hukm), or their relationship.
الوصف (al-waṣf): The attribute or cause (illa) cited in the analogy.
الحكم (al-ḥukm): The legal ruling (e.g., obligation, prohibition) that is sought to be applied to the branch.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of muman'a is involved when the opponent argues that wiping the head is like istinja because both are purifications by wiping, and the Hanafi response is that istinja is not a wiping purification?
A) Muman'a in the ruling itself
B) Muman'a in the description itself
C) Muman'a in the suitability of the description
D) Muman'a in the attribution of the ruling

Key Takeaway
Muman'a is a precise analytical tool to reject qiyas by exposing contradictions in the description, ruling, or their relationship; mastering it sharpens your legal reasoning.

Objection in Qiyas: Marriage of a Young Non-Virgin
Example of mumana'a in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi objection of mumana'a by identifying whether the effective attribute (illah) is actually present in both the original and the branch in a given qiyas.

Core Idea
The effective attribute must be actual, not potential, for analogy to be valid.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi objection (mumana'a) against an analogy between a young non-virgin and an adult non-virgin. The opponent seeks to require the girl's consent, but the Hanafi scholar questions whether the attribute of 'having an opinion' is truly present. Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to evaluate the validity of qiyas arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's analogy
Find the original case (asl: adult non-virgin) and the branch (far': young non-virgin). The claimed ruling: both require consent.
The analogy hinges on the attribute of being a thayyib.
2
Determine the claimed illah
The effective cause is that 'her opinion is sought' because she is a non-virgin. But specify: what type of opinion?
The Hanafi scholar distinguishes between present (ra'y hadir) and newly acquired (ra'y mustahdath).
3
Apply the objection (mumana'a)
Check if the claimed illah exists in the branch. For 'present opinion', the young girl lacks it. For 'newly acquired opinion', the adult does not have it.
The illah must actually exist in both asl and far'.
4
Consider the counter-objection
The opponent raises the case of an insane woman who may later regain sanity. The Hanafi response: only a present opinion matters; a future opinion cannot be a condition.
Illustrates that potential attributes do not serve as valid illah.
5
Conclude on the qiyas
The analogy fails because the claimed attribute is absent in either the asl or the far'. The young non-virgin is not required to consent personally; the guardian's opinion suffices.
This shows the Hanafi method of scrutinizing the actuality of the illah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم في الثيب الصغيرة : إنها ثيب تُرجى مشورتها، فلا تنكح إلا برأيها، كالثيب البالغة ؛ لأنا نقول برأي حاضر ، أم برأي مستحدث؟ فأما الحاضر : فلم يوجد في الفرع ، وأما المستحدث : فلا يوجد في الأصل. فإن قال : لا حاجة لي إلى هذا. قلنا له: عندنا لا تُنكح إلا برأيها ؛ لأن رأي الولي رأيها. وهو أن فإن قال : بأيهما كان انتقض بالمجنونة ؛ لأن لها رأياً مستحدثاً أيضاً ؛ لأن الجنون يحتمل الزوال، لا محالة، فيظهر به فقه المسألة، الولاية ،ثابتة فلا يمنعُها إلا رأي ،قائم فأما المعدوم قبل الوجود فلا يحتمل أن يكون شرطاً مانعاً، أو دليلاً قاطعاً.
Their statement about a young non-virgin: that she is a non-virgin whose opinion is sought, so she is not married except with her opinion, like an adult non-virgin. For we say: Is it a present opinion or a newly acquired opinion? As for the present, it does not exist in the branch. As for the newly acquired, it does not exist in the original. If he says: I do not need this. We say to him: According to us, she is not married except with her opinion, because the guardian's opinion is her opinion. That is, if he says: By which of the two is it refuted by the insane woman? Because she also has a newly acquired opinion; because insanity is likely to disappear, inevitably, and thus the fiqh of the matter becomes clear. Guardianship is established, so nothing prevents her except a present opinion. As for that which is non-existent before existence, it cannot be a preventing condition or a conclusive proof.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This passage is an example of an objection (mumana'a) in qiyas. The opponent analogizes a young non-virgin girl to an adult non-virgin, arguing that both must consent to marriage. The Hanafi author objects by questioning the type of opinion required: if a present opinion (ra'y hadir) is needed, it does not exist in the branch (the young girl has no present opinion due to immaturity); if a newly acquired opinion (ra'y mustahdath) is expected, it does not exist in the original (the adult's opinion is already present). The opponent then objects that the same issue applies to an insane woman, who also may later acquire a sound opinion. The Hanafi response clarifies that the guardian's opinion substitutes for hers, and only a present opinion matters; a future opinion cannot be a condition or proof. This illustrates the Hanafi method of questioning the presence of the effective attribute (illah) in either the original or the branch.

Key Terms
ثيب (thayyib): A non-virgin woman, one who has been previously married.
رأي حاضر (ra'y hadir): A present, existing opinion or judgment currently held.
رأي مستحدث (ra'y mustahdath): A newly acquired opinion that may develop in the future.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the young non-virgin, what type of opinion does the Hanafi author argue is absent in the branch?
A) A newly acquired opinion
B) A present opinion
C) A future opinion
D) The guardian's opinion

Key Takeaway
A valid qiyas requires that the effective attribute (illah) actually exist in both the original and the branch, not merely potentially.

Objection in Qiyas: Mumana'a on the Original – Wiping the Head vs Istinja
Example of mumana'a (objection on the original) in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate a qiyas by identifying whether the original (asl) shares the same legal category (jins) as the branch (far'), applying the Hanafi method of mumana'a.

Core Idea
A qiyas fails if the original's legal basis differs from the branch's – istinja removes impurity, mas-h purifies by wiping.

In Hanafi usul, a common objection (mumana'a) is to deny that the original case (asl) belongs to the same genus (jins) as the branch (far'). This passage illustrates this with the analogy between wiping the head (mas-h) and istinja. By examining the legal nature of each, we see why the analogy collapses and why tathlith (repetition) does not apply to wiping acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Qiyas
The opponent argues that wiping the head is a purification by wiping (tahara mas-h), so it should be done thrice (tathlith) like istinja, which also involves wiping.
Note the claimed commonality: both involve wiping.
2
Recognize the Hanafi Objection
The Hanafi author objects that istinja is not 'tahara mas-h' but rather a removal of actual impurity (najasa haqiqiyya). Thus the original (istinja) is not of the same legal category as the branch (mas-h).
This is mumana'a: denying that the asl shares the same jins.
3
Apply the Legal Principle
The Hanafi principle: repetition (tathlith) is related to washing (ghusl), which fulfills its purpose by repetition, whereas wiping (mas-h) is meant to be light; repetition would spoil it.
Thus the ruling differs based on the nature of the act.
4
Derive the Implication
Since the analogy is invalid, the ruling for wiping the head stands as no tathlith. The opponent's qiyas fails due to mumana'a on the asl.
This illustrates a key method of refuting qiyas in Hanafi usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثل قولهم في مسح الرأس : إنه طهارة مسح، فيسن تثليثه كالاستنجاء. فنقول: إن الاستنجاء ليس بطهارة مسح، بل طهارة عن النجاسة الحقيقية، فيضطر إلى الرجوع إلى فقه المسألة، وهو بيان ما يتعلق به التكرار وهو الغسل، وما يتعلق به التخفيف، وهو المسح، وهما في طرفي نقيض التكرار في أحدهما : يُحقق غرضه، وفي الثاني: يُفسده، ويُلحقه بالمحظور، وهذا أكثر من أن يُحصى.
Like their statement about wiping the head: it is a purification by wiping, so it is recommended to do it thrice like istinja. We say: Istinja is not a purification by wiping, but rather a purification from actual impurity (najasa haqiqiyya). So one must refer back to the fiqh of the matter, which is a clarification of what repetition pertains to—that is washing—and what pertains to leniency—that is wiping. These are at opposite extremes. Repetition in one (washing) achieves its purpose; in the other (wiping) it spoils and makes it prohibited. This is more than can be enumerated.
Source pages p. 661

Commentary
This is an example of objection (mumana'a) in the original (asl). The opponent analogizes wiping the head (mas-h) in wudu to istinja (cleaning after toilet), claiming both are 'purification by wiping' and thus should be done thrice (tathlith) as sunna. The Hanafi author objects that istinja is not a 'purification by wiping' (tahara mas-h) but rather a removal of real impurity (najasa haqiqiyya). The legal basis is fundamentally different: washing (ghusl) benefits from repetition, while wiping (mas-h) is meant to be light. Repetition in wiping defeats its purpose and may even render it invalid. Thus the analogy is invalid because the original (istinja) does not share the same legal category as the branch (mas-h).

Key Terms
طهارة مسح (tahara mas-h): A type of ritual purification that is done by wiping, such as wiping the head or over khuffs.
تثليث (tathlith): Doing something three times, often recommended in acts of worship.
ممانعة (mumana'a): An objection in qiyas that denies that the original case (asl) shares the same effective cause (illa) or legal category (jins) as the branch (far').

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi author reject the analogy between wiping the head and istinja?
A) Wiping the head is not a purification
B) Istinja is not a 'tahara mas-h' but removal of najasa
C) Repetition is never recommended
D) Istinja is done with water

Key Takeaway
A qiyas must verify that the asl and far share the same legal category; otherwise, mumana'a renders the analogy invalid.

Objection in Ruling: Wiping the Head vs Washing Face
Example of mumana'a in hukm: wiping head vs washing face

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the Hanafi method of mumana'a in hukm to evaluate qiyas arguments.

Core Idea
When the attribute is absent, the analogy breaks — repetition in washing is for coverage, not a general sunna.

In this passage, the author demonstrates how the Hanafi school refutes an analogy by objecting to the very ruling (hukm) being analogized. The opponent claims that since wiping the head is a pillar like washing the face, it should also be done thrice. The Hanafi response uncovers that the sunna in washing is not repetition but completion, and repetition is only necessary because the fard covers the entire face. This precise analysis shows that not all rulings transfer between similar acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's qiyas
Determine the asl (washing the face), far' (wiping the head), and the claimed illa (both are pillars).
2
Recognize the Hanafi objection (mumana'a in hukm)
The Hanafi argument denies that the ruling in the asl is tathlith; it is actually takmil (completion).
The objection targets the ruling itself, not the presence of the illa.
3
Analyze the necessity of repetition
Repetition in washing arises because the fard covers the entire face, requiring extra washes for certainty. This need is absent in wiping.
The effective cause (necessity for coverage) is not present in the far'.
4
Conclude the failure of analogy
Since the attribute that justifies repetition in the asl is missing in the far', the qiyas is invalid.
The Hanafi ruling: tathlith is not sunna for wiping the head.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الممانعة في الحكم فمثل قولهم في مسح الرأس: إنه ركن في الوضوء، فيسن تثليثه كغَسل الوجه. فنقول : إن غسل الوجه لا يُسن تثليثه، بل يسن تكميله بعد تمام فرضه، وقد حصل التكميل ها هنا ولكن التكرار صير إليه في الغسل لضرورة أن الفرض استغرق محله ، وهذا المعنى معدوم في هذا. ولأن المشروع في الأصل إطالته لا تكراره كما في غيره من الأركان، لكن التكرار وجب بالضرورة؛ لما قلنا.
As for objection in the ruling: such as their statement about wiping the head: it is a pillar in wudu, so it is recommended to do it thrice like washing the face. We say: Washing the face is not recommended to be done thrice, rather it is recommended to complete it after its fard is done. Completion is achieved here, but repetition in washing is resorted to out of necessity because the fard occupies its entire locus (the face), and this meaning is absent here (in wiping). And because what is prescribed in the original (washing) is elongation (itala) not repetition, as in other pillars, but repetition became necessary due to what we said.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This is another example of objection (mumana'a) this time in the ruling (hukm) itself. The opponent analogizes wiping the head to washing the face, claiming both are pillars (rukn) of wudu and thus both should be done thrice (tathlith). The Hanafi author objects: washing the face is not recommended to be done thrice per se; the sunna is to complete and extend the washing after the fard is fulfilled. Repetition in washing is only needed because the fard covers the whole face, so one does three washes to ensure coverage. For wiping the head, the fard does not require full coverage (just a quarter of the head by Hanafi view), so repetition is not recommended and would be a waste. Thus the ruling in the original (washing face) is not 'tathlith' but 'takmil' (completion), and the analogy fails.

Key Terms
تكميل (takmil): Completing the act by extending beyond the obligatory minimum.
إطالة (itala): Elongation of the act, such as washing beyond the fard area.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi author, why is tathlith recommended for washing the face but not for wiping the head?
A) Washing the face is wajib, wiping is sunna
B) The fard of washing covers the whole face, necessitating repetition for certainty
C) Wiping the head is done only once in the Quran
D) The pillar status differs between the two

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi objection in hukm (mumana'a) reveals that apparent similarities in rulings may conceal different underlying rationales, invalidating the analogy.

Objection in Qiyas: Fasting Ramadan and Specific Intention
Objection in Qiyas: Requirement of Specific Intention for Ramadan Fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that an absolute intention (itlaq) for a specific obligatory fast suffices as specification (ta'yin) when the context is clear, and explain how the Hanafi rebuttal (mumana'a) neutralises the opponent's qiyas.

Core Idea
In Hanafi usul, a general intention suffices for Ramadan because the context itself specifies, blocking the opponent's analogy.

The requirement of specific intention (ta'yin al-niyya) for obligatory acts is a key topic in Hanafi usul. This passage demonstrates a classic use of mumana'a (obstruction) in qiyas: the opponent argues by analogy that general intention should suffice, but the Hanafi response dissects the timing of specification, revealing a flaw in the analogy. Understanding this debate sharpens your ability to evaluate legal analogies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's qiyas
The opponent argues that just as other obligatory acts do not require explicit specification, so the Ramadan fast should not. This is the basis of the qiyas.
The qiyas is implied; the passage does not state it explicitly but the Hanafi response targets it.
2
Locate the Hanafi rebuttal (mumana'a)
The Hanafi response asks whether specification is required before or after starting the fast. This is a classic mumana'a technique to show that the claimed attribute is absent in either the original or branch.
The question forces the opponent to specify timing, exposing the weakness.
3
Analyse the timing argument
If specification after start, it is not found in the original (other obligations). If before start, it is not found in the branch (Ramadan). Thus the analogy fails because the claimed attribute is not consistent.
This demonstrates that the opponent's qiyas is invalid due to lack of a common effective cause.
4
Understand the Hanafi conclusion
The Hanafis state that ta'yin is required, but the absolute intention (itlaq) to fast on a Ramadan day is itself ta'yin because the context designates the day as Ramadan.
The principle 'itlaquhu ta'yin' is a key Hanafi position in this matter.
5
Apply the principle
Recognise that in the Hanafi school, contextual factors like the distinctness of Ramadan can transform a general intention into a specific one.
This principle is limited to cases where the context uniquely identifies the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم في صوم رمضان إنه صوم فرض، فلا يصح إلا بتعيين النية. يقال له : بعد التعيين، أو قبله؟ فإن قال بعده : لم نجده في الأصل، فصحت الممانعة. فإن قال : قبلَه : لم نجده في الفرع ، فصحت الممانعة أيضاً. فإن قال : لا حاجة لي إلى هذا. قلنا له: عندنا لا يصح إلا بالتعيين، غير أن إطلاقه تعيين.
Their statement about fasting Ramadan: it is an obligatory fast, so it is not valid except with a specific intention (ta'yin al-niyya). It is said to him: After the specification, or before it? If he says after it: we do not find it in the original, so the objection is valid. If he says before it: we do not find it in the branch, so the objection is also valid. If he says: I do not need this. We say to him: According to us, it is not valid without specification, except that its mere utterance (itlaq) is tantamount to specification.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage presents a debate about whether a specific intention (ta'yin) is required for fasting Ramadan. The opponent apparently claims that a general intention without specifying Ramadan suffices, and he uses an analogy (perhaps to other obligatory fasts). The Hanafi author retorts by asking whether the specification is required after the fast has begun or before. If after, then in the original (asl, perhaps another obligatory fast) we do not find that specification is required after starting; if before, then in the branch (Ramadan) we do not find that the intention must be specified before each day. The author then states that in the Hanafi madhhab, specification is necessary, but the mere act of intending to fast Ramadan (without explicit 'I intend to fast Ramadan') is considered as specification because the context designates it. This illustrates a clever objection (mumana'a) by showing that the claimed attribute (necessity of ta'yin at a particular time) is absent in either the original or branch.

Key Terms
تعيين النية (ta'yin al-niyya): Specifying in the intention which act is being performed, e.g., which fast.
إطلاق (itlaq): Absolute or general utterance without explicit specification.
الممانعة (al-mumana'a): In usul: an objection that obstructs the opponent's qiyas by showing that the claimed attribute is missing in either the original or branch.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because ta'yin is never required
B) Because the mere utterance of intention for Ramadan acts as specification
C) Because the fast is not obligatory
D) Because the opponent agreed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the requirement of ta'yin can be satisfied by contextual specification (itlaquhu ta'yin), as demonstrated by the Ramadan fast example, thereby blocking the opponent's qiyas.

The Objection of Mumana'a in Qiyas: Selling an Apple for an Apple
Hanafi objection (mumana'a) to a qiyas analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the objection of mumana'a to a qiyas by identifying a mismatch in the attribute (temporary vs absolute) of the effective cause (illa) between the original and branch.

Core Idea
For qiyas to be valid, the effective cause must match in nature – temporary cannot justify absolute.

In this lesson, we examine a classic Hanafi objection (mumana'a) in qiyas: the opponent argues that selling an apple for an apple without equal measure is prohibited by analogy to selling a heap of grain for a heap of grain. The Hanafi scholar counters by probing the nature of the prohibition. This technique sharpens your ability to scrutinize qiyas by demanding exact correspondence of the illa's attribute, a core skill in Hanafi usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original (asl) and branch (far')
The original is selling a heap of grain for a heap of grain by estimation (mujazafa). The branch is selling an apple for an apple by estimation. Both involve exchanging the same foodstuff without precise measurement.
The original's ruling is prohibition due to riba al-fadl.
2
Determine the illa and its attribute
The illa is 'exchange of the same foodstuff by estimation.' The opponent assumes it causes prohibition. The Hanafi questions: is the prohibition in the original temporary (muaqqata) or absolute (mutlaqa)?
Temporary means it can be removed by rectification (mukhallas); absolute means no remedy.
3
Test both possibilities
If the opponent says temporary: then the branch must also have a temporary prohibition. But apples cannot be equalized (no mukhallas), so the prohibition in the branch would be absolute – mismatch. If the opponent says absolute: then the original must also have absolute prohibition. But in Hanafi fiqh, the original (heap sale) has a temporary prohibition because one can add or adjust – mismatch.
Either way, the illa's attribute does not match.
4
Conclude the mumana'a
Because the nature of the prohibition differs, the qiyas fails. The objection (mumana'a) is valid: the effective cause is not present in the branch as it is in the original.
This shows the need for precise correspondence in the illa's attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولهم في بيع التفاحة بالتفاحة : إنه بيع مطعوم بجنسه مجازفة، فيحرم كالصبرة بالصبرة. فيقال له: يحرم حرمةً مؤقتةً، أو مطلقة؟ فإن قال : مؤقتةً : لم نجدها في الفرع ؛ لعدم المخلص. وإن قال : مطلقةً: لم نجدها في الأصل ؛ لأن الحرمة عندنا في الأصل متناهية مؤقتة، فصحت الممانعة.
Their statement about selling an apple for an apple: it is a sale of foodstuff of its own kind by estimation (mujazafa), so it is prohibited like a heap for a heap. It is said to him: Is it prohibited with a temporary prohibition or an absolute one? If he says temporary: we do not find it in the branch (the apple sale) because there is no way out (mukhallas). If he says absolute: we do not find it in the original (the heap sale) because according to us, the prohibition in the original is finite and temporary, so the objection is valid.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage discusses an analogy comparing selling one apple for another (unequal in weight) to selling a heap of grain for a heap of grain (both examples of riba al-fadl). The opponent claims both are prohibited. The Hanafi author objects by asking about the nature of the prohibition: temporary or absolute. If temporary (like until equality is established), the branch (apple sale) has no way to achieve equality (mukhallas) since individual apples are not divisible, so the prohibition would be effectively permanent. If absolute, the original (heap sale) in Hanafi fiqh has a temporary prohibition (since one can add or adjust to achieve equality), not absolute. Thus the analogy fails on either count. This shows a refined usul principle: the effective attribute (temporary vs. absolute prohibition) must match exactly in both original and branch.

Key Terms
مجازفة (mujazafa): A sale without exact measurement or weighing, based on estimation.
مخلص (mukhallas): A way out or means to rectify the transaction to make it permissible.
ممانعة (mumana'a): An objection in qiyas that the illa claimed is not present in the far' (branch) as it is in the asl (original).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi author argue that the analogy between apple sale and heap sale fails?
A) Apples are not foodstuff
B) The prohibition in the original is temporary, but in the branch it would be absolute, so the illa's attribute does not match
C) Both are prohibited absolutely
D) The heap sale is not a valid analogy

Key Takeaway
For qiyas to be valid, the effective cause must match not only in essence but also in its essential attribute (e.g., temporary vs absolute prohibition).

Refuting an Analogy: The Case of Washing the Face Thrice
Hanafi refutation of an analogy regarding face washing repetition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to APPLY the Hanafi method of mumāna'a (objection) to a qiyas argument by identifying the missing ʿilla in the branch.

Core Idea
Repetition in face washing was a necessity, not a Sunnah; analogy fails when the cause is absent.

This lesson examines a Hanafi objection to an analogy that claims since the Prophet ﷺ washed his face thrice in wudu, other acts should also be repeated thrice. The key lies in distinguishing between takmīl (completion) and takrār (repetition). The Hanafi school argues that the cause (ʿilla) for repetition — the need to ensure full coverage of the face — is absent in other acts, thus invalidating the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the aṣl and farʿ
Determine the original case (face washing thrice) and the branch case (the act being analogized). The passage does not name the branch explicitly, but it is implied to be another act of wudu.
The aṣl is the ruling that the face is washed thrice; the farʿ is any other act (e.g., wiping the head) for which repetition is claimed.
2
Locate the claimed ʿilla
What is the opponent's reason for analogizing? They likely assume that the Prophet's action of washing thrice sets a general Sunnah of repetition.
The Hanafi view rejects this ʿilla as too broad.
3
Analyze the Hanafi objection (mumāna'a)
The passage states that repetition was necessitated by the need for completion (takmīl) — the entire face must be washed, and a single wash may not cover it all. This necessity (ʿilla) is unique to the face.
The phrase 'This meaning is absent' indicates the ʿilla is missing in the branch.
4
Conclude the ruling for the branch
Since the ʿilla is absent, the analogy fails. The branch act does not require repetition; it follows the general principle of single washing unless specified.
The thamarah al-khilaf: the branch is not repeated thrice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول : إن غسل الوجه لا يُسن تثليثه، بل يسن تكميله بعد تمام فرضه، وقد حصل التكميل ها هنا ولكن التكرار صير إليه في الغسل لضرورة أن الفرض استغرق محله ، وهذا المعنى معدوم في هذا. ولأن المشروع في الأصل إطالته لا تكراره كما تكراره، كما في غيره من الأركان، لكن التكرار وجب بالضرورة؛ لما قلنا.
We say: Washing the face is not prescribed to be done three times; rather, its completion is prescribed after the fulfillment of its obligatory part. Completion has occurred here, but repetition was necessitated in washing by necessity because the obligatory act occupied its place. This meaning is absent in this [matter]. And because what is legislated in the original is prolongation, not repetition, as in other pillars, but repetition became necessary; for what we said.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi objection (mumāna'a) to a qiyas (analogy). The opponent likely argued that just as washing the face in wudu is repeated three times, other acts should also be repeated. The Hanafi refutes by distinguishing: repetition in face washing was due to necessity (the entire face must be washed, but single washing suffices only if complete; repetition ensures coverage). In the current case (branch), this necessity is absent, so the analogy fails. The core principle is that the sharī'a intends completion, not repetition, except where unavoidable.

Key Terms
تثليث (tathlīth): Performing an act three times.
تكميل (takmīl): Completing the act by ensuring all required parts are covered.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why is the face not washed thrice as a Sunnah in wudu?
A) Because the face is not included in the obligatory parts.
B) Because repetition was only necessitated to ensure complete coverage of the face, not as a separate Sunnah.
C) Because the Prophet ﷺ never washed his face thrice.
D) Because washing thrice would invalidate the wudu.

Key Takeaway
Repetition in face washing was a means to complete the obligatory wash, not an independent Sunnah; analogies to other acts fail when the necessity is absent.
