Mumāna'a: Refuting the Analogy on Intention Specification
Hanafi objection to analogy requiring specific intention in Ramadan fasts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the Hanafi mumāna'a objection to invalidate an analogy requiring explicit intention specification in Ramadan fasts.

Core Idea
The Hanafi refutes the analogy by showing specification cannot be placed in either original or branch.

In this passage, al-Bazdawi presents a Hanafi objection (mumāna'a) to an analogy requiring a specific intention (ta'yīn al-niyya) for every obligatory fast. The opponent argues that since Ramadan fast is obligatory, it requires explicit specification like other obligatory fasts. The Hanafi response dismantles this analogy by questioning the timing of specification, revealing a logical flaw in the opponent's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Analogy
The opponent analogizes Ramadan fasts to other obligatory fasts, claiming that since it is obligatory, it requires a specified intention (ta'yīn al-niyya).
This is a simple qiyas: asl = other obligatory fasts; far' = Ramadan fast; hukm = requiring specification.
2
Apply the Hanafi Mumāna'a: Question the Timing
The Hanafi asks: does the specification occur after the act or before? This creates two logical possibilities to test the analogy.
Mumāna'a means 'objection' — rejecting the validity of the analogy by showing a contradiction.
3
Analyze Each Possibility
If specification is after the act, it is not found in the original (original intentions precede the act). If before, it is not found in the branch (the month itself already specifies). Either way, the analogy fails.
In the original (asl), intentions come before the act, so specification after is impossible. In the branch (far'), the month is determined, so specification before is redundant.
4
Understand the Opponent's Escape and Hanafi Clarification
The opponent might claim no need for this argument. The Hanafi then clarifies that in their madhhab, specification is indeed required, but an absolute intention (iṭlāq) serves as implicit specification for Ramadan.
The Hanafi position is not that no intention is needed, but that the absolute intention is sufficient due to the month's determination.
5
Derive the Usul Principle
This is a standard Hanafi use of mumāna'a to refute qiyas by exposing an inconsistency in the timing of the attribute (specification).
This technique is common in Hanafi usul when the opposing analogy is based on a shared attribute that cannot be consistently applied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثل قولهم في صوم رمضان إنه صوم فرض، فلا يصح إلا بتعيين النية. يقال له : بعد التعيين، أو قبله؟ فإن قال بعده : لم نجده في الأصل، فصحت الممانعة. فإن قال : قبلَه : لم نجده في الفرع ، فصحت الممانعة أيضاً. فإن قال : لا حاجة لي إلى هذا. قلنا له: عندنا لا يصح إلا بالتعيين، غير أن إطلاقه تعيين.
And like their statement regarding fasting in Ramadan: it is an obligatory fast, so it is not valid without specifying the intention. It is said to him: after specification or before? If he says: after it, we say: we did not find it in the original, so the objection is valid. If he says: before it, we say: we did not find it in the branch, so the objection is also valid. If he says: I have no need for this. We say to him: according to us, it is not valid without specification, but the absolute [intention] is a specification.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This is another example of Hanafi mumāna'a (objection) to a qiyas. The opponent analogized Ramadan fasts to other obligatory fasts, claiming that specification of intention (ta'yīn al-niyya) is necessary. The Hanafi refutes by analyzing the temporal relation: you cannot find specification after the act in the original (because intentions precede acts), nor before the act in the branch (because if specified before, it would be found in the original). The opponent may claim no need for this, but the Hanafi clarifies that their own madhhab holds that specification is required, but an absolute intention suffices as implicit specification for Ramadan because it is already designated.

Key Terms
تعيين النية (ta'yīn al-niyya): Specifying the exact type of fast in the intention.
إطلاق (iṭlāq): Absolute, unspecified intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why is the opponent's analogy about specification of intention invalid?
A) Because specification is not required at all.
B) Because the timing of specification cannot be consistently maintained between the original and branch.
C) Because fasting in Ramadan is not obligatory.
D) Because the opponent did not provide a proof.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mumāna'a exposes a logical flaw in the analogy requiring specification, establishing that an absolute intention suffices for Ramadan fasts.

8 min
Refuting the Analogy of Apple-for-Apple Sale
Hanafi refutation of analogy prohibiting fruit exchange due to riba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in an analogy (qiyas) by examining whether the attribute (prohibition) is temporary or absolute, and apply the Hanafi method of mumana'a (objection).

Core Idea
Analogy fails when the attribute in the original is temporary but the branch would require an absolute prohibition.

This passage from Al-Bazdawi's Usul presents a classic Hanafi objection to a qiyas in sales law. The opponent analogizes selling a single apple for another (unequal exchange) to selling a heap of wheat for a heap, claiming both are prohibited due to riba because they are foodstuffs sold by estimation (mujāzafa). The Hanafi response is a powerful tool: by asking whether the prohibition is temporary or absolute, the analyst shows the analogy is inconsistent. Understanding this method is essential for evaluating analogical reasoning in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the opponent's analogy
Identify the original case (asl), branch case (far'), and the attribute (illa) claimed to be common. Here, the original is selling a heap of wheat for a heap (prohibited due to riba), the branch is selling one apple for another, and the claimed illa is 'sale of a foodstuff with its kind by estimation'.
The opponent assumes the prohibition is absolute and uniform.
2
Classify the prohibition in the original
Determine whether the prohibition in the original (heap for heap) is temporary (ḥurma muwaqqata) or absolute (ḥurma muṭlaqa). In the Hanafi view, it is temporary because equalization is possible (e.g., by making the heaps equal).
The Hanafi understanding is that riba prohibition in such sales is temporary until equality is achieved.
3
Apply the same classification to the branch
If the prohibition is temporary, ask: can it be removed in the branch? In selling one apple for another, there is no way to equalize (since the apple is indivisible). Therefore a temporary prohibition does not apply. If the prohibition is absolute, then it would apply to the branch, but the original does not have an absolute prohibition.
This creates a logical inconsistency: neither classification fits both cases.
4
Conclude the objection (mumāna'a)
The analogy fails because the attribute (type of prohibition) is not shared. The Hanafi concludes with mumāna'a: the opponent cannot consistently claim the same ruling for both cases.
This is a valid objection in qiyas theory known as man' al-illa (denial of the effective cause).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثل قولهم في بيع التفاحة بالتفاحة : إنه بيع مطعوم بجنسه مجازفة، فيحرم كالصبرة بالصبرة. فيقال له: يحرم حرمةً مؤقتةً، أو مطلقة؟ فإن قال : مؤقتةً : لم نجدها في الفرع ؛ لعدم المخلص. وإن قال : مطلقةً: لم نجدها في الأصل ؛ لأن الحرمة عندنا في الأصل متناهية مؤقتة، فصحت الممانعة.
And like their statement regarding selling an apple for an apple: it is a sale of a foodstuff with its kind by estimation, so it is prohibited like [selling] a heap for a heap. It is said to him: is the prohibition temporary or absolute? If he says: temporary, we do not find it in the branch because there is no way out. If he says: absolute, we do not find it in the original because the prohibition in the original is finite, temporary, so the objection is valid.
Source pages p. 662

Commentary
This passage illustrates a Hanafi objection to an analogy in sales law. The opponent analogizes selling an apple for an apple (unequal exchange) to selling a heap of wheat for a heap of wheat, both prohibited due to riba (usury). The Hanafi questions the nature of the prohibition: temporary (until equalization) or absolute? In the original (sale of foodstuffs by estimation where equalization is possible), the prohibition is temporary because one can equalize. In the branch (selling a single apple for another), there is no way to equalize precisely, so if the opponent says temporary, it's not applicable; if absolute, the original does not have absolute prohibition. Thus the analogy fails.

Key Terms
مجازفة (mujāzafa): Sale by estimation without exact measurement.
حرمة مؤقتة (ḥurma muwaqqata): A temporary prohibition that can be lifted by equalization.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi objection succeed against the analogy of selling an apple for an apple?
A) Because apples are not foodstuffs.
B) Because the prohibition in the original is temporary while in the branch it would be absolute, and neither matches.
C) Because selling fruit by estimation is always permissible.
D) Because the opponent did not specify the type of prohibition.

Key Takeaway
An analogy in fiqh fails if the attribute (here, the type of prohibition) is not consistently found in both original and branch cases.

Conditions of the Attribute in Qiyas: Effect and Attribution
Rules on attribute suitability in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two conditions (athar and nisba) for a valid attribute in Hanafi qiyas and apply them to evaluate whether a proposed attribute is suitable.

Core Idea
For an attribute to ground qiyas, it must have a detectable effect and be causally linked to the ruling.

In Hanafi usul, not every descriptive quality can serve as the effective cause (illa) for qiyas. This passage from al-Bazdawi introduces two essential conditions: the attribute must produce a tangible legal effect (athar), and the ruling must be properly attributable to it (nisba). These conditions ensure that analogical reasoning is grounded in observable reality, not mere speculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The passage explicitly presents condition 3 (suitability/effect) and condition 4 (attribution). Note the Arabic terms: صلاح الوصف and نسبة الحكم.
2
Understand the proof for the effect condition
The author argues that any attribute without visible effect cannot be a proof. He uses the analogy of an unbeliever's testimony to show that deficient evidence is rejected.
3
Understand the proof for the attribution condition
Mere existence of the attribute is insufficient; one must demonstrate that the ruling is causally connected to it. The examples (brother not enslaved, women's testimony not accepted for marriage) illustrate this.
4
Recognize the objection and response
An opponent may deny the need for effect. The author rejects this as an untenable claim, comparing it to an unacceptable witness set.
5
Apply the rule
Test a proposed attribute against these two conditions to see if it is suitable for qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قلنا به وإن قال إعتاقه لم نجده في الأصل، ولم نقل به في الفرع، ويظهر به فقه المسألة. يصح إلا بمعناه، ٣- وأما صلاح الوصف فما سبق ذكره في أنه لا وهو الأثر، فكل ما لم يظهر أثره : منعناه من أن يكون دليلاً. فإن قال: عندي الأثر ليس بشرط : لم يُقبل منه الاحتجاج بما ليس بحجة على الخصم ، كمثل كافر أقام بينةً كفاراً على مسلم : لم تُقبل؛ لما قلنا. ٤- وأما نسبة الحكم إلى الوصف فلأن نفس الوجود لا يكفي بالإجماع. وذلك مثل قولهم في الأخ : إنه لا يعتق على أخيه؛ لعدم البعضية؛ لأن حكم الأصل لم يثبت ؛ لعدم البعضية. وكذلك لا يثبت النكاح بشهادة النساء مع الرجال؛ لأنه ليس بمال، كالحد ؛ لأن الحد عندنا لا يثبت بها لا لأن ذلك ليس بمال. وكذلك كل نفي وعدم جعل وصفاً لزمه هذا الاعتراض؛ لأن العدم لا يصلح وصفاً موجباً، ونفس الوجود لا يصلح حجة؛ لأنهم يسلمون شرط الصلاح، فلا بد من إقامة الدلالة على نسبة الحكم إليه.
And we say with it, and if he says 'his manumission' we did not find it in the original, and we do not say with it in the branch, and by it the fiqh of the issue becomes clear. It is valid except with its meaning. 3 - As for the suitability of the attribute, what was previously mentioned is that it is not, and it is the effect, so everything whose effect does not appear prevents us from it being a proof. If he says: 'I do not require the effect as a condition,' his argument is not accepted because he uses what is not a proof against the opponent, just as a disbeliever who brings disbelievers as witnesses against a Muslim: it is not accepted, due to what we said. 4 - As for attributing the ruling to the attribute, mere existence is not sufficient by consensus. That is like their statement regarding the brother: he does not become a slave to his brother due to the lack of partiality; because the ruling of the original was not established due to the lack of partiality. Similarly, marriage is not established by the testimony of women along with men because it is not property, like the hadd penalty; because the hadd penalty according to us is not established by it, not because that is not property. And similarly, every negation and non-existence made as an attribute is subject to this objection; because non-existence is not suitable as a positive attribute, and mere existence is not suitable as a proof; because they accept the condition of suitability, so it is necessary to establish the evidence for attributing the ruling to it.
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi's Usul al-Fiqh discusses two important conditions for the attribute (al-wasf) to be valid for qiyas: (1) that the attribute must have a discernible effect (athar), and (2) that the ruling must be properly attributed to the attribute (nisbat al-hukm). The author presents these as points 3 and 4 of an enumeration. For the first condition, he asserts that any attribute without an observable effect cannot be accepted as evidence. He then addresses an objection: if someone claims that effect is not a condition, their argument is rejected like an unbeliever's testimony against a Muslim. For the second condition, he explains that mere existence of the attribute is insufficient; one must demonstrate the connection between the attribute and the ruling. Examples: the brother is not manumitted due to lack of partial ownership; women's testimony alone does not establish marriage because it is not property, analogized to hadd penalties. The general rule is that negation and non-existence cannot serve as positive attributes, and mere existence cannot be proof without establishing a proper link to the ruling.

Key Terms
صلاح الوصف (ṣalāḥ al-waṣf): The suitability of the attribute to serve as a valid basis for analogy, which requires that it have a discernible effect.
الأثر (al-athar): The effect or consequence of the attribute, which must be observable for the attribute to be considered suitable for qiyas.
نسبة الحكم إلى الوصف (nisbat al-ḥukm ilā al-waṣf): The proper attribution of the ruling to the attribute, requiring a demonstrable connection beyond mere coexistence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, why is the mere existence of an attribute insufficient for establishing a ruling via qiyas?
A) Because existence alone does not indicate causation.
B) Because the attribute must have a recognizable effect and be properly attributed to the ruling.
C) Because only attributes mentioned in the Quran are valid.
D) Because existence is not a legal attribute.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi qiyas, an attribute must have a discernible legal effect (athar) and the ruling must be causally linked to it (nisba) for the analogy to be valid.

Fasad al-Wad': The Fundamental Flaw in Qiyas
Defines the third type of defect in qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish fasad al-wad' from munāqaḍa and identify the legal principle that fasad al-wad' nullifies a qiyas entirely.

Core Idea
Fasad al-wad' destroys the rule; contradiction can be avoided in another context.

In Hanafi usul, a valid qiyas requires a sound legal premise. Al-Bazdawi now introduces a critical defect: fasad al-wad', where the attribute or analogy itself is fundamentally misplaced, rendering the entire qiyas invalid. This goes beyond mere contradiction, which may be salvageable. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the soundness of analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of fasad al-wad'
Read the first part of the passage: 'النوع الثالث: وهو فساد الوضع، وهذا ينقض القاعدة أصلاً، وهو فوق المناقضة'. Grasp that fasad al-wad' completely nullifies the rule (base), while contradiction only creates a temporary inconsistency.
The term 'القاعدة' here refers to the legal rule being derived.
2
Contrast with munāqaḍa
Note the phrase 'لأنها حَجَل مجلس يحتمل الاحتراز عنه في مجلس آخر' – contradiction can be avoided in another context. Fasad al-wad' is permanent and fatal.
The word 'حَجَل' is obscure; it likely means 'circle' or 'difficulty'.
3
Identify the legal consequence
Fasad al-wad' invalidates the entire analogy, making it unusable for deriving rulings. This is a higher-level defect than mu'araḍa (objection).
In Hanafi usul, the defects of 'illa include fasad al-wad' as the most severe.
4
Apply the definition to a simple case
Consider an analogy where the attribute cannot logically support the ruling. For example, using 'taste' to prohibit riba by analogy with marriage is fasad al-wad' because taste sustains life, not prohibits.
We will analyze specific examples in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث: وهو فساد الوضع، وهذا ينقض القاعدة أصلاً، وهو فوق المناقضة ؛ لأنها حَجَل مجلس يحتمل الاحتراز عنه في مجلس آخر. وأما فساد الوضع فيُفسد القاعدة أصلاً، مثاله: تعليلهم لإيجاب الفرقة بإسلام أحد الزوجين، ولإبقاء النكاح مع ارتداد أحدهما: أنه فاسد في الوضع؛ لأن الإسلام لا يصلح قاطعاً للحقوق والردة لا تصلح عفواً. ومثله قولهم في الصَّرورة إذا حج بنية النفل: إنه جائز عن الفرض؛ لأنه يتأدى بإطلاق النية، فكذلك بنية النفل وهذا فاسد في الوضع؛ لأن العلماء إنما اختلفوا في حمل المطلق على المقيد واعتباره به وهذا حَمْلَ المقيد على المطلق، واعتباره به وهو فاسد في وضع الشرع. ومثله : التعليل بالطعم لتحريم الربا اعتباراً بالنكاح، وهو فاسد في الوضع ؛ لأن الطَّعم يقع به القِوامُ، فلا يصلح للتحريم، والحرية عبارة عن الخلوص، فيصلح للتحريم إلا بعارض. ومثله قولهم في الجنون : إنه لما نافى تكليف الأداء: نافى تكليف القضاء، وهو فاسد في الوضع؛ لأن الوجوب في كل الشرائع بطريق الجبر، والأداء بطريق الاختيار، كما قيل في النائم والمغمى عليه، والقضاء الذي هو بدل يعتمد انعقاد السبب للأداء على الاحتمال، فصار هذا التعليل مخالفاً للأصول. وكذلك قولهم : ما يمنع القضاء إذا استغرق شهر رمضان: يمنع بقدر ما يوجد : هذا فاسد أيضاً في الوضع؛ لأن الفصل بين اليُسر والحرج في حقوق صاحب الشرع مستمر في أصول الشرع، كالحيض يُسقط الصلاةَ دون الصوم، والسفر أثر في الظهر دون الفجر، وكالحيض إذا تخلل في كفارة القتل : لا يوجب الاستقبال بخلاف كفارة اليمين عندنا وبخلاف ما إذا نذرت أن تصوم عشرة أيام متتابعة ؛ لما ذكرنا، فكذلك ها هنا الاستغراق حرج، وليس في القليل حرجٌ مثله.
The third type: invalidity of the legal premise [fasad al-wad']. This nullifies the rule fundamentally, and it is beyond contradiction; because contradiction is a circle of sitting that can be avoided in another sitting. But invalidity of the legal premise nullifies the rule fundamentally. Example: their reasoning for imposing separation upon the Islam of one spouse and for retaining marriage upon the apostasy of one of them: it is invalid in the legal premise because Islam is not suitable as a cutter of rights, and apostasy is not suitable as a pardon. Similarly, their statement regarding the one who has not performed Hajj [al-sarrūr] if he performs Hajj with the intention of supererogation: that it is valid for the obligatory because it is performed by absolute intention, so likewise with intention of supererogation: this is invalid in the legal premise because the scholars only differed in carrying the absolute to the qualified and considering it by it, and this is carrying the qualified to the absolute and considering it by it, and it is invalid in the legal premise of the Law. Similarly, the reasoning by taste for the prohibition of riba by analogy with marriage: it is invalid in the legal premise because taste sustains life, so it is not suitable for prohibition; and freedom is an expression of liberation, so it is suitable for prohibition except with an impediment. Similarly, their statement regarding insanity: because it contradicts the obligation of performance, it contradicts the obligation of qada': this is invalid in the legal premise because obligation in all laws is by compulsion, and performance is by choice, as was said regarding the sleeping and unconscious person; and qada' which is a substitute depends on the occurrence of the cause for performance based on probability; so this reasoning becomes contrary to the principles. And similarly, their statement: 'What prevents qada' if it occupies the entire month of Ramadan prevents to the extent that it exists': this is also invalid in the legal premise because the distinction between ease and hardship in the rights of the Lawgiver is continuous in the principles of the Law, like menstruation causing abandonment of prayer but not fasting, and travel affecting zuhr but not fajr, and like when menstruation intervenes in the expiation for killing: it does not require restarting contrary to the expiation of an oath according to us, and contrary to when she vows to fast ten consecutive days; due to what we mentioned, so likewise here: complete occupation is hardship, and there is no hardship like it in small amount.
Source pages p. 664

Commentary
This passage from al-Bazdawi defines the third type of defect in qiyas: fasad al-wad' (invalidity of the legal premise). Unlike contradiction (munāqaḍa) which can be avoided in another context, fasad al-wad' completely nullifies the entire analogy because the attribute or comparison is fundamentally flawed. The author provides several examples: (1) Using Islam as a reason to end marriage and apostasy to retain marriage — Islam should not cut rights, nor apostasy forgive; (2) Claiming that a pilgrim who has not performed Hajj (ṣarrūr) can perform Hajj with the intention of supererogation and have it count as obligatory — this reverses the relationship between absolute and qualified; (3) Using taste to prohibit riba by analogy with marriage — taste sustains life, so it is unsuitable for prohibition, while freedom (the attribute in marriage) is about liberation; (4) Insanity repelling the duty of performance cannot automatically repel the duty of qada', because obligation is by compulsion and performance by choice; (5) The argument that what prevents qada' when the entire month of Ramadan is occupied prevents proportionately — this is invalid because hardship is not comparable; menstruation and travel examples show that the Lawgiver distinguishes between ease and hardship. The common thread is that the attribute or comparison is placed in a context where it cannot legally or logically support the ruling.

Key Terms
فساد الوضع (fasād al-waḍ‘): Invalidity of the legal premise; the attribute or comparison cannot legally or logically support the ruling.
المناقضة (al-munāqaḍa): Contradiction that can be avoided in different contexts, as opposed to fasad al-wad' which nullifies the rule.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Bazdawi, how does fasad al-wad' differ from contradiction (munāqaḍa)?
A) Contradiction is always fatal; fasad al-wad' can be fixed.
B) Fasad al-wad' nullifies the rule fundamentally; contradiction can be avoided in another context.
C) They are synonymous.
D) Fasad al-wad' is weaker than contradiction.

Key Takeaway
Fasad al-wad' is a fatal defect in qiyas that destroys the legal rule, unlike contradiction which is context-dependent.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Examples of Fasad al-Wad': Analyzing Invalid Analogies
Provides five illustrative examples of fasad al-wad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze each example of fasad al-wad' from the source, identifying why the legal premise is invalid.

Core Idea
Each example shows an attribute placed where it cannot legally function.

Al-Bazdawi provides five concrete examples of fasad al-wad'. Each illustrates a different way the legal premise is misplaced: using an attribute that cannot support the ruling, reversing logical relations, or ignoring fundamental legal principles. By dissecting these cases, we learn to spot similar flaws in analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
First example: Islam and apostasy in marriage
Read the first example: تعليلهم لإيجاب الفرقة بإسلام أحد الزوجين... The reasoning makes Islam a cutter of rights and apostasy a pardon. Both are unsuitable; hence fasad al-wad'.
This example shows that the attribute (Islam) cannot legally sever rights; the premise is inverted.
2
Second example: Hajj with supererogatory intention
Read the example of al-ṣarrūr. The analogy claims that performing Hajj with supererogatory intention fulfills the obligatory because a general intention does. This reverses the relationship between absolute and qualified.
The correct qiyas is from absolute to qualified, not vice versa.
3
Third example: Taste in riba analogy
The reasoning uses 'taste' to prohibit riba by analogy with marriage. But taste sustains life, so it is unsuitable for prohibition; while freedom (attribute in marriage) is about liberation, suitable for prohibition.
The attribute (taste) is fundamentally misplaced.
4
Fourth example: Insanity and qada'
The argument that insanity repels performance thus also repels qada'. Al-Bazdawi says obligation is by compulsion and performance by choice; qada' is a substitute with different rules. The analogy is invalid.
This shows ignoring the nature of obligation and performance.
5
Fifth example: Proportionate prevention of qada'
The claim that if complete occupation of Ramadan prevents qada', then partial occupation prevents proportionately. Al-Bazdawi rejects this because the Lawgiver distinguishes between ease and hardship; complete occupation is hardship, but small amounts are not comparable.
Examples from menstruation and travel support the principle of hardship distinction.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Invalid Qiyas: Inverting Absolute and Restricted
Critiquing an analogy that inverts mutlaq/muqayyad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an analogy inverts the relationship between mutlaq and muqayyad, making it fasid fi al-wad'.

Core Idea
Carrying a restricted ruling to an absolute case inverts legal structure and invalidates the analogy.

This passage critiques an argument that a nafl intention for Hajj suffices for the obligatory Hajj, drawing on the idea that since the intention is absolute, it covers both. The author shows this is invalid because it is the reverse of the accepted principle: scholars only discussed carrying the absolute to the restricted, not the other way around. The first example about Islam and apostasy similarly illustrates a structural misplacement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposed analogy
The claim: Hajj with nafl intention is valid for fard because intention is absolute; similarly, nafl intention should suffice. This analogizes from the case where absolute intention covers both to the case where restricted intention is used.
2
Recognize the structural inversion
The accepted operation is haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad (carrying the absolute to the restricted). Here, the claimant is doing the opposite: carrying the restricted (nafl intention) to the absolute (covering fard), which is invalid in the law's structure.
3
Link to the first example
The author also mentions that Islam does not cut off rights and apostasy does not pardon—this similarly violates the structured categories set by the Lawgiver.
This is a brief illustration of structural misplacement.
4
Conclude the invalidity
Because the analogy violates the established wad' (legal structure), it is fasid fi al-wad' and cannot be accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الوضع؛ لأن الإسلام لا يصلح قاطعاً للحقوق والردة لا تصلح عفواً. ومثله قولهم في الصَّرورة إذا حج بنية النفل: إنه جائز عن الفرض؛ لأنه يتأدى بإطلاق النية، فكذلك بنية النفل وهذا فاسد في الوضع؛ لأن العلماء إنما اختلفوا في حمل المطلق على المقيد واعتباره به وهذا حمل المقيد على المطلق واعتباره به وهو فاسد في وضع الشرع. ومثله : التعليل بالطعم لتحريم الربا اعتباراً بالنكاح، وهو فاسد في الوضع؛ لأن الطَّعم يقع به القِوامُ، فلا يصلح للتحريم، والحرية عبارة عن الخلوص، فيصلح للتحريم إلا بعارض.
In the legal structure: because Islam is not suitable as a cut-off for rights, and apostasy is not suitable as a pardon. Likewise their statement about the one who has never performed Hajj (sarrurah) if he performs Hajj with the intention of supererogatory (nafl): that it is valid for the obligatory (fard) because it is performed with absolute intention, so likewise with nafl intention. And this is invalid in the legal structure because scholars only disagreed about carrying the absolute (mutlaq) upon the restricted (muqayyad) and treating it accordingly. And this is carrying the restricted upon the absolute and treating it accordingly, which is invalid in the law's structure. Another example: the causal reasoning by edibility (ta'm) for the prohibition of riba by analogy with marriage (nikah). This is invalid in the legal structure because edibility provides subsistence (qiwam), so it is not suitable for prohibition. Freedom (hurriyah) expresses pureness (khulus), so it is suitable for prohibition except with an impediment.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage discusses invalid analogies (qiyas fasid) in the context of legal structure (wad'). The first example critiques the idea that Islam cuts off rights and apostasy cancels them; this is flawed because Islam does not act as a complete barrier and apostasy does not automatically pardon. The second example concerns a pilgrim performing Hajj with a nafl intention; some claim it suffices for fard, but this is invalid because it inverts the normal rule: scholars only debate carrying the absolute to the restricted, not the reverse. The third example compares riba prohibition based on edibility to the prohibition in marriage; but edibility relates to physical sustenance, which is ill-suited for a prohibition, whereas freedom (like in manumission) is a quality of purity that can be a cause for prohibition barring an impediment. The passage underscores that sound analogy must respect the foundational categories of the law.

Key Terms
الوضع (al-wad'): The foundational legal structure or framework set by the Lawgiver.
حمل المطلق على المقيد (haml al-mutlaq 'ala al-muqayyad): Interpreting an absolute expression in light of a restricted one, typically to unify rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the analogy regarding Hajj with nafl intention considered fasid fi al-wad'?
A) Because the intention is not valid.
B) Because it violates the principle of carrying absolute to restricted.
C) Because an obligatory act cannot be performed with nafl intention.
D) Because scholars disagree about Hajj.

Key Takeaway
An analogy that inverts the direction of textual interpretation (carrying restricted to absolute) is fasid fi al-wad'.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Invalid Qiyas: Unsuitable 'Illa (Edibility for Riba)
Critiquing an analogy with an unsuitable 'illa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a proposed 'illa is suitable (munasib) for the ruling, and identify when an analogy fails because the 'illa is not co-extensive with the hikmah.

Core Idea
Edibility is unsuitable as an effective cause for prohibition because it relates to subsistence, not prohibition.

The third example in the passage critiques an analogy that tries to justify the prohibition of riba based on edibility (ta'm) by comparing it to marriage. The author points out that edibility provides qiwam (sustenance) and thus cannot logically be a cause for prohibition. In contrast, freedom (hurriyah) is a quality of purity suitable for prohibition except when impeded. This lesson explores how an 'illa must be structurally appropriate for the ruling it supports.

Guided Path
1
Extract the proposed analogy
The claim: Prohibition of riba is caused by edibility (ta'm), by analogy with marriage (nikah). The idea is that if something is edible, it can be subject to riba prohibition, similar to how certain traits in marriage cause prohibition.
2
Identify the 'illa being used
The 'illa is ta'm (edibility). The claimant asserts that edibility is the effective cause for the prohibition of excess in exchange.
3
Evaluate the suitability of the 'illa
The author argues that ta'm provides qiwam (physical subsistence), so it is not suitable as a cause for prohibition. A suitable 'illa for prohibition should relate to a quality that prevents harm or preserves purity, like freedom (hurriyah) which implies khulus (purity).
The contrast with freedom shows the required attribute.
4
Conclude the invalidity
Because the 'illa of edibility is not suitable (munasib) for the ruling of prohibition, the analogy is fasid fi al-wad'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الوضع؛ لأن الإسلام لا يصلح قاطعاً للحقوق والردة لا تصلح عفواً. ومثله قولهم في الصَّرورة إذا حج بنية النفل: إنه جائز عن الفرض؛ لأنه يتأدى بإطلاق النية، فكذلك بنية النفل وهذا فاسد في الوضع؛ لأن العلماء إنما اختلفوا في حمل المطلق على المقيد واعتباره به وهذا حمل المقيد على المطلق واعتباره به وهو فاسد في وضع الشرع. ومثله : التعليل بالطعم لتحريم الربا اعتباراً بالنكاح، وهو فاسد في الوضع؛ لأن الطَّعم يقع به القِوامُ، فلا يصلح للتحريم، والحرية عبارة عن الخلوص، فيصلح للتحريم إلا بعارض.
In the legal structure: because Islam is not suitable as a cut-off for rights, and apostasy is not suitable as a pardon. Likewise their statement about the one who has never performed Hajj (sarrurah) if he performs Hajj with the intention of supererogatory (nafl): that it is valid for the obligatory (fard) because it is performed with absolute intention, so likewise with nafl intention. And this is invalid in the legal structure because scholars only disagreed about carrying the absolute (mutlaq) upon the restricted (muqayyad) and treating it accordingly. And this is carrying the restricted upon the absolute and treating it accordingly, which is invalid in the law's structure. Another example: the causal reasoning by edibility (ta'm) for the prohibition of riba by analogy with marriage (nikah). This is invalid in the legal structure because edibility provides subsistence (qiwam), so it is not suitable for prohibition. Freedom (hurriyah) expresses pureness (khulus), so it is suitable for prohibition except with an impediment.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage discusses invalid analogies (qiyas fasid) in the context of legal structure (wad'). The first example critiques the idea that Islam cuts off rights and apostasy cancels them; this is flawed because Islam does not act as a complete barrier and apostasy does not automatically pardon. The second example concerns a pilgrim performing Hajj with a nafl intention; some claim it suffices for fard, but this is invalid because it inverts the normal rule: scholars only debate carrying the absolute to the restricted, not the reverse. The third example compares riba prohibition based on edibility to the prohibition in marriage; but edibility relates to physical sustenance, which is ill-suited for a prohibition, whereas freedom (like in manumission) is a quality of purity that can be a cause for prohibition barring an impediment. The passage underscores that sound analogy must respect the foundational categories of the law.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause or ratio legis in analogical reasoning.
القِوام (al-qiwam): Sustenance or physical maintenance.
الخلوص (al-khulus): Pureness, freedom from impurity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is edibility (ta'm) considered an invalid 'illa for the prohibition of riba in this passage?
A) Because edibility is not found in all ribawi items.
B) Because edibility provides subsistence (qiwam) and is not suited for prohibition.
C) Because marriage is not a valid basis for analogy.
D) Because freedom (hurriyah) is a better 'illa.

Key Takeaway
An analogy is invalid if its proposed 'illa is unsuitable for the ruling; edibility cannot cause prohibition because it provides subsistence, not harm.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Insanity and Qada': A False Analogy Exposed
Refuting a flawed qiyas about qada' and insanity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the flaw in the analogy that insanity negates qada' by distinguishing between obligation (wujub) by compulsion and performance (ada') by choice.

Core Idea
Obligation is by compulsion, performance by choice; their rules differ.

This lesson examines a common legal analogy: since insanity prevents the obligation of performance, it should also prevent the obligation of makeup (qada'). The Hanafi school refutes this by exposing a fundamental distinction between the nature of obligation and performance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent argues that because insanity negates the obligation to perform an act (ada'), it should also negate the obligation to make it up (qada'). State this in your own words.
Opponent: 'Insanity conflicts with the obligation of performance, so it conflicts with the obligation of qada'.'
2
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response is that obligation (wujub) is imposed by compulsion (jabr), while performance (ada') is a matter of choice (ikhtiyar). These are different in nature.
Text: 'لأن الوجوب في كل الشرائع بطريق الجبر، والأداء بطريق الاختيار'
3
Apply the distinction to qada'
Qada' is a substitute (badal) that depends on the cause of the original obligation being established with probability. It is not the same as the original performance.
Text: 'القضاء الذي هو بدل يعتمد انعقاد السبب للأداء على الاحتمال'
4
Conclude the invalidity
Because the two cases have different operative causes (illa), the analogy fails. This is a classic example of flawed qiyas.
Text: 'فصار هذا التعليل مخالفاً للأصول'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضاء،
ومثله قولهم في الجنون : إنه لما نافى تكليف الأداء: نافى تكليف القضاء، وهو فاسد في الوضع؛ لأن الوجوب في كل الشرائع بطريق الجبر، والأداء بطريق الاختيار، كما قيل في النائم والمغمى عليه، والقضاء الذي هو بدل يعتمد انعقاد السبب للأداء على الاحتمال، فصار هذا التعليل مخالفاً للأصول.
وكذلك قولهم : ما يمنع القضاء إذا استغرق شهر رمضان: يمنع بقدر ما يوجد : هذا فاسد أيضاً في الوضع؛ لأن الفصل بين اليُسر والحرج في حقوق صاحب الشرع مستمر في أصول الشرع، كالحيض يُسقط الصلاةَ دون الصوم، والسفر أثر في الظهر دون الفجر، وكالحيض إذا تخلل في كفارة القتل : لا يوجب الاستقبال بخلاف كفارة اليمين عندنا وبخلاف ما إذا نذرت أن تصوم عشرة أيام متتابعة ؛ لما ذكرنا، فكذلك ها هنا الاستغراق حرج، وليس في القليل حرجٌ مثله .

ولا كلام في الحدود الفاصلة، ولا حرج في استغراق الإغماء؛ لأنه قلما يمتد شهرا، وفي الصلوات استوى الإغماء والجنون في الفتوى وإن اختلفا في الأصل، فكان القياس في الإغماء أن لا يُسقط. واستحسنًا في الكثير ، وكان القياس في الجنون أن يُسقط، واستحسنًا في القليل ؛ لأنهما سواء في الطول والامتداد الداعي إلى الحرج، والصبا ممتد أيضاً، فكان مثل الجنون.
وبخلاف الكفر ؛ لأنه ينافي الأهلية، وينافي استحقاق ثواب الآخرة، بخلاف الجنون.
وكذلك التعليل لتعيين النقود اعتباراً بالسلع، ولفسخ البيع بإفلاس المشتري اعتباراً بالعجز عن تسليم المبيع : فاسد في الوضع ؛ لما عُرف من التفرقة بين المبيع والثمن في أصل وضع الشرع، والبياعات تخالف التبرعات في أصل الوضع هذه للإيثار بالأعيان، وهذه: لالتزام الديون.
قال الله عز وجل : ﴿إِذَا تَدَايَنتُم بِدَيْنٍ ﴾ أي تبايعتم بنسيئة، فبطلت وجوه المقاييس في ذلك جملةً، على ما عُرف شرحه في موضعه.
The matter of qada'. Another example is their statement about insanity: since it conflicts with the obligation of performance (ada'), it conflicts with the obligation of qada' (makeup). This is invalid in the legal structure because obligation in all laws is by compulsion (jabr), while performance is by choice (ikhtiyar), as said about the sleeper and the unconscious. Qada' as a substitute depends on the cause for performance being based on probability, so this reasoning contradicts the principles. Similarly, their statement: what prevents qada' if one is engulfed by the month of Ramadan prevents only to the extent that it is found. This is also invalid in the legal structure because the distinction between ease and hardship in the rights of the Lawgiver is continuous in legal principles—like menstruation drops prayer but not fasting, travel affects zuhr but not fajr, and like if menstruation intervenes in the expiation of killing, it does not require restarting, unlike the expiation of an oath according to us, and unlike if one vows to fast ten consecutive days. For what we mentioned, so here engulfment is hardship, and a little is not like it in hardship. There is no dispute about the separating boundaries, and there is no hardship in total unconsciousness because it rarely extends a month. In prayers, unconsciousness and insanity are equal in fatwa although different in principle. The qiyas for unconsciousness would be not to drop, but we exercised istihsan in a large amount. The qiyas for insanity would be to drop, but we exercised istihsan in a small amount because they are equal in length and duration leading to hardship, and childhood is also extended, so it is like insanity. And unlike disbelief (kufr) because it contradicts legal capacity and contradicts deserving reward of the afterlife, unlike insanity. Likewise, the reasoning for determining currencies by analogy with commodities, and for rescinding a sale due to the buyer's bankruptcy by analogy with inability to deliver the sold item, is invalid in the legal structure because of the known distinction between the sold item and the price in the law's foundation. Sales differ from gifts in their basic structure: these are for altruism with specific items, these are for undertaking debts. Allah Almighty said: {When you contract a debt} meaning when you buy on credit, so the faces of analogies are invalidated in that altogether, as its explanation is known in its place.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage continues the critique of invalid analogies. It begins with the issue of qada' (makeup of missed acts). The opponents argue that since insanity negates the obligation of performance, it also negates the obligation of qada'. The author refutes this by noting that obligation (wujub) is by compulsion, while performance is by choice; qada' is a substitute depending on a probable cause for the original obligation. Thus the analogy fails. Next, they address the claim that if Ramadan is completely engulfed (e.g., insanity covers the entire month), then qada' is prevented only to the extent of the missed days. This is refuted because the Lawgiver consistently distinguishes between ease and hardship—for example, menstruation drops prayer but not fasting, travel affects zuhr but not fajr. Total engulfment is hardship; slight hardship is not comparable. Then the text discusses unconsciousness (ighma') and insanity (junun) regarding prayers and fasting, noting that in fatwa they are treated similarly despite different principles; istihsan (juristic preference) is employed for extensive periods due to hardship. Disbelief (kufr) differs because it negates legal capacity and reward, unlike insanity. Finally, two more invalid analogies are given: equating currency with commodities (since the law treats them differently) and rescinding a sale due to buyer's bankruptcy with inability to deliver (since the law distinguishes between sold item and price). A Quranic verse (al-Baqarah 282, 'When you contract a debt') is cited to argue that debt transactions are regulated differently, invalidating analogies in that domain.

Key Terms
الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship that triggers leniency or exemption in the law.
الاستغراق (al-istighraq): Complete coverage, e.g., insanity covering the entire month of Ramadan.
الأهلية (al-ahliyya): Legal capacity to acquire rights and obligations.
الجبر (al-jabr): Compulsion; obligation is imposed without the subject's choice.
الاختيار (al-ikhtiyar): Choice; performance of an act requires voluntary capacity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the analogy that insanity should negate qada' considered invalid?
A) Because insanity does not affect legal capacity.
B) Because obligation is by compulsion, performance by choice.
C) Because qada' is optional.
D) Because the Quran explicitly states it.

Key Takeaway
Insanity negates performance (ada') but not the obligation (wujub); therefore qada' remains required.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
Istighraq and Hardship: When Quantity Changes the Ruling
Refuting flawed qiyas about partial vs full coverage of insanity in Ramadan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that continuous hardship (haraj) triggers istihsan, overriding qiyas, and distinguish between full coverage (istighraq) and partial coverage.

Core Idea
Full hardship (istighraq) triggers leniency; partial hardship does not.

This lesson addresses the argument that if insanity covers the entire month of Ramadan, then qada' should be prevented only for the days affected. The Hanafi response invokes the principle that the Lawgiver consistently distinguishes between ease and hardship, and that total engulfment constitutes hardship warranting istihsan.

Guided Path
1
State the opponent's claim
The opponent says that if insanity covers the entire month of Ramadan, then qada' is prevented only to the extent that the insanity is found (i.e., for those days). This implies a proportional view.
Text: 'ما يمنع القضاء إذا استغرق شهر رمضان: يمنع بقدر ما يوجد'
2
Identify the Hanafi counter
The Hanafi response is that the Lawgiver consistently separates between ease (yusr) and hardship (haraj) in His laws. Examples: menstruation drops prayer but not fasting; travel affects zuhr but not fajr.
Text: 'الفصل بين اليُسر والحرج في حقوق صاحب الشرع مستمر في أصول الشرع'
3
Understand the role of istighraq
Total engulfment (istighraq) – like insanity for the entire month – creates a hardship that justifies leniency. Partial coverage does not have the same impact.
Text: 'فكذلك ها هنا الاستغراق حرج، وليس في القليل حرجٌ مثله'
4
Apply istihsan where qiyas fails
In cases of prolonged hardship, qiyas (analogy) would lead to excessive burden, so istihsan is used to provide ease. This is consistent with other examples like unconsciousness in prayers.
Text: 'والقياس في الإغماء أن لا يُسقط. واستحسنًا في الكثير'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship that triggers leniency or exemption in the law.
الاستغراق (al-istighraq): Complete coverage, e.g., insanity covering the entire month.
اليُسر (al-yusr): Ease; the opposite of haraj.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in cases of hardship or need.

According to the passage, what is the basis for the Hanafi ruling that unconsciousness does not drop qada' for prayer in most cases?
A) The hadith 'The pen is lifted'
B) Qiyas with insanity
C) The principle of istihsan based on lack of hardship
D) The Quranic verse on debt

Key Takeaway

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Invalid Analogies in Transactions: Currency and Bankruptcy
Refuting flawed qiyas about currency and bankruptcy using the Quranic distinction between mabi' and thaman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why analogies between currency and commodities and between bankruptcy and inability to deliver are invalid in Hanafi usul, citing the Quranic verse on debt.

Core Idea
The Shari'ah distinguishes between the sold item (mabi') and the price (thaman) in its foundational structure.

This lesson examines two more invalid analogies: equating currency with commodities for the purpose of determining the currency, and rescinding a sale due to the buyer's bankruptcy by analogy with inability to deliver the sold item. The Hanafi response is that the law fundamentally distinguishes between the sold item and the price, and between sales and gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two analogies
The first analogy is about determining which currency is used (ta'yin al-nuqud) by comparing to commodities. The second is about rescinding a sale due to buyer's bankruptcy (faskh al-bay' bi-iflas al-mushtari) by comparing to inability to deliver the sold item.
Text: 'التعليل لتعيين النقود اعتباراً بالسلع، ولفسخ البيع بإفلاس المشتري اعتباراً بالعجز عن تسليم المبيع'
2
Understand the foundational distinction
The law treats the sold item (mabi') and the price (thaman) differently. The sold item is a specific entity, while the price is a debt (dayn). Sales ('uqud al-mu'awada) are different from gifts (tabarru'at) in their basic structure.
Text: 'التفرقة بين المبيع والثمن في أصل وضع الشرع'
3
Cite the Quranic evidence
The Quranic verse {When you contract a debt} (al-Baqarah 2:282) is brought to show that debt transactions have their own rules, and analogies from commodity sales are invalid.
Text: 'قال الله عز وجل : ﴿إِذَا تَدَايَنتُم بِدَيْنٍ ﴾ أي تبايعتم بنسيئة'
4
Conclude the invalidity
Because of these fundamental differences, the analogies are invalid in the legal structure (fasad fi al-wad').
Text: 'فبطلت وجوه المقاييس في ذلك جملة'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The qiyas for insanity would be to drop, but we exercised istihsan in a small amount because they are equal in length and duration leading to hardship, and childhood is also extended, so it is like insanity. And unlike disbelief (kufr) because it contradicts legal capacity and contradicts deserving reward of the afterlife, unlike insanity. Likewise, the reasoning for determining currencies by analogy with commodities, and for rescinding a sale due to the buyer's bankruptcy by analogy with inability to deliver the sold item, is invalid in the legal structure because of the known distinction between the sold item and the price in the law's foundation. Sales differ from gifts in their basic structure: these are for altruism with specific items, these are for undertaking debts. Allah Almighty said: {When you contract a debt} meaning when you buy on credit, so the faces of analogies are invalidated in that altogether, as its explanation is known in its place.
Source pages p. 665

Commentary
This passage continues the critique of invalid analogies. It begins with the issue of qada' (makeup of missed acts). The opponents argue that since insanity negates the obligation of performance, it also negates the obligation of qada'. The author refutes this by noting that obligation (wujub) is by compulsion, while performance is by choice; qada' is a substitute depending on a probable cause for the original obligation. Thus the analogy fails. Next, they address the claim that if Ramadan is completely engulfed (e.g., insanity covers the entire month), then qada' is prevented only to the extent of the missed days. This is refuted because the Lawgiver consistently distinguishes between ease and hardship—for example, menstruation drops prayer but not fasting, travel affects zuhr but not fajr. Total engulfment is hardship; slight hardship is not comparable. Then the text discusses unconsciousness (ighma') and insanity (junun) regarding prayers and fasting, noting that in fatwa they are treated similarly despite different principles; istihsan (juristic preference) is employed for extensive periods due to hardship. Disbelief (kufr) differs because it negates legal capacity and reward, unlike insanity. Finally, two more invalid analogies are given: equating currency with commodities (since the law treats them differently) and rescinding a sale due to buyer's bankruptcy with inability to deliver (since the law distinguishes between sold item and price). A Quranic verse (al-Baqarah 282, 'When you contract a debt') is cited to argue that debt transactions are regulated differently, invalidating analogies in that domain.

Key Terms
المبيع (al-mabi'): The sold item, a specific entity ('ayn).
الثمن (al-thaman): The price, which is a debt (dayn) in the seller's liability.
الدين (al-dayn): Debt; a liability that is not a specific physical object.
البيع (al-bay'): Sale, a contract of exchange (mu'awada).
التبرع (al-tabarru'): Gift or donation, a contract of altruism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason why analogies between currency and commodities are invalid?
A) Currency is not a physical item.
B) The law distinguishes between the sold item and the price.
C) The Quran explicitly forbids such analogies.
D) Both are debt instruments.

Key Takeaway
The Shari'ah fundamentally distinguishes between the sold item (mabi') and the price (thaman); analogies that ignore this distinction are invalid.

Munaqada: Contradiction in Qiyas
Introducing munaqada as a type of flawed analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a qiyas commits munaqada and apply the Hanafi preference for transmitted evidence (athar) over such flawed analogy.

Core Idea
Two things sharing a general category need not share all rulings; contradiction arises when analogy ignores legal differentiation.

This lesson introduces munaqada (contradiction), the fourth type of invalid qiyas according to Hanafi usul. When an opponent argues that two cases must be identical because they share an attribute, but the law itself distinguishes them, the analogy collapses. Through the example of wudu and tayammum, you will see how the Hanafi school relies on transmitted evidence (athar) rather than forced analogies.

Guided Path
1
Define munaqada
Understand that munaqada is a contradiction in analogy where an attribute is used to assert similarity while ignoring legal differences.
The term literally means 'contradiction' or 'inconsistency'.
2
Examine the Shafi'i claim
Analyze the argument that because wudu and tayammum are both purifications, they must be alike in rulings (e.g., requiring intention).
The claim is based solely on the common category 'tahara'.
3
Evaluate the Hanafi counter
Identify the Hanafi refutation: the two purifications already differ in limbs, quantity, and action; additionally, washing a garment is a purification without intention, disproving the opponent's demand for uniformity.
This leads to reliance on athar (transmitted evidence) over qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوع الرابع وهو المناقضة فتلجئ إلى القول بالأثر أيضاً، مثل قول الشافعي رحمه الله في الوضوء والتيمم : إنهما طهارتان، فكيف افترقتا ؟
لأنه إن قال : وجب أن يستويا كان باطلاً بلا شبهة؛ لأنهما قد افترقا عدد الأعضاء، وفي قدر الوظيفة، وفي نفس الفعل. وإن قال: وجب أن يستويا في النية : انتقض ذلك بغسل الثوب،
As for the fourth type, which is contradiction (munaqada), it also leads to relying on transmitted evidence (athar). For example, the statement of al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, regarding wudu and tayammum: 'Both are purifications, so how are they different?' Because if he said: 'They must be equal,' it would be false without doubt, for they differ in the number of limbs, in the quantity of duty, and in the actual action. And if he said: 'They must be equal in intention (niyyah),' that is contradicted by the washing of a garment (ghasl al-thawb).
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth type of invalid analogy: munaqada (contradiction). This type occurs when an attribute common to two cases is used to claim they must be identical, yet the law itself distinguishes them. The example given is from Imam al-Shafi'i, who reportedly argued that since wudu (ablution with water) and tayammum (dry ablution) are both purifications (taharatani), they should be alike in rulings. The author refutes this by noting that they already differ in number of limbs washed, quantity of duty, and the action itself. If al-Shafi'i then argues they should at least be equal in the requirement of intention (niyyah), the author points out that this is contradicted by the case of washing a garment (ghasl al-thawb), which is also a purification but does not require intention. Thus, the common attribute of 'purification' does not force uniformity; the law may impose differences. This type of reasoning leads to relying on transmitted evidence (athar) rather than flawed analogy.

Key Terms
المناقضة (al-munaqada): A contradiction in analogy where an attribute is used to assert both similarity and difference inconsistently.
الأثر (al-athar): Transmitted evidence, such as hadith or practice of the Companions.
الطهارة (al-tahara): Ritual purification, both with water and with earth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the fourth type of invalid qiyas discussed in the passage?
A) Fasid fi al-wad'
B) Munaqada
C) Qiyas ma' al-fariq
D) Qiyas al-aks

Key Takeaway
Munaqada (contradiction) occurs when an analogy inconsistently applies a common attribute, and the correct response is to rely on transmitted evidence (athar) over flawed comparison.

Invalidity of Qiyas in Financial Transactions
Critiquing analogies in currency and sale rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify invalid analogies in fiqh by applying the Hanafi distinction between sales and donations, and evaluate why Quranic evidence precludes certain qiyas.

Core Idea
Sales and donations differ in foundation; analogies that ignore this are invalid in Shariah.

In this passage, the author critiques two specific analogies: treating currency like goods for specification and rescinding a sale due to buyer insolvency by analogy to inability to deliver. The Hanafi school argues that these analogies fail because Shariah fundamentally distinguishes between the sold item and the price, and between sales and donations. This lesson examines the textual evidence (Quran 2:282) and the usul principle that some qiyas are invalid (fasid fi al-wad') due to foundational differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two analogies
Read the passage and list the two cases where the author says qiyas is invalid: (1) analogizing currency specification to goods, (2) analogizing rescission of sale due to insolvency to inability to deliver.
These are the examples.
2
Understand the foundational distinction
The author states that Shariah distinguishes between the sold item (mabee') and the price (thaman), and between sales (bayi'at) and donations (tabarru'at). Donations involve altruistic transfer of physical assets, while sales involve undertaking debts.
This distinction is key.
3
Locate the Quranic evidence
The author cites Quran 2:282: 'When you contract a debt' and interprets it as referring to deferred payment sales (bayi' bi nasi'ah). This verse establishes the unique nature of debt transactions.
The verse shows that debt/sale transactions are a separate category.
4
Explain why the analogies fail
Because the analogies treat elements that are fundamentally different (e.g., currency as goods, or insolvency as inability to deliver) as if they were the same. The Shariah's original classification (wad') prevents such analogies.
The qiyas is fasid fi al-wad'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك التعليل لتعيين النقود اعتباراً بالسلع، ولفسخ البيع بإفلاس المشتري اعتباراً بالعجز عن تسليم المبيع : فاسد في الوضع؛ لما عُرف من التفرقة بين المبيع والثمن في أصل وضع الشرع، والبياعات تخالف التبرعات في أصل الوضع هذه للإيثار بالأعيان، وهذه: لالتزام الديون. قال الله عز وجل : ﴿إِذَا تَدَايَنتُم بِدَيْنٍ ﴾ البقرة/ 282 : أي تبايعتم بنسيئة، فبطلت وجوه المقاييس في ذلك جملة، على ما عُرف شرحه في موضعه.
And likewise, the reasoning for specifying currencies by analogy to goods, and for rescinding the sale due to insolvency of the buyer by analogy to inability to deliver the sold item: it is invalid in its very foundation, because the distinction between the sold item and the price is known in the original establishment of the Shariah. And sales differ from donations in their original foundation: the latter are for altruism (giving away physical assets), and these [sales] are for undertaking debts. Allah Almighty said: {When you contract a debt} (Al-Baqarah 2:282), meaning when you make a deferred payment sale, thus all faces of analogy in that regard are invalidated, as its explanation is known in its place.
Source pages p. 666

Commentary
This passage condemns certain analogical extensions as fundamentally invalid (fasid fi al-wad'). The first example is analogizing the specification of currencies (like determining which currency is default) to the treatment of goods. The second is analogizing the rescission of a sale due to the buyer's insolvency to the case where the seller cannot deliver the sold item. The Hanafi position is that Shariah fundamentally distinguishes the sold item (mabee') from the price (thaman). Sales are about exchanging goods for debts or prices, while donations (tabarru'at) are about altruism with physical assets. The Quranic verse about debts (2:282) is cited as evidence that deferred payment sales are the norm, and thus analogies that disregard these foundational distinctions are invalid. The author notes that the full explanation is given elsewhere.

Key Terms
البياعات (al-bayi'at): Sales transactions; exchanges of goods for price/debt.
التبرعات (al-tabarru'at): Donations; altruistic transfers of property without compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The 'illa is not present.
B) They contradict the Quranic verse about writing down debts.
C) Shariah fundamentally distinguishes between the sold item and the price, and between sales and donations.
D) The Prophet prohibited such analogies.

Key Takeaway
Analogical reasoning in fiqh is invalid if it disregards the foundational classifications (wad') established by Shariah, even if a superficial 'illa appears to exist.

Women's Testimony in Marriage: Hanafi Usul Argument
Hanafi rebuttal of opposing school on women's testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi usul argument that women's testimony in marriage is an original proof (asliyyah) and analyze the qiyas rebuttal against the opposing school.

Core Idea
Women's testimony in marriage is established by original proof, not necessity, because marriage is validated by shubhat.

This passage presents a Hanafi usul rebuttal to the claim that women's testimony cannot prove marriage because it is not property. The Hanafi response redefines women's testimony as an original proof (asliyyah) rather than merely necessary (daruriyyah), and uses the concept of shubha (ambiguity) to justify its application in marriage contracts. By analyzing this argument, you will understand how the Hanafi school employs qiyas and istihsan to defend its positions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
The text begins by citing an opposing view: marriage is not property (mal), so it cannot be established by women's testimony.
This is likely the Shafi'i position.
2
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal via counter-example
The author invalidates the opposing analogy by pointing to virginity (bikarah) and matters unseen by men, where women's testimony is accepted even though they are not financial matters.
This shows the opposing qiyas is defective.
3
Distinguish necessary vs. original proof
The Hanafi argues that women's testimony is not merely a proof of necessity (like the midwife) but an original and general proof (asliyyah), since it is accepted in many cases.
Generality indicates originality.
4
Apply the shubha argument
Marriage is a transaction that is established by shubhat (ambiguities), as evidenced by its validity with jest (hazl), which does not hold for property.
Thus women's testimony, which also involves shubha, is suitable for marriage.
5
Conclude the refutation
The analogy fails completely, leading to a transition (intiqal) to another ruling.
The passage ends by opening the chapter of aspects of transition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومثله قوله في النكاح إنه ليس بمال فلا يثبت بشهادة النساء. وهو باطل بالبكارة، وكل ما لا يَطَّلع عليه الرجال، فيضطره إلى الفقه ، وهو أن يقول : إن شهادة النساء حجةٌ ضرورية، فكانت حجةً في موضع الضرورة، وما يُبتذل في العادة. بخلاف النكاح فيظهر به فقه المسألة؛ لأنا لا نُسلّم أن هذه الحجة ضرورية، بل هي أصلية، تثبت بشهادة الرجال والنساء، ولو كانت ضروريةً لاختصت بإثبات حكم خاص، كما في شهادة القابلة، وحيث عمت أحكامها عرفنا أن شهادة الرجال مع النساء أصل وليست بضرورية، إلا أن ذلك أصلية ؛ لأن عامة حقوق البشر نظير هذه فيها ضربَ شُبهة، وهي مع الحجة في احتمال الشبهة، والنكاح من جنس ما يثبت بالشبهات، فكان فوق ما لا يسقط بالشبهات في أصل الوضع فبطل القياس به من كل وجه. ألا يُرى أنه يثبت مع الهزل الذي لا يثبت به المال، فلأن يثبت بما يثبت به المال : أولى، وإذا ثبت دَفْعُ العلل بما ذكرنا من وجوهه: كانت غايتُه أن يُلجئ إلى الانتقال. وهذا باب وجوه الانتقال
And similarly his statement about marriage that it is not property, so it is not established by the testimony of women. And it is invalidated by virginity and everything that men are not aware of, so he is compelled to [give] the fiqh, which is to say: the testimony of women is a necessary proof, so it is a proof in a place of necessity and what is customarily exposed. Unlike marriage, so the fiqh of the issue appears thereby; because we do not concede that this proof is necessary; rather it is original, established by the testimony of men and women. And if it were necessary, it would be specific to establishing a specific ruling, as in the testimony of the midwife. And since its rulings became general, we know that the testimony of men with women is original and not necessary, except that that is original because most rights of mankind are similar to this in that they contain a kind of ambiguity (shubha), and they are with the proof in the possibility of ambiguity. And marriage is of the type that is established by ambiguities, so it is above that which is not invalidated by ambiguities in the original establishment, so the analogy is invalidated from every aspect. Do you not see that it is established with jest (hazl) which does not establish property? So that it should be established by that which establishes property is more appropriate. And when the refutation of the effective causes (al-ilal) is established by what we have mentioned from its aspects, its ultimate end is to compel transition. And this is the chapter of aspects of transition.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage continues the Hanafi rebuttal of an opposing view (likely Shafi'i) that the testimony of women is not accepted in marriage because marriage is not a financial matter (mal). The author first invalidates the opposing analogy by pointing to virginity (al-bikarah) and matters not seen by men, where women's testimony is accepted. Then he argues that the opposing claim that women's testimony is a proof only out of necessity (darurah) is incorrect; rather, it is an original proof (asliyyah) established by the combination of men and women. If it were only necessary, it would be restricted to specific cases like the midwife. Since it is general, it is original. Furthermore, marriage is a matter that is established by shubhat (ambiguities), as evidenced by its validity with jest (hazl), which does not establish property. Therefore, women's testimony, which also involves shubha, is appropriate for marriage. The author concludes that the analogy fails and leads to a transition to another ruling.

Key Terms
شهادة النساء (shahadat al-nisa'): Testimony of women in legal cases; discussed here in the context of marriage contracts.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Ambiguity or resemblance that affects legal rulings; here it is used to justify acceptance of women's testimony in marriage.
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or joke; marriage is valid even if made in jest, unlike financial contracts.
أصلية (asliyyah): Original proof; a proof that is not derived from necessity but is inherently valid.
ضرورية (daruriyyah): Necessary proof; accepted only in cases of necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because women's testimony is a necessary proof like a midwife's.
C) Because marriage is established by shubhat (ambiguities), and women's testimony involves shubha.
D) Because men's testimony is not available.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts women's testimony in marriage because it considers women's testimony an original proof and marriage a contract established by shubhat.

The Four Modes of Transition (Intiqal): Classification of Valid and Invalid Shifts
Classification of four modes of intiqal with ruling on validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the four modes of transition in juristic argumentation and identify which are valid and invalid according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Valid transitions maintain the link between ruling and 'illa; invalid ones break the chain.

In this passage from Al-Bazdawi's usul work, we learn about four ways a jurist can shift argument from one cause ('illa) to another or from one ruling to another. Understanding these modes is essential for proper jadal (dialectical reasoning) and for recognizing when an argument becomes disconnected. The first three modes are considered valid across schools, while the fourth is specifically rejected by the Hanafis.

Guided Path
1
List the four modes of intiqal
From the Arabic text, extract the four modes Abu al-Bazdawi enumerates. Write them in order.
Focus on the structure: first mode is about 'illa to 'illa to prove first 'illa; second is ruling to ruling by same 'illa; third is to new ruling and new 'illa; fourth is to new 'illa to prove original ruling.
2
Classify each mode as valid or invalid
The text states that the first three are 'sahihah' (valid) and the fourth is 'batil' (invalid) according to the Hanafis. Note that some other scholars consider the fourth 'mustahsan' (good).
3
Understand the rationale for validity
The first three are valid because the arguer's purpose is to establish the ruling by that 'illa; as long as he works to prove that 'illa, he is not 'munqati' (cut off). The fourth is invalid because he abandons the first 'illa without proof, causing discontinuity.
The key concept here is 'inqita' (interruption) – the argument becomes disconnected.
4
Apply the example of the minor depositee
The text gives an example: a minor depositee who consumes the deposit. The argument for non-liability is based on the 'illa of 'having authority to consume'. If the opponent objects to this 'illa, the arguer must prove it. Shifting to a different 'illa to prove the same ruling without proving the first is invalid (fourth mode).
This example illustrates the practical consequence of invalid transition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا باب وجوه الإنتقال وهي أربعة أوجه:
الأول: الإنتقال من علة إلى علة أخرى لإثبات الأولى.
والثاني: الإنتقال من حكم إلى حكم آخر بالعلة الأولى.
والثالث: الإنتقال إلى حكم آخر وعلة أخرى.
وهذه كلها صحيحة.
والرابع: الإنتقال من علة إلى علة أخرى لإثبات الحكم الأول لا لإثبات العلة الأولى.
وهذا الوجه باطل عندنا، ومن الناس من استحسن هذا أيضاً.
أما الوجوه الأولى: فإنما صحت؛ لأنه لم يدع إلا الحكم بتلك العلة، فما دام يسعى في إثبات تلك العلة لم يكن منقطعاً، وذلك مثل من علل بوصف بوصف ممنوع، فقال في الصبي المودع إذا استهلك الوديعة: لم يضمن؛ لأنه مسلط على الإستهلاك، فلما أنكره الخصم: احتاج إلى إثباته، وهذا هو الفقه بعينه.
وكذلك إذا ادعى حكماً بوصف، فسلم له ذلك: لم يكن إنقطاعاً؛ لأن غرضه إثبات ما ادعاه والتسليم يحققه، فلم يكن به بأس.
This is the chapter on the modes of transition, and they are four aspects: First: Transition from one cause to another cause to establish the first [cause]. Second: Transition from one ruling to another ruling by the first cause. Third: Transition to another ruling and another cause. All of these are valid. Fourth: Transition from one cause to another cause to establish the first ruling, not to establish the first cause. This aspect is invalid in our [school], and some people consider it good as well. As for the first three aspects, they are valid only because he does not claim except the ruling by that cause, and as long as he strives to establish that cause, he is not cut off. For example, one who gives a cause by a description that is objected to, and says concerning the minor depositee if he consumes the deposit: he does not guarantee because he has authority over consumption. When the opponent denies that, he needs to prove it, and this is the very fiqh. Similarly, if someone claims a ruling by a description, and the opponent concedes that to him, it is not a cut-off because his purpose is to establish what he claims, and the concession realizes it, so there is no harm in it.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bazdawi's usul work introduces the chapter on 'Modes of Transition' (Intiqal) in juristic argumentation. The author lists four types of transitions that can occur when a jurist (mujtahid) argues using a cause ('illa). The first three modes are considered valid: (1) moving from one 'illa to another to prove the first 'illa itself, (2) moving from one ruling to another using the same 'illa, and (3) moving to a different ruling and a different 'illa simultaneously. These are valid because the arguer's goal is to establish the ruling by some 'illa, and as long as he is working to prove that 'illa, the argument is not considered a dead end. The fourth mode—moving from one 'illa to another to prove the original ruling without proving the first 'illa—is invalid in the Hanafi school. Some other scholars (likely the Mutakallimin or Shafi'is) consider it acceptable, but the Hanafis reject it because it fails to establish the necessary link. The author provides an example: a minor who is entrusted with a deposit and consumes it. The argument that he is not liable because he is authorized to consume is based on an 'illa (authority). If the opponent denies this 'illa, the jurist must prove it; simply moving to another 'illa without proving the first is invalid. This passage underscores the importance of coherence and proper sequence in juristic reasoning.

Key Terms
انتقال (intiqāl): Moving from one legal cause ('illa) or ruling to another in the course of argumentation.
علة (ʿilla): The effective cause or ratio legis that links a ruling to its basis in qiyas.
انقطاع (inqiṭāʿ): A break or interruption in argumentation where the arguer fails to defend his claimed 'illa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Al-Bazdawi, which mode of transition is considered invalid in the Hanafi school?
A) Transition from one cause to another to establish the first cause.
B) Transition from one ruling to another using the first cause.
C) Transition from one cause to another to establish the original ruling without establishing the first cause.
D) Transition to another ruling and another cause.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi jadal, only transitions that continue to prove the originally claimed 'illa or honestly open a new argument are valid; abandoning the first 'illa without proof is invalid.

The Fourth Mode of Transition: Hanafi Rejection vs. Opposing View
Detailed analysis of the disputed fourth mode with opposing views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi justification for rejecting the fourth mode of intiqal and evaluate the counterargument from other scholars.

Core Idea
Shifting to a new 'illa without proving the first is invalid because it breaks the logical chain.

This passage focuses specifically on the fourth mode of transition in argumentation, which the Hanafis consider invalid. We explore the reasoning behind this rejection and learn about a differing view from some other scholars who deem it acceptable. The example of the minor depositee helps illustrate why the Hanafis insist on proving the original 'illa before moving on.

Guided Path
1
State the definition of the fourth mode
The fourth mode is 'transition from one 'illa to another 'illa to establish the first ruling, not to establish the first 'illa.' That is, the arguer abandons his original 'illa and presents a new one to support the same ruling, without first proving the original 'illa.
2
Identify the Hanafi ruling
The text says 'this aspect is invalid in our [school]' — i.e., the Hanafis deem it bāṭil (invalid).
3
Note the opposing view
The text adds: 'and some people consider it good as well'. These are likely Mutakallimūn or Shāfi'īs who accept this mode.
4
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi argument: the arguer's purpose is to establish the ruling by a specific 'illa. If he abandons that 'illa without proof, he becomes 'munqati' (cut off). The opponent is not obliged to accept a different 'illa without a connection to the original claim.
The key term is 'inqiṭāʿ' (interruption).
5
Apply the example
In the case of a minor depositee consuming the deposit, the arguer claims non-liability because 'he is authorized to consume'. If the opponent objects to this 'illa, the arguer must prove it. He cannot simply shift to a different 'illa (e.g., 'he is not morally responsible') to prove non-liability without first proving the authority. Doing so would be the invalid fourth mode.
