وهذا باب وجوه الإنتقال وهي أربعة أوجه:
الأول: الإنتقال من علة إلى علة أخرى لإثبات الأولى.
والثاني: الإنتقال من حكم إلى حكم آخر بالعلة الأولى.
والثالث: الإنتقال إلى حكم آخر وعلة أخرى.
وهذه كلها صحيحة.
والرابع: الإنتقال من علة إلى علة أخرى لإثبات الحكم الأول لا لإثبات العلة الأولى.
وهذا الوجه باطل عندنا، ومن الناس من استحسن هذا أيضاً.
أما الوجوه الأولى: فإنما صحت؛ لأنه لم يدع إلا الحكم بتلك العلة، فما دام يسعى في إثبات تلك العلة لم يكن منقطعاً، وذلك مثل من علل بوصف بوصف ممنوع، فقال في الصبي المودع إذا استهلك الوديعة: لم يضمن؛ لأنه مسلط على الإستهلاك، فلما أنكره الخصم: احتاج إلى إثباته، وهذا هو الفقه بعينه.
وكذلك إذا ادعى حكماً بوصف، فسلم له ذلك: لم يكن إنقطاعاً؛ لأن غرضه إثبات ما ادعاه والتسليم يحققه، فلم يكن به بأس.
This is the chapter on the modes of transition, and they are four aspects: First: Transition from one cause to another cause to establish the first [cause]. Second: Transition from one ruling to another ruling by the first cause. Third: Transition to another ruling and another cause. All of these are valid. Fourth: Transition from one cause to another cause to establish the first ruling, not to establish the first cause. This aspect is invalid in our [school], and some people consider it good as well. As for the first three aspects, they are valid only because he does not claim except the ruling by that cause, and as long as he strives to establish that cause, he is not cut off. For example, one who gives a cause by a description that is objected to, and says concerning the minor depositee if he consumes the deposit: he does not guarantee because he has authority over consumption. When the opponent denies that, he needs to prove it, and this is the very fiqh. Similarly, if someone claims a ruling by a description, and the opponent concedes that to him, it is not a cut-off because his purpose is to establish what he claims, and the concession realizes it, so there is no harm in it.
Source pages p. 668

Commentary
This passage from Al-Bazdawi's usul work introduces the chapter on 'Modes of Transition' (Intiqal) in juristic argumentation. The author lists four types of transitions that can occur when a jurist (mujtahid) argues using a cause ('illa). The first three modes are considered valid: (1) moving from one 'illa to another to prove the first 'illa itself, (2) moving from one ruling to another using the same 'illa, and (3) moving to a different ruling and a different 'illa simultaneously. These are valid because the arguer's goal is to establish the ruling by some 'illa, and as long as he is working to prove that 'illa, the argument is not considered a dead end. The fourth mode—moving from one 'illa to another to prove the original ruling without proving the first 'illa—is invalid in the Hanafi school. Some other scholars (likely the Mutakallimin or Shafi'is) consider it acceptable, but the Hanafis reject it because it fails to establish the necessary link. The author provides an example: a minor who is entrusted with a deposit and consumes it. The argument that he is not liable because he is authorized to consume is based on an 'illa (authority). If the opponent denies this 'illa, the jurist must prove it; simply moving to another 'illa without proving the first is invalid. This passage underscores the importance of coherence and proper sequence in juristic reasoning.

Key Terms
انتقال (intiqāl): Moving from one legal cause ('illa) or ruling to another in the course of argumentation.
علة (ʿilla): The effective cause or ratio legis that links a ruling to its basis in qiyas.
انقطاع (inqiṭāʿ): A break or interruption in argumentation where the arguer fails to defend his claimed 'illa.
مستحسن (mustaḥsan): Considered good or acceptable, often through istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi justification for rejecting the fourth mode of intiqal?
A) The new 'illa is likely weaker than the first
B) The arguer becomes disconnected (munqati') from his original claim
C) The ruling must be based on the first 'illa only
D) The opponent has no right to object to the new 'illa

Key Takeaway
The fourth mode of intiqal is invalid in Hanafi jadal because it breaks the continuity of proof; the arguer must establish his first 'illa before moving to another.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
12 min
The Four Modes of Transition (Intiqal) in Hanafi Debate
Classifies and rules on four modes of intiqāl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four modes of intiqāl in Hanafi debate theory, classify each as valid or invalid, and explain the Hanafi objection to the fourth mode using the principle of inqiṭā‘.

Core Idea
In Hanafi debate, you cannot switch to a new cause to save a failing argument—that is inqiṭā‘ (disconnection).

In Hanafi uṣūl al-fiqh, debate (jadal) is governed by strict rules to ensure clarity and finality. One key concept is intiqāl, the transition from one cause or ruling to another when an opponent challenges a proof. This passage from al-Bazdawi classifies four types of transition, ruling the first three valid and the fourth invalid. The dispute over the fourth mode, and the use of the story of Ibrahim (AS) by some scholars, provides a rich exercise in legal reasoning and proof analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four modes
Read the first part of the passage and list the four types of intiqāl mentioned. Note what each transition moves from and to.
The author numbers them explicitly: 1-4.
2
Classify each mode as valid or invalid
Determine which modes the Hanafi school accepts (sahīḥ) and which it rejects (bāṭil). Mark the first three as valid and the fourth as invalid.
The text says 'These all are valid' except the fourth.
3
Understand the rationale for validity of the first three
Explain why each of the first three modes is considered valid: the debater is still trying to prove the original cause, or the new claim is conceded, etc.
The text gives examples like the deposited child (ṣabī al-mūda‘) and the contract of kitābah.
4
Analyze the fourth mode and the opposing view
State the fourth mode: moving from one cause to another to prove the first ruling (not the first cause). Identify the opposing scholars who accept it and their evidence from the story of Ibrahim.
The opposing view is attributed to 'some people' (min al-nās).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal
Understand why the Hanafi school rejects the fourth mode: the purpose of debate is finality, and an endless shift defeats that purpose. Analyze how the Hanafi response distinguishes the Ibrahim story.
The key point: Ibrahim's first argument was already decisive; Nimrod was defeated (buhita). The second argument was extra, not a salvage.

لأن غرضه إثباتُ ما ادعاه والتسليم يحققه، فلم يكن به بأس.

فإن قال : عندي لا يمنع هذا العقد.

They are four modes:
Chapter: The Modes of Transition
1- The first: transition from one cause to another cause to prove the first [cause].
2- The second: transition from one ruling to another ruling by the first cause.
3- The third: transition to another ruling and another cause.
All these are valid.
4- The fourth: transition from one cause to another cause to prove the first ruling, not to prove the first cause.
This mode is invalid according to us, but some people have also considered this good. As for the first modes: they are valid only because he did not claim except the ruling by that cause, and as long as he strives to prove that cause, he is not cut off. For example, one who gives a cause by a description that is disputed, and says concerning the deposited child when he consumes the deposit: he is not liable because he is authorized to consume. When the opponent denied this, he needed to prove it, and this is fiqh itself.
Similarly, if he claims a ruling by a description, and the opponent concedes that, it is not a disconnection; because his purpose is to prove what he claimed, and the concession confirms it, so there is no harm in it.
If he can prove another ruling by that description, that is a sign of complete fiqh and correctness of the description, like our saying: Kitabah (contract of manumission) is a contract that can be rescinded by cancellation, it prevents [the slave] from being used for expiation, like Ijarah and sale.
If he says: According to me, this contract does not prevent.
It is said to him: It must not cause a deficiency in the slave that prevents [the slave] from being used for expiation, or does not entail what prevents.
And if he gives a cause by another description for another ruling, there is no harm in it; because what he claimed has become conceded, so there is no harm, but such [argument] is not free from some inattention.
As for the fourth, some people considered it good and argued by the story of Ibrahim, peace be upon him, in his disputation with the accursed one, for he transitioned to another proof to prove that very ruling, as Allah narrated about him in His saying: "For Allah brings the sun from the east, so bring it from the west." So the disbeliever was confounded. [Al-Baqarah 2:258]
And the correct [view] is that such [transition] is considered a disconnection; because debate (nazar) was legislated to clarify the truth, and if it is not terminating, clarification does not occur. Just as if he is required to rebut: he is not allowed to avoid it by an additional description, then it is more appropriate that he is not allowed to accept a new reasoning.
As for the story of Ibrahim, it is not of this kind; because the first argument was conclusive. Do you not see that he [the tyrant] responded with a false matter, namely his saying: "I give life and cause death." [Al-Baqarah 2:258]
Since that is the case, the accursed one was cut off, except that Ibrahim, peace be upon him, when...
Source pages p. 669

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi usul exposition on the modes of 'intiqal' (transition) in argumentation during debate. The author presents four types of moving from one 'illa (cause) or hukm (ruling) to another when an opponent challenges a proof. The first three are deemed valid: (1) moving from one cause to another to prove the original cause, (2) moving from one ruling to another using the same cause, and (3) moving to a new ruling and a new cause. The Hanafi position is that the fourth mode—shifting to a new cause to sustain the original ruling (without proving the first cause)—is invalid (batil). Some unnamed scholars (min al-nas) accept it, citing the Quranic story of Ibrahim debating Nimrod. The author rebuts that the story is not a valid precedent: Ibrahim’s first argument was already decisive, so Nimrod was already defeated; the second proof was merely an additional demonstration, not a shift to salvage a failing argument. The Hanafi rule is that once an opponent’s objection breaks the initial reasoning, a new unconnected cause cannot be introduced for the same ruling; that would be ‘inqita’ (disconnection) and would defeat the purpose of debate, which is to reach clarity through a consistent chain of reasoning.

Key Terms
انتقال (intiqāl): Moving from one 'illa (cause) or hukm (ruling) to another during a debate to support one's claim.
علة (‘illa): The effective cause or ground for a ruling in analogy (qiyās).
انقطاع (inqiṭā‘): Disconnection; the state where a debater fails to answer an objection and is considered defeated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the fourth mode of transition (intiqāl) invalid?
A) Because it requires unanimous agreement of all scholars.
B) Because it allows the debater to avoid defeat by introducing a new cause for the same ruling, undermining the finality of debate.
C) Because it is not mentioned in the Quran or Sunnah.
D) Because the Hanafis consider all transitions invalid.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi debate, a debater cannot introduce a new cause for the same ruling after the initial cause is refuted; doing so constitutes inqiṭā‘ and defeats the purpose of debate.
